<!-- page 5 -->
Kitab al-Riyad
On the judgment between the Two Reformers (al-Sadayn) — the authors of *al-Islah* and *al-Nusra*

Authored by
the Ismaili Da'i al-Ajall
Hamid al-Din Ahmad ibn Abdullah al-Kirmani, Hujjat al-Iraqayn

Edited and Verified by
Arif Tamir

Dar al-Thaqafa – Beirut

<!-- page 6 -->

<!-- page 7 -->
Introduction

The Islamic world at the close of the third century of the Muhammadan Hijra was a domain dominated by science, philosophy, and literature — these defined its cultural circle and its society. New worldwide intellectual movements, complex doctrinal differences, and endless verbal disputation neither settled with any single thinker nor stayed confined to any single place. How valuable, then, for the researcher who wishes to study the origins of these differences in depth, to consult the opinions of the writers and scholars who lived in that distant era — who left scientific traces and new modes of expression that reveal their time, their grasp of philosophical questions, and the extent of their effort in interpreting texts and explicating verses — so that a reasonable opinion can be formed that helps bring out reality and truth.

These disputes often spilled beyond the oral discussions raised in the clubs and scientific centers of the day, developing into long treatises that appeared in the pages of books and publications. Among the schools that contributed a large share to this field were ((the Ismailis)), and it is striking that we do not see this group conclude its verbal contest with other groups before another, newly fashioned kind of debate emerges — a debate between

<!-- page 8 -->
those whom they themselves called to. In this is clear proof of the intellectual freedom that prevailed in their lives — freedom from the constraints that dominate conservative societies, where the community holds a ruler around itself, obliging it not to deviate and to keep to the old, inherited course.

The first debate — or, in more accurate terms, the first scientific discussion — opened for the Ismailis new horizons, moved them into an atmosphere of distant scientific liberation, and made them a subject of conjecture and discussion in the Islamic world: namely, the appearance of the book ((al-Mahsul)). This book was put into circulation at the beginning of the fourth century AH (the tenth century AD). It is attributed to the great Syrian preacher ((Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Nasafi)), who is credited with converting the doctrine of the Samanid state in Khurasan to Ismailism, and who was executed in the year 331 AH (942 AD) — as stated in the book ((al-Farq bayn al-Firaq)) by its author Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi. It is very strange that ((Hujjat al-Iraqayn)), the great preacher and greatest philosopher ((Hamid al-Din Ahmad al-Kirmani)) — who discussed the subject and delved deeply into its investigation — did not mention the author of ((al-Mahsul)) at all; his talk was focused on ((Abu Hatim al-Razi and Abu Ya'qub al-Sijistani)). There must, then, be fundamental reasons for this omission that justify it; perhaps the book ((al-Mahsul)) is attributed to al-Nasafi wrongly.

In any case, the acclaim that the book ((al-Mahsul)) received among Ismaili intellectuals and scholars of that time moved Abu Hatim al-Razi, the chief preacher in northwestern Persia, to write a book in which he discussed what was said in the book ((al-Mahsul)); his book was called ((al-Islah)). It is clear that the opinions which al-Razi advanced caused a reaction in another great preacher named Abu Ya'qub al-Sijistani, who considered them contrary to the truth and to the principles. This is what led him to compose a book in which he discusses al-Razi's opinions, called ((al-Nusra)), in which the author championed ((al-Mahsul)) and attacked Abu Hatim al-Razi relentlessly. It appears that the book

<!-- page 9 -->
*al-Nusra* is among al-Sijistani's earliest works, as al-Kirmani alludes in his own book. Not long afterwards, al-Kirmani reviewed the entire subject, completed it, and composed his precious treatise ((al-Riyad)) on the judgment between ((al-Sadayn)) — as he termed them (the authors of *al-Islah* and *al-Nusra*). With great skill he rehearsed the opinions that had appeared in the three books, subjected them to scientific criticism, and compared them with ((the rule of the guiding da'wa)) — in other words, with the philosophical and doctrinal theories of the Fatimid official court of his time. In general, we find that al-Kirmani consistently prefers al-Razi's theories over al-Sijistani's, and objects to the latter on the grounds that they do not correspond to the truth and tend toward deviation. Moreover, he notes that al-Razi himself overlooked some of the sayings that came in the book ((al-Mahsul)), as we observe in the tenth chapter of the book ((al-Riyad)).

It is also worth mentioning the scientific ideas it contains. A philosophical group such as this deserves to be studied, examined, and scrutinized in depth. Unfortunately, the books ((al-Mahsul)) and ((al-Nusra)) have been lost; as for the book ((al-Islah)) by al-Razi and ((al-Riyad)) by al-Kirmani, they are still preserved.

((Kitab al-Riyad))

Al-Kirmani's book ((al-Riyad)) is considered one of the important books that express the philosophical theories of Ismailism in the fourth century AH. I have received two copies of it: the first from India, the second from Tehran, sent to me by the Orientalist W. Ivanow on behalf of the Ismaili Studies Society in India (The Ismaili Society), which commissioned me to investigate and explain the book, and to write its footnotes and comments as one of the working members of this association.

Before entering into the details, I must research and review what is stated in

<!-- page 10 -->
the book in the way of opinions, sayings, arguments, and statements, beginning with a brief overview of the lives of the three philosophers who took part in the discussion — namely al-Razi, al-Sijistani, and al-Kirmani.

Al-Razi:

He is Abu Hatim Ahmad ibn Hamdan al-Laythi al-Warsanani al-Razi, and his Ismaili name is "our master ((Abu Hatim)) the Eminent." He was a preacher of Ismailism in the land of al-Rayy and then moved to Baghdad, where he took up residence. His exact date of birth is not known. He was active in the Ismaili da'wa during the reign of the Imamate of Muhammad al-Mahdi Billah¹ ((Ubayd Allah)), the founder of the Fatimid state in the Maghrib. He was distinguished by the sweetness of his speech and his fine eloquence, for which he was famous; ((al-Marwazi)), the prince of al-Rayy — a militant Sunni — was drawn to him, and he succeeded in persuading the princes of those lands to enter Ismailism. He became one of the standard-bearers of politics and religion and one of the guides of the Islamic scientific renaissance in the late third and early fourth centuries. He took refuge in the theological and philosophical sciences. The great Orientalist Paul Kraus² said about him:

He was a leading preacher of Ismailism, and played an important role in political affairs in Tabaristan and Daylam, especially in Rayy and Isfahan, until elder statesmen responded to him — such as Asfar ibn Shirawayh, Mardawij the Commander, and others.

The truth is that Abu Hatim al-Razi was one of the standard-bearers of the scientific renaissance, not in Ismailism alone but in the Islamic world at large, and he contributed to the dissemination of philosophical teachings throughout the eastern countries, especially on the periphery of general Islamic culture. Despite all that,

¹ This Imam lived in Salamiyya, Syria, and migrated from there to the Maghrib when the pressure of the Abbasids intensified — after the Qarmatians split off and rebelled against the call, and especially after the great Syrian preacher Abu Abdullah al-Shi'i, who paved the way for the establishment of the Fatimid state in the Maghrib, became his caller [cf. *Our Story from the Levant to the Maghrib* — Publications of the Lebanese House of the Book, Beirut, Lebanon, 1959].
² *Philosophical Letters* — Paul Kraus — p. 291.

<!-- page 11 -->
he was not spared from the persecution of enemies in Daylam, and was forced into hiding in the last years of his life. He died in 322 AH, after the Fatimid al-Qa'im had assumed charge of the affairs of the Ismaili Imamate in the Maghrib, at the age of sixty-two, as some historians have said.

He was a contemporary of Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn Zakariya al-Razi, the famous physician and author of well-known philosophical views in which he diverged from many of Aristotle's naturalistic and metaphysical theories, denying the reconciliation of philosophy and religion and holding that philosophy is the only way to reform the individual and society. Violent and repeated discussions took place between the two Razis, attended by some scholars and political leaders. Abu Hatim recorded these discussions in his book ((A'lam al-Nubuwwa)) (Signs of Prophethood), where, although he does not name the man he is criticizing, he calls him an atheist. There is conclusive evidence that this atheist was none other than Abu Bakr al-Razi. The great philosopher al-Kirmani ((Hujjat al-Iraqayn)) mentioned in his book ((al-Aqwal al-Dhahabiyya)) (The Golden Sayings) that the discussions concerning prophethood and the religious rites took place between Abu Bakr al-Razi and Abu Hatim on the island of al-Rayy during the days of Mardawij, and in his presence. Abu Hatim was able to humble Abu Bakr al-Razi and refute his views in his book ((A'lam al-Nubuwwa)), in pages overflowing with charm and miracles, by which he has shut the eyes of the arrogant and the stubborn from any means of escape or evasion.

Among his most important works is the book ((al-Zina))³ on the Arabic letters, their derivatives, the reasons for their position, and the meanings of words — a great book on the authentic Arabic dictionaries, the language in which the Qur'an was revealed and which the Muslims spoke, and out of which Islamic terminology grew. This book runs to 1,200 pages. He dedicated it to the Fatimid Imam who succeeded Muhammad al-Mahdi Billah ((Ubayd Allah)), and to him

³ This book is being researched by Dr. Husayn al-Hamdani of Cairo University.

<!-- page 12 -->
[belong] the book ((A'lam al-Nubuwwa)), ((al-Islah)), ((al-Jami')), and others.

Al-Sijistani:

Abu Ya'qub Ishaq al-Sijistani — or ((al-Sijzi)) — is considered to be at the forefront of the scholars who struggled, labored, and dedicated themselves to establishing cosmic philosophical rules upon ideological intellectual foundations and solid scientific principles and fixed pillars, no matter what crises befell them. In fact, he is one of those who sacrificed everything they had in order to spread it and circulate it in other lands, even late in his life. He was accused of heresy and atheism by the conservative public, and was finally killed for the sake of his faith. Al-Sijistani played an important role in the field of philosophy and led intellectual reform in the third century of the Hijra; his intellectual impact appeared in his pupil Hamid al-Din al-Kirmani ((Hujjat al-Iraqayn)), who followed his curriculum, championed his teachings, and drew from the rising flood of his springs. And if we know that al-Kirmani learned philosophy in al-Sijistani's school of thought, we may place al-Sijistani in the first rank among the famous Muslim scholars, thinkers, and philosophers of the world.

He was a contemporary of the esoteric Ismaili call in the Age of Manifestation — that is, the period of the flowering of the Fatimid state and its emergence as an Islamic state with a civilizational, scientific, social, and political entity — although he lived in a country whose people followed a sect different from his own madhhab, so that he was forced to take ((taqiyya)) as a cover and to be very cautious in his movements and his preaching. For this reason his life has come to be enveloped in a certain mystery and ambiguity. We may not be at fault if we cannot get to know his biography fully, or report everything about him. On the other hand, we have received only a little about the preacher al-Khayr al-Nasafi ((al-Nakhshi)), the author of the book ((al-Mahsul)) — differing from what the authors have reported about his efforts and his contact with Nasr ibn Ahmad

<!-- page 13 -->
the Samanid in Transoxiana, until the Samanid embraced the Ismaili call. We also do not know much about Abu Hatim al-Razi, the linguist and author of the oldest book in the science of the Arabic language, which is the book ((al-Zina)); nor about ((Hujjat al-Iraqayn)) Hamid al-Din al-Kirmani — despite the arrival of most of his writings and works to us — even though al-Mu'ayyad fi al-Din Hibat Allah al-Shirazi, a friend and companion of Abu al-Ala al-Ma'arri, wrote his biography in his own hand, [yet] we cannot know anything about him. So too we say of the mysterious lives of the senior men of the Ismaili da'wa — the proofs and preachers of Algeria, Syria, Iran, Yemen, and others — whose lives are even more shrouded, since the books in which they wrote their biographies have been lost, with only a few surviving.

He is attributed to ((Sijistan)), a province in the south of Khurasan, from a Persian family. It has been said that he was of the family of the Persian hero ((Rustam)), and there are those who say that he is from Iraq, his grandfather having come from Kufa and settled in Sijistan. Some researchers who have studied his philosophy claim that he died in 331 AH. But this view does not accord with historical reality, since it is known that al-Sijistani was the teacher of al-Kirmani, and al-Kirmani remained alive until the year 411 AH. So at what time, then, did al-Kirmani take from him the philosophical sciences of the call? There is an explicit text in the book ((al-Iftikhar)) — al-Sijistani himself states in it that he composed it in 360 AH. The book ((al-Iftikhar)) has been mentioned in the book ((al-Riyad)) by al-Kirmani. That is, he was a preacher in the province of Bukhara during the caliphate of the Imam ((al-Mu'izz li-Din Allah al-Fatimi)) — meaning that he was a contemporary of the great preacher ((Ja'far ibn Mansur al-Yamani)), and of the scholar and jurist Qadi ((al-Nu'man ibn Muhammad ibn Hayyun al-Maghribi al-Tamimi)), the judge of the Fatimid state, and of other great authors and scholars of the da'wa in that golden age of manifest science. There is no evidence of the scientific value of al-Sijistani lacking from his books and writings that


<!-- page 14 -->

He left these works behind. They were composed in Arabic, with some written in Persian. Isma'il ibn 'Abd al-Rasul ibn Mata Khan al-Ujayni, who died in 1183 AH, listed them in his catalogue of the books, and al-Biruni referred to them in his own writings, as did al-Baghdadi in his *al-Farq bayn al-Firaq* (The Difference Between the Sects).

As we have said, al-Sijistani left behind a prolific scientific corpus regarded as among the deepest philosophical writing of its time. His works number more than thirty. Among the most famous are *Ithbat al-Nubuwwa* (Proofs of Prophecy), *Kashf al-Mahjub* (1), *Tuhfat al-Mustajibin* (Masterpiece of the Respondents) (2), and *al-Yanabi'* (3) — a book he divided into forty *yanabi'* ("springs"), which came to be counted among those books whose depth is rarely surpassed. He seems to have composed it for a particular class of readers: senior preachers and those of the highest ranks in the da'wah, those whose philosophical studies had reached their summit. As for why he organized it into forty springs, that reflects his desire to have each spring expound one of the forty *hudud* that constitute the higher da'wah.

Among his other books — as catalogued by the great traveler and Persian poet Nasir-i Khusraw in his *Zad al-Musafirin* — are *Asas al-Da'wah* (Foundations of the Da'wah), *Ta'wil al-Shara'i'* (Interpretation of the Laws), *Yanabi' al-Barakat* or *Yanabi' al-Najat*, *al-Risalah al-Bahirah* (The Brilliant Epistle), *Kitab al-Iftikhar*, *al-Mawazin* — divided into nineteen scales — *Sullam al-Najat* (The Ladder of Salvation), *al-Nusrah*, *al-Maqalid*, *Salwat al-Ahzan* (Consolations of Sorrows), *Asrar al-Ma'ad* (Secrets of the Return), *Mawa'iz fi al-Akhlaq* (Admonitions on Ethics), *al-Gharib fi Ma'na al-Iksir* (The Stranger on the Meaning of the Elixir), *Anis al-Qulub* (Companion of the Hearts), *Tarkib al-Arwah* (Composition of the Spirits), *Aman al-Ihtidar* (Security at the Hour of Death), *Khaza'in al-Adillah* (Treasures of Evidence), *al-Burhan*, and others.

(1) This book was researched and published by the orientalist Henri Corbin in Tehran together with 'Arif Tamir (may God have mercy on him), in *Khams Rasa'il Isma'iliyya* (Five Ismaili Epistles).

(2) and (3) The orientalist Henri Corbin, in collaboration with 'Arif Tamir, included these in *Thalath Rasa'il Isma'iliyya* (Three Ismaili Epistles), among the publications of the Institut Français in Tehran, Iran.

<!-- page 15 -->

In what follows, we summarize al-Sijistani's views on theology as expressed across most of his writings. He holds that the Creator of all creators is ancient, exalted, and pre-eternal in His primacy, and that the world of beings and creators is contingent — for if it were not, there would have had to be something preceding it, even if that world were itself old. The Creator precedes everything, since it is impossible to attach His might to what is finite or His existence to nothingness. By necessity, He transcends any apprehension of attributes: He is not grasped by the senses, does not fall under sight, is not perceived by the eyes, is not described as "naturalized," is not the object of conjecture or vision, is not characterized by the senses, and is not apprehended by analogy. He resembles no people. He is exalted above any peer, rival, or counterpart — exalted above the bounded fancies that fall into confusion when describing His might, beyond the comprehension that falls short of the character of His kingdom. He is seen without His greatness being apprehended; He has no likeness, no equal, no contrary, no peer. He has no opposite, for an opposite would be a rival pointing to His identity through His creation and the traces of His names — as His prophets do. The intellect has no room and analogy has no purchase in attaining His heights, for an analogy between the Creator and what He creates is impossible. He is the cause of every existent because He is the Creator of the creators, the Inventor of the inventors, the cause of beings, the Lord of all things, the Creator and Completer, the One who imparts the best of states. He is not bounded by any thought nor surrounded by any estimation. He has no names, because names were established for His existents and not as attributes, since attributes belong to His essences; and the letters of language cannot lead to the utterance of His name nor allow Him to be called by any of them, because all of them are among His inventions, and every name He created acknowledges Him as its inventor. He is pre-eternal, the source and possessor of the source of primacy in order, because the first *hadd* emerged from Him and the One overflowed from Him while He remains the Creator of creators, the Maker of makers, the Originator of originated things, the One who exists perpetually in His individuality and steadfastness.

The first act of creation was accompanied by the first number, which He made the origin of numbers — just as He made the Intellect the origin of creatures and the primal speaker of the world of religion. Beyond all this, He acquires none of the attributes: He is not a body, nor in a body; He does not rationalize Himself, nor does He apprehend by the senses; He is not in any image or form, nor in any substance; nothing in any language can be expressed of Him. He exists — but not as the kinds of entities inferred from the existence of other entities by being effected from a cause. He exists only as He must exist. Other existents lean upon one another for their being, some upon others; and those others, too, are among existents whose being is not fixed and which do not subsist of themselves.

From here al-Sijistani moves to the existents in their order and sequence. He says:

The Creator brought nothing into being at the beginning of creation except the Intellect, bounded in its essence, in which are contained the forms of all creatures. The name *al-Qalam* (the Pen) is given to the Intellect, because the Pen inscribes the inscriptions of creation from the beginning. The Intellect is also called *al-'Arsh* (the Throne), meaning the seat of the One enthroned upon it — the Majestic who knows who without Him would be degraded. The Intellect is also called (1), meaning the primordial source from which the creatures emerged: everything that exists, and everything imprinted upon it, precedes the rest of the *hudud* in receiving the effects of wisdom, because of its closeness to it and its union with it — which is knowledge and command in one sense. It is admissible that the act of the Intellect precedes its power, and this excellence is found in nothing other than it, because all the *hudud* below it have their powers before their acts; this excellence belongs specifically to the Intellect, so that it may be complete in it.

The Intellect is also called *al-Hukm* (the Decree), meaning that through the Intellect the soul resolves to perceive information and obtain its requirements; one may say the Intellect is the decree of God Almighty among His creation. The Intellect is also called *al-Hayuli*, meaning that the Intellect possesses the capacity of what is reflected from the forms. The Intellect is also called *al-Shams* (the Sun), meaning that the Intellect perceives the realities.

<!-- page 16 -->

Then there is the Soul (*al-Nafs*), the second creation, perpetuated by the first creation. It is called *Nafs* because it perpetually breathes (*tatanaffas*) to benefit from it; and the regular pattern of these breaths is the very fabric of creation. The Soul is also called *al-Lawh* (the Tablet), meaning that what was disclosed from the Intellect — through the disclosure of wisdom — dominates the Soul; and what flows from the Soul flows in proportion to its purity and gentleness. The Soul is also called *al-Malik* (the King), meaning that the Soul is the dominion and content of the Intellect, because in the Soul an excellence has manifested by which the Intellect, too, is disclosed in the figure of the King. The Soul is also called *al-Tali* (the Follower), meaning that the one that recites after the Intellect, in receiving the effects of the divine Word, is the Soul; and one may say that the Soul, by its power, recites the Intellect by acting upon it. The Soul is also called *al-Qadar* (Destiny), meaning that what is joined to the Soul is among the benefits of the Intellect, and that estimation and determination encompass it. The Soul is also called *al-Sura* (the Image), meaning that the Soul is conceived from the essence of the Intellect; when one attempts to follow it as it descends in rank, its light is true to itself — as the moon draws its light from the light of the sun, and when they meet in a single place its light is attributed to both. Together, the Intellect and the Soul are called by a single word: *al-Aslan*, the Two Principles.

These are some of al-Sijistani's philosophical views on theology, presented here briefly and joined to whatever we have been able to gather of his biography. Perhaps the impulse that drove him to take up the burden of these sciences was his disposition for arranging his texts in attractive order and exquisite coordination.

## Hamid al-Din Ahmad al-Kirmani

A scientific giant whose life story is shrouded in mystery; a great philosopher who lived in a flourishing scientific age. He left behind glorious pages of thought, profound investigations and the deepest of lines — a body of work that, for generations afterwards, is, at the very least, a precious treasure and an immortal heritage.

The philosophers of the Islamic world consider him the greatest mind produced by the Ismaili intellectual school in the age of the Fatimid state. His book *Rahat al-'Aql* (The Repose of the Intellect) (a) is among those valuable works whose strength, durability, and depth has scarcely an equal among the books of the philosophers and their remnants. Its demand was small and its popularity slow, restricted to a particular class of brilliant scholars and philosophers capable of mastering it.

The great preacher and Yemeni historian Idris 'Imad al-Din mentioned him in his book, saying: "He is the foundation of the call upon which it leans, by which it is exalted, through which its beacon is set straight, and by means of which problems are identified and dilemmas resolved." The Syrian Ismaili preacher Nur al-Din Ahmad described him in his book *al-Fusul wa al-Akhbar* (Chapters and Reports), saying: "Had the Ismaili call produced nothing but al-Kirmani, that alone would have been pride and glory enough."

His impact and stature became apparent during the reign of the Fatimid Caliph al-Hakim bi-Amr Allah. His famous title was *Hujjat al-'Iraqayn* (the Argument of the Two Iraqs), meaning that he was in charge of the cultural centers of the da'wah in Persia and Iraq. In Cairo his rank was that of a *Hujjah* — one of the twelve Hujjahs entrusted with managing the affairs of the da'wah, the intellectual front of the world. He was later appointed head of *Dar al-Hikmah* in Cairo, the cultural institution one may rightly call the first university established in the world.

He came on a delegation to Cairo in 408 AH at the invitation of al-Sadiq al-Ma'mun, host of the preachers of the Fatimid state during the reign of al-Hakim bi-Amr Allah, at a time when religious controversies had grown heated and new calls had filled the market with innovations bent on exaggeration and on departing from truth and from foundations. He delivered lectures and addresses at *Dar al-Hikmah* and produced —

(1) This book was printed under the supervision of the Ismaili Studies Society of India and edited by Dr. Muhammad Kamil Husayn and Dr. Mustafa Hifni of Cairo.

<!-- page 17 -->

— numerous investigations and books, the most famous being *Risalat al-Wa'iza* (The Preacher's Epistle), in reply to al-Hasan al-Farghani, who claimed that al-Hakim bi-Amr Allah was divine; as well as the epistles *al-Mabasim* (The Glad Tidings), *al-Masabih* (The Lamps), and others. In them he was able to establish the Imamate as a necessary cosmic reality, with reasoned and compelling arguments saturated with Hebrew, Syriac, and Persian expressions drawn from the books of the prophets in their revealed wording. He then took up Platonic views as the foundation of his investigations and expounded them with a skill no one before him had attained — all this as a general call to consolidate the philosophical-intellectual order, raise its level, and erase every trace of doubt, dispute, suspicion, and debate.

In any case, in these lines I do not aim to introduce the philosopher al-Kirmani merely as one of the Ismaili preachers who played an important role in the field of thought during the reign of the Fatimid Caliph al-Hakim bi-Amr Allah, nor only as one of the philosophers who served the Fatimid call with clear intellectual contributions. I present him, rather, as a philosopher among the world's philosophers — one who walked upon the stage of cosmic philosophy and did everything in his power to found a philosophical school upon a new rational foundation, drawing on the innovative modern theories of his time and on the rational systems consonant with the theory of the unity of existence held by the Second Teacher and the Chief Sheikh, but with a wider exposition and a departure from the general curriculum laid down for Ismaili preachers, whether his predecessors or his contemporaries — preachers who were compelled to follow a carefully circumscribed general method whose limits could not be transgressed or crossed.

Returning to al-Kirmani's writings — reading them attentively and analyzing what we find in them — we may settle upon the view that contradiction, dissonance, and an attempt by some of the existents to erase others is present in them; yet these existents endure in spite of this dissonance and this contradiction. None of them loses anything of its being through the presence of an opposite; rather, they are all preserved under existence. All of this is identical to the theory of the Second Teacher

<!-- page 18 -->

concerning creativity, in which he says: "The preservation of the continuity of the existence of a thing has, in itself, a permanence not bound to any of the existents other than the Creator." (1)

Ibn Sina, in the course of discussing the possible and the necessary among existents, articulates the meaning of the continuity of existence over the existents by saying: "The fact that existents may exist in themselves while not necessitating the existence of others does not contradict the fact that they are always present in others." (2)

This closely approximates what the three philosophers — the Second Teacher, the Chief Sheikh, and the Argument of the Iraqs — maintain on this matter. It also closely approaches what the philosopher Descartes said in the modern age: "There is no distinction between the act by which God preserves the world and the act by which He created it." (3) This is what is known in his philosophy as the theory of *continuous creation*.

In truth, al-Kirmani shared much with the Muslim philosophers and was influenced by some of them, especially those who drew on Greek philosophy; yet he is distinguished from them in that, when he presented these philosophies, he stamped his own work with the marks of novelty, ingenuity, and innovation. He was the forerunner — to the moderns among the European philosophers, and to the ancients among the philosophers of the East — in renewal. Accordingly one may say that al-Kirmani left behind books and produced ideas that should be regarded not merely as Ismaili intellectual positions but as Islamic philosophical positions of a high, general order — positions that look to the essence of things and to deep rational considerations, in a steady and elaborated manner in which genius and ingenuity are manifest.

Yes — al-Kirmani spoke from within the Neoplatonic systems, and he exerted all his powers to deploy some of their materials in his attractive philosophical and verbal style. He labored to establish the matter of Prophethood and Imamate from both the philosophical and the religious sides.

(1) Al-Farabi, *'Uyun al-Masa'il*, p. 60.

(2) Ibn Sina, *al-Isharat*, p. 240.

(3) Descartes, *Discourse on Method* — Part V — and *Principles of Philosophy*, paragraph (21).


On this basis we can say that he upheld the theory that strict adherence to the apparent letter of the Shari'a leads only to a formalistic worship, confining devotion to its outward shell — while interpretation and philosophical discourse, conducted within the bounds permitted to those who have reached a high station in philosophy and the sciences, carry the believer further. All of these themes become evident if we look closely at *al-Risala al-Mudi'a* (The Illuminating Treatise), *al-Masabih fi Ithbat al-Imama* (The Lamps in Proving the Imamate), *Tanbih al-Hadi wa al-Mustahdi* (Alerting the Guide and the Guided), and *Rahat al-'Aql* (Repose of the Intellect).

Beyond this, al-Kirmani is a major asset in the development of the intellectual system: he expanded its theoretical discourse with new elements, broadened many of its topics, laid down the fundamental rules of interpretation, and treated questions of contradiction and analogy — especially on the subject of the Imamate, which had long been the field of unstable and unsettled opinions and theories. This is why his book *Rahat al-'Aql* defines the rules, origins, ranks, and standing of the Imamate, and establishes new foundations, systems, and order for it.

Al-Kirmani was prominent in the doctrine of the da'wa of existence; in the absolute negation, with respect to God, of al-Issayah (is-ness) and al-Lysiyah (is-not-ness) along with all attributes; in the doctrine of the da'wa in Tawhid; in the doctrine of the da'wa concerning the two origins of origination (ibda') and emanation (inbi'ath); and in the question of the precedence between the Imamate and the Prophethood — all argued in a purely scientific and logical manner. He did not stop there. He combined, at a single stroke, the first two origins — the Active Intellect and the Universal Soul — with the system of the ten Neoplatonic intellects upheld by the Second Teacher [al-Farabi], and he aligned the world of origination (the world of intellects, or the spiritual world) and the celestial world (al-jirmani — the world of the planets and stars) with the physical world (everything below the lunar orbit) and the world of religion (the world of religion's boundaries, al-hudud). He then drew geographical, astronomical, terrestrial, and corporeal diagrams of this scheme, executed with great artistry and creativity, displaying his ingenuity in expression and his high station in philosophy.

Returning to *Rahat al-'Aql* and reviewing what it contains, one finds it filled with expressions and proofs concerning prophetic teaching about God and the negation of divine attributes. Among the positions he upholds: that no attribute attains to God; that He is not a body and not in a body; that He does not rationalize Himself and does not perceive Himself; that He is not in a form or in a substance, nor opposed to anything, nor likened to anything; that no language can express Him; and that He has no rank among the existents. All of this attests to his wide knowledge of the various sciences, especially the rational ones. On this point the Mu'tazila and the speculative theologians agree with him, and as Ibn Rushd is on the question of demonstrating the existence of God, so we see al-Kirmani in agreement with Descartes, even where they differ in details and in their method of expression. If we extend this inquiry further, we see that he incorporated into his solutions and ideas certain opinions of the philosophers who preceded him and of his contemporaries — such as Plato, Aristotle, al-Kindi, al-Farabi, and Ibn Sina.

From al-Baqillani, al-Baghdadi, and al-Ghazali we note that they ruled the Isma'ilis to have invalidated their own creed by denying the divine attributes. But al-Kirmani answered the charge directly, saying: "Explicit nullification consists in directing the particle of negation — 'no' — toward the identity itself, as in saying 'not He' or 'there is no god.' This is not what the Isma'ilis say. For them, the negation falls upon the attributes alone; the particle of negation is directed at the attributes without touching the identity." Whatever the case, al-Kirmani gave this subject special attention in *Rahat al-'Aql*. In that book we see him depart from the practice of all other philosophers, writers, historians, and scientists. Instead of dividing his book into the usual chapters, sections, and subsections that other thinkers and authors employ, he likens his book to a city encircled by seven walls. Anyone who enters must pass through all seven; and to pass through each wall, one must traverse seven branching paths (mashari') that issue from it — except for the seventh wall, the outermost, which encircles the entire city and bears fourteen such branches. We will not, here, undertake to explain what al-Kirmani means by his walls and branches, for fear of prolonging the discussion — though this does not prevent us from acknowledging his eminence in philosophy, the power of his thought, and his deep grasp of the essence of things.

Al-Kirmani composed a number of works, the most important of which include: *al-Risala al-Wadi'a* (The Divine Treatise) (1), *Risalat al-Nazm* (The Treatise of Order), *al-Risala al-Nuriyya* (The Light Treatise), *al-Risala al-Mudi'a* (The Illuminating Treatise), *al-Risala al-Lazima* (The Necessary Treatise), *al-Risala al-Hadiyya* (The Guiding Treatise), *Risalat al-Mubashshir* (The Treatise of the Preacher), *al-Risala al-Kafiya* (The Sufficient Treatise), *Tanbih al-Hadi wa al-Mustahdi* (Alerting the Guide and the Guided), *Ma'asim al-Huda* (The Handholds of Guidance), *al-Aqwal al-Dhahabiyya* (The Golden Sayings), *Mufasalat al-Khitab wa Tamthil al-Haqq al-Mutajalli min al-Shubha* (Distinguishing the Discourse and Representing the Manifest Truth from Doubt), *Risalat al-Awwabi* (The Treatise of the Returner), *Risalat al-Fihrist* (The Treatise of the Index), *al-Aqdar wa al-Haqa'iq* (Quantities and Realities), *Risalat al-Tawhid fi al-Ma'ad* (The Treatise of Tawhid in the Hereafter), *Taj al-'Uqul* (The Crown of Minds), *Mizan al-'Aql* (The Balance of the Intellect), *Kitab al-Naqd wa al-Iltizam* (The Book of Criticism and Obligation), *al-Kifaya al-Nafsiyya* (The Psychological Sufficiency), *Kitab al-Mawazin* (The Book of Scales), *al-Majalis al-Baghdadiyya wa al-Basriyya* (The Baghdadi and Basran Councils), *Risalat al-Sha'rani fi al-Khawass* (al-Sha'rani's Treatise on the Elite), *Rahat al-'Aql* (Repose of the Intellect), and *Risalat Usbu' al-Dawr al-Mastur* (The Treatise of the Week of the Hidden Cycle) (2).

Finally, it must be said that al-Kirmani is among the philosophers who hold a prominent place in our philosophical world. Studying his writings and his output is of real importance: it gives a clear picture of the impact of philosophy on the history of thought, and is of significant value for those interested in Oriental Studies and Islamic Philosophy.

## Contents of al-Riyad

Al-Kirmani opens *al-Riyad* by praising and glorifying God, as was the standing convention of all scholars of those eras, and concludes the opening by pointing to the Imam of his time — al-Hakim bi-Amr Allah of the Fatimids. He then says:

"I saw Abu Hatim al-Razi setting right the errors in the book *al-Mahsul* (The Harvest). Then Abu Ya'qub al-Sijistani came and defended the author of *al-Mahsul*, insisting on the soundness of his argument and the strength of his theory, and claiming that the matters at issue are not minor Ismaili philosophical fragments or branches that we can pass over — all of which amounts to an affront to the creed and a disruption of its fundamental principles and foundations. Abu Ya'qub appears to have backed certain texts against Abu Hatim al-Razi, attacking him; and in some places he speaks in a manner that has nothing whatever to do with the topic. In any case, certain passages in *al-Mahsul* concern Tawhid, and it was incumbent on Abu Hatim to correct and discuss them. But he did not. He preferred instead the long stories that deal with subjects of lesser importance — historical material drawn from the Torah — and filled his book with that, leaving *al-Mahsul* itself uncorrected and neglected. He thereby created a kind of intellectual confusion among the adherents of the da'wa by presenting them with divergent opinions, especially after they had occupied themselves with the reading of *al-Mahsul* — and this has affected the purity of the idea of Tawhid, corrupting the foundations of the Shari'a and the ordering of the boundaries (al-hudud)."

Al-Kirmani then says: "That is why I have decided to quote here the original sayings contained in *al-Islah* (The Correction), where he corrects some of what is mentioned in *al-Mahsul*, together with what is mentioned in *al-Nusra* (The Defense) in support of *al-Mahsul* — presenting my own opinion and commentary. My purpose is to set forth the origins in their correct sense and to draw the distinctions clearly. When we reach this point, it becomes easy to expose the error, while the correct teaching remains pure of doubt and uncertainty and far from any disparity or confusion."

Al-Kirmani divided *al-Riyad* into ten books (abwab), and each book into several sections (fusul). With his literary and scientific tact, the strength of his expression, and the breadth of his learning, he selected what he considered important and fundamental from *al-Islah* and *al-Nusra*, reading them in his own style — a style clear and concise, a kind of art utterly different from that of al-Razi.

The first book of *al-Riyad* is divided into thirty-eight sections, all dedicated to the subject of the perfection of the soul. The author of *al-Islah* says: "The soul is perfect; it is a complete origin that came from perfection, because the intellect is perfection itself." The author of *al-Nusra* contradicts him: "The author of *al-Islah* does not realize that 'the perfect' is higher and more complete than 'perfection,' for the second — perfection — is an attribute of the first, and is therefore described in it. The perfect, which bears this attribute, is therefore the higher, and the noun of its attribute is appended to it. The perfect exists in its own right; when the perfection exists, it becomes the follower, not the followed."

Here the dispute between truth and fiction intensifies, and the author of *al-Riyad* uncovers the logical error: by 'perfection,' the author of *al-Islah* did not mean perfection in general, but the highest perfection — the intellect — which is the cause of the existence of the perfect soul that has been affected by it. The effect cannot be higher than the cause. Al-Kirmani's position therefore leads us to conclude that al-Sijistani's attack on al-Razi was misplaced and incorrect. Al-Kirmani then takes up the position of al-Razi, who says: although the soul is perfect, its acts are not, because these acts depend on time; the soul is an emanation from the intellect, while the intellect is itself a being composed of origination, time, and perfection, unlike the soul, which emanates within time and depends on the supply flowing to it from the intellect. This dependence neither affects the soul's substance nor renders it deficient — just as the intellect's dependence on the divine will does not render it materially inferior. These perfections or imperfections are born of the soul and are not its substance. One may add that all that is mentioned in the first book of *al-Riyad* — namely, these two quotations — is drawn from *al-Islah*, and that al-Kirmani strongly contests the opinions of *al-Nusra*, holding that al-Sijistani misunderstood these theories. He likens him to a novice settler who has entered the world of ideals with no aim but material vanity.

The author of *al-Nusra* says, citing the saying of Empedocles (1) by way of protest, that "[the soul] came to be either because it was subject to nothing but the Almighty, and then the soul became deficient because it was subjected to nature." We say: the philosophers who preceded these thinkers, even those who were virtuous in their time, remained a source of error in many of the matters they discussed concerning the things of the mind. They spoke about these things purely from rational considerations. Had they lived in our day and seen the springs of blessing — how they overflow from the House of Revelation with the realities of things as they are — the Alawite da'wa would have done their effort justice and expressed for them many of the beliefs they held in the absence of the senses. They would then have been among those for whom God has lit a light upon the mysteries of things. In their own time, however, they followed only their opinions and their own intellects, each going, in his belief, wherever those pointed him.

(1) A great philosopher who lived around 440 BC and was a contemporary of Parmenides, though somewhat younger. His doctrine is related to that of Heraclitus with slight modification. He came from the city of Akragas, on the southern coast of Sicily. He was a democratic politician and claimed himself to be a god. Legends recount that he performed miracles — sometimes by magic, sometimes through his scientific knowledge — and it is narrated that he was able to control the wind and brought a dead woman back to life. All that he wrote was in verse, as Parmenides had done; he was praised by Lucretius, who was influenced by him. Among his contributions to science: he held that air is an independent element, a claim later confirmed by rigorous scientific evidence; he was aware that plants reproduce sexually; he held a theory of evolution and survival of the fittest. In astronomy, he said that the moon is illuminated by rays reflected onto it from the sun. He founded the Italian school of medicine, a school later followed by Plato and Aristotle. He held that earth, air, fire, and water are the four elements, that the material world is spherical in shape, and he supported certain Pythagorean views — though he refused to adopt monotheism. He went further to say that the course of nature is governed by chance and necessity in its order more than it is subordinated to any desired end; in these respects his philosophy is closer to science than the philosophies of Parmenides, Plato, and Aristotle.

Their writings, then, rest on the same ground as the writings of the people of *al-Zahir* in the Hanafi school: when they relied on their own intellects in conducting the paths of religion, their opinions diverged, and among them there were distinctions — some closer to truth, others further from it — while the standard of religion is what is testified to by the horizons and the souls. From the above discussion, and from the demonstration that the belief in this matter, by his own account, is false — free of exaggeration and inflation — what then is the meaning of what Empedocles said, except as the author of *al-Nusra* cited it in his protest? The saying of Empedocles is precisely what we aim to refute. We ask God to enlighten our minds, to seal our affairs and our acts with happiness and good deeds, and to support us by His kindness and generosity.

The second book of *al-Riyad* is divided into nine sections and is dedicated to the relationship between intellect and soul. Al-Sijistani objects to al-Razi for treating the topic of motion and rest — a foundational topic that long caused difficulty for the ancient philosophers. Here, motion and rest are taken as two effects united in 'the First' and 'the Second.' This supports what Abu Hatim al-Razi said: that the First has originated and has become bound up with origination, because the truth of its essence is dual (the originator and the originated); these two effects are present in 'the First.' When the Second was brought into being from the First, that bringing-into-being likewise became dual (the same source emanating, and the same thing emanated), and so the two effects are connected to the two basic principles. As for motion, it is the influence the First exerts upon the Second, which then acts upon all assets and existents; rest, by contrast, is the Second's acceptance of the First's statement in all the basic existents.

Al-Razi says: "I am not claiming that motion and rest are born of the soul through a power received from the intellect. I am insisting that both are traces left in the soul by the power that the intellect generates in form and matter (al-sura wa al-hayula). For this reason, form and matter have become the basis of this world." As for al-Sijistani,


<!-- page 28 -->
He says in his book (Al-Nusra): This argument is incoherent, because movement is generated from a particular intention (("to seek it")), and the movement of physical bodies is intended to fill a void — such as the upward movement of flame or the downward movement of earth, and so on.

From this line of reasoning, al-Kirmani concludes that al-Razi was right while al-Sijistani was wrong.

The author of (Al-Islah) said: We say that movement and stillness are two effects united in the First and the Second, because when the First was creating, it was united with creating, and by that union it was acted upon by power. But we say "two effects" because creating and the creator are two: one is creating and the other is the creator himself; and although creating and the creator are two, they are one — namely the All. These two effects exist first in potency and are then emitted: the Second is from the First, and from that emission two effects actually arose in it. One is the Resurrection itself, and the other is the Origin itself. The two origins are united in matter, and one of the two effects is movement, which is higher than the other, namely stillness — just as one of the originals, the First, is higher than the other.

The third chapter discusses the topic, "Are the soul and the spirit alike to the First?" This chapter is divided into six sections and begins with phrases from the book (Al-Nusra) — these being among the texts cited in the book (Al-Islah) — composed in a way that elucidates their original meaning. Here al-Kirmani says: al-Sijistani criticizes what al-Razi never said.

The author of (Al-Islah) said, in reply to what the author of (Al-Nusra) had set out to prove: We do not say that natures and dark skeletons are born from the Second so as to make the Second confirm the First, because that world is the world of honor and virtue while this world is the world of darkness and gloom; and the Second, even if it is born from the First, resembles it and does not resemble the state of al-Hayuli. The author of Al-Nusra said: This is a pure mistake, for if that world were illuminated —
- 26

<!-- page 29 -->
there would be no darkness in it, and this world is dark and there is no light in it, and nothing could fall between the two.

In the fourth chapter, which contains eight sections, al-Kirmani addresses the topic of whether souls are parts of the first truths. He draws attention to the fact that al-Sijistani changes the meaning of what is stated in the book (Al-Islah) by altering the structure of its phrases. Al-Kirmani agrees with the opinion concerning partial souls — namely, that the sum of these souls does not constitute the universal soul. Al-Sijistani then returns and speaks about this world, and in his discourse he alludes to the complete truths and says that the human soul belongs to the region between the world of nature and the world of resurrection. It is the composite known by natural force, and the soul may be elevated to the highest world by practicing piety and then by uniting with the Second. Al-Sijistani does not stop at this point; he says:

The intellect is the first resurrection, while the speaking souls are the second resurrection. What occurred in the first resurrection is also resurrected in the second.

Al-Kirmani criticizes this opinion and refers the reader to what he has written — especially to the epistle (The Preacher) and (The Illuminated) — where he says: "The intellect is nothing other than the Word itself, and the Word is nothing other than the intellect itself. This is corroborated by the saying of our master Imam al-Mu'izz li-Din Allah — may God's prayers be upon him — in the book (Interpretation of the Sharia)(1), where he speaks of intellects and indicates that the Word is the intellect, and that these names belong to it. In addition, we have already explained and discussed the Creator, the intellect, the Word, and what is said concerning the world of creating and the first resurrection in the epistle (Illuminating) and others. These theses suffice the researcher."

(1) This book is among the writings of Qadi al-Nu'man ibn Hayyun al-Maghribi al-Tamimi, and al-Kirmani's reference here to a work classified under Imam al-Mu'izz al-Fatimi indicates that Imam al-Mu'izz is the source of his information.
- 27

<!-- page 30 -->
Al-Kirmani does not stop at this point. He goes on to quote the sayings of al-Razi recorded in the book (Al-Islah), where he says:

Movement and stillness stand to the form and the image as the soul stands to the body — and the form and the image stand to movement and stillness as the body stands to the soul — because the soul is subtler than the body, and the body is denser than the soul; and movement and stillness are subtler than form and image.

It seems that al-Sijistani rejects this analysis, supporting the theory of the author of (Al-Mahsul). Al-Kirmani says that both were wrong — especially al-Razi — with regard to movement, which is the action of the engine that we call the soul; while stillness is the cessation of the engine's action, and so on. If the engine is the soul, it cannot resemble movement, which is its own action; and the same applies to stillness. Furthermore, it is impossible to join movement and stillness in a single thing, because they are mutually exclusive, whereas the form and the image are mixed inseparably and constitute a unity. This topic has been fully expounded in the book (Rest of the Mind)(2).

The fifth chapter is divided into seven sections, and its subject is that the human being is the fruit of the material world — as al-Razi says — and that we regard him as belonging to the whole world and to its foundation and roots. Al-Hayuli, too, is the basis of the subjectivity formed within it by movement, stillness, and other factors that lead to the formation of different souls — such as region and the like. Al-Sijistani names this philosophy "the world," which does not acknowledge the existence of the soul after its separation from the body. The orientalist (Goldziher), in the Islamic Encyclopedia, gives a long explanation of these beliefs —

(2) It is very strange to find here that the book (Rest of the Mind) was written before (Al-Riyad), even though al-Kirmani cites (Al-Riyad) on pages 22, 23, and 364. It comes to mind that the cross-citation in the two books was probably inserted later.
- 28

<!-- page 31 -->
— this school, but without giving a full and inevitable answer; and it is believed that this group is the school of Democritus and Epicurus, followed by Lucretius. Al-Kirmani says that both are right in one respect and wrong in another. Man is the result of the interaction of natural, planetary, and physical action; and man is the purpose of the Second and the purpose of the work of nature. The meaning is that man is created and divided into two parts — one belonging to dead nature, and one belonging to God — and therefore he is the highest of creatures.

We say: How can what he has said be true, when order and arrangement come only from the intellect? Or how is it true that universal things are perfected only by the intellect and the soul, while that which is not affected by their effects is the same as the animals — for which reason God lifted the pen from the soul until the effects of the intellect take hold in it? We have said above that the issue lies in the agents, not in totalities — not every agent seeks by his action to achieve a particular goal or purpose, as is decreed by saying that the First Intellect itself is not a compound, so it must stand in order and arrangement; but the intellect only attains true perception.

The sixth chapter is divided into six sections and revisits the theme of movement, stillness, form, and image. It maintains that what is reported in the book (Al-Mahsul), and what was conveyed by Abu Hatim al-Razi and al-Sijistani, was all wrong; and it refers the reader to consult his book (Al-Masabih).

The author of (Al-Nusra) then undertakes to define the body and the soul. He defines the body by its accepting the three dimensions, then defines the soul as the similarity of parts, making it the course of water, fire, and air — each of which is similar in its parts. This is impossible, in addition to what was said above, because the soul does not become a body; and "soul" and "spirit" are synonymous names for one thing, and that thing is not corporeal. If it were a body, the nine qualities and accidents that the body requires would necessarily belong to it, and it could have been grasped by a sense —
- 29

<!-- page 32 -->
— and no illusion of the nine accidents, nor even one of them, would remain in it. And when there are no physical norms in it, it must not be a body. Our well-known book (With Lamps) contains what cures this.

The seventh chapter is divided into seven sections and deals with the classification of the world's composite materials. In fact, it is dedicated in its entirety to explaining the human station, which is a class of its own, and its relation to the rest. This is why al-Razi says: man is a plant that grows upside-down upon the world's substances. Al-Sijistani agrees with him in one respect but differs from him in another, since the human body decomposes or dissolves into such substances. Al-Razi classes the human being and likens him to an animal because he possesses the senses, while al-Sijistani attributes this faculty of feeling to the growing vegetative soul. Al-Razi says that the speaking human soul itself constitutes the point of difference from the animal, while al-Sijistani replies that the animal also possesses the senses. When al-Kirmani weighs the two opinions, he rules that al-Razi is closer to the truth than al-Sijistani. He finally says:

The resemblance is not only between man and the material world, but also between him and the spiritual world — the one that is united in itself — and everything preceded him in the scale of existence. He then displays the parallels between him and everything in the material and spiritual worlds:

"In the world there are different movements, and in the human being likewise the six movements. In the world there is an engine that moves it, and in the human being there is also an engine that moves it, namely the soul. In the world there are the four elements, and in the human being likewise the four natures. In the world there are rivers, valleys, different tastes, mountains, and minerals; and in the human being likewise — as the author of (Al-Islah) has shown — bones, cartilage, flesh, blood, what flows from it, and what issues from it. In the world there is that which grows and increases without sensation; and in the human being likewise — that which grows and increases without sensation, such as hair and nails, and his body, which grows and increases from his youth to his old age. In the world there is the sentient animal as well —"
- 30

<!-- page 33 -->
"— and in the human being the sentient faculties. The whole world is structures and compositions, fully constituted by what exists; and one aspect of this is the analogy with the world. What we have mentioned is a brief summary, which our writers detail under the title 'Rest of the Mind' (Rahat al-Aql)."

The eighth chapter is divided into twenty-four sections, and its subject is judgment and destiny (al-qada and al-qadar). These two expressions are mentioned in the Holy Quran and are taken as bearing distinct synonyms; yet both signify "judgment" (al-qada). This topic left its mark on the long scholarly debate that took place in the Islamic era and did not arrive at a decisive result. Thousands of Islamic theological books have been written on this topic — but in fact the question is misplaced and time spent on it is wasted.

Al-Razi, the author of (Al-Islah), says: In view of the saying of (Al-Mahsul), judgment agrees with the First — that is, with what precedes — while destiny agrees with the Second, that is, with what follows. This is wholly mistaken, because destiny precedes judgment just as the First precedes the Second. The meaning of destiny is estimation — namely, that He has made the thing possible — and the meaning of judgment is detail, namely the separation of the thing or its parts. And the Second, that is, the manifest judgment, cannot precede the First. Al-Razi cites several verses from the Holy Quran to support his interpretation, and he likens destiny, in its detail, to the cloth drawn in the tailor's imagination before he cuts it (i.e., judges it) and gives it its form, after which its change is no longer possible.

Al-Sijistani objects to this interpretation in defense of his basic theory — which agrees with what is stated in the book (Al-Mahsul) — and says that judgment does not mean detail, even though destiny means estimation. The first describes and obliges, while the second is empty and final; and the tailor's analogy does not fit the case, because judgment is —
- 31

<!-- page 34 -->
— the idea of the garment that results from the art of tailoring (which is sewing); and the second is grounded in the soul, which is estimation, and therefore it has become destiny. Then al-Kirmani comes and discusses the ideas and opinions, saying: If judgment agrees with the First and with the natiq (speaker), and destiny with the Second and with the asas (foundation), we must concede that the speaker corresponds to the foundation as shown in the book — though it is the speaker who is responsible for defining the Sharia. But it is wrong to conflate the speaker and the foundation, because the speaker is concerned with the material world, while the foundation is concerned with the spiritual world. The proverb of the speaker and the foundation is that of the sun and the moon. Here there is an aspect worth noting: some preachers tended to place the rank of the foundation above the rank of the speaker, saying that spiritual worship is higher than the natural Shari'a worship.

The author of (Al-Nusra) related a story from the scholars: When God Almighty created the intellect, He emptied it of the two worlds, because it is the sum of the images of the two worlds. And we say: the intellect that is the sum of the images of the two worlds is not the First Creative Intellect, nor the Second Intellect — which is the first emanator — but rather the intellect that rises from the world of nature by acquisition. In order to parallel the first emission, a second emission emerged, and thus it became a collection of the images of the worlds, despite their multitude, and it may engrave itself with the images of created things.

The ninth chapter is divided into thirty-three sections. In it al-Kirmani speaks about the basis of the principle and the law of Adam and the laws that came after him. He then proceeds to refer to the Great Resurrection, saying: The Great Resurrection will come when the doors of religious instruction are closed, when the call (da'wa) that has been completed stops, and when the Qa'im dissolves the borders in the administration of the da'wa — after which it is no longer necessary, wherever the matter has reached, for a new creation to appear.

This chapter also includes sections devoted to the question of whether Adam — mentioned in the Holy Quran as one of God's interlocutors, conveying revelation to humanity, and called natiq (in Ismaili phrasing, the one who holds the role) — truly preached the law of —
- 32


<!-- page 35 -->
new, and to whom, and how? In this chapter we find no attachment to philosophical conjecture; the concern is only with how to gather and explain the few symbols and signs scattered through the scriptures, and how to combine and reconcile these often contradictory references in order to extract a convincing theory.

The Christians made Adam — the simple man mentioned in the Old Testament of the Bible (the Torah) — into a mighty figure quite different from others. Islam, alongside a great deal of other views, inherited this new story about Adam. Of all the groups, the Ismailis came closest to the truth about his role, because they considered him to have stood at the beginning of the world. It is enough proof that the angels were commanded to prostrate to him in his honor. In the Ismaili system, the seven speaker-prophets (nutaqa) are acknowledged as a first set rather than a final one, so Adam — the first — and the others differ slightly in their work and duties, because Adam did not have the "determination" (azm), and the Qa'im will not come bearing a new law. This is why Muhammad is considered the seal of the apostles, and his law the last.

Al-Kirmani mentions the Great Resurrection because at that time the call (da'wa) will have been completed and the borders (hudud) will no longer be effective, the task of education having come to an end, and there will be a new creation. It seems this idea reverses the intention of what is expected from the Ismaili call when it reaches its summit — when the whole world becomes united under their authority, owing allegiance to one religious doctrine, namely the true law of the Islamic religion that they preach.

The author of *al-Nusra*, after his interpretation of what Allah said in the story of the son of Noah — "I will take refuge on a mountain that will protect me from the water" — appears to be saying: "I will take refuge in a successor (lahiq)." Then Noah says: "There is no protector today from the command of Allah," meaning that the matter could not be received by way of the suffixes, for he did not know the rank of revelation, even if it was through a suffix.

The Book of Riyad [3]

<!-- page 36 -->
Adam had a revelation for his son so that he might know that.

As for the tenth chapter, which is the last of the book, it is a critique of all the passages that were mentioned in the book *al-Mahsul* (The Harvest), which Abu Hatim al-Razi left uncorrected. There are roughly nine passages in the fifteenth chapter of this section, and we consider it necessary to provide a translation of the basic passages contained in *al-Mahsul* to give an idea of its style. Al-Kirmani's commentary in the second stanza is presented as an example of this method. He says:

In fact, the author of *al-Mahsul*, when he undertook the propagation and began to explain the foundations and rules of the call, opened the doors of new milestones by writing a number of the books of those who were involved in it. The majority accepted his judgment on *al-Mahsul* despite the apology of the author of *al-Islah*; and this came as proof of error and of the complexity of the subject. Unfortunately, the reality of the situation turns out to be the opposite of what they saw and imagined. Al-Razi's corrections prove that he did not challenge the value of *al-Mahsul* or its author, neither by exposing faults nor by passing judgment on them, because al-Razi was one of the men specially concerned with repairing and elevating religious knowledge, with explaining the truth of the faith, and with affirming man's duties toward God.

From the opinions of al-Kirmani recorded in his books, you would judge him to be a great author and a writer trained in manners and principles. One of his epistles is *al-Wa'iz* (The Preacher); from it one sees that he registers an important observation and corrects it, where another would have resorted to insult and abuse. Here are some of the passages he corrects:

1 — The author of *al-Islah* says, on the subject of monotheism: God is the Creator of things and other-than-things — intelligible, intellectual, and logical.

<!-- page 37 -->
He means that such materials or topics may come under the rule of such elements, or may not.

Al-Kirmani replies: this contradicts the theory that the intellect is the foundation of creation, behind which there is no god, and that nothing can precede the intellect in its existence or be prior to it. This theory entails partnership; it contradicts the law of monotheism. The statement about the creation of "things and other-than-things," both material and non-material — unless he has confused and imagined the illusion that there is no world other than this world and nothing that negates creation — raises the question: is it possible to say that God is the creator and at the same time to deny this?

2 — In the fourth chapter, the author of *al-Mahsul* says: The Creator made things from nothing only, and there is nothing with Him, and He has no end. And if we say only "He and nothing," we would have denied things and other-than-things, and would have made the two of them creators together. We have said that His self (His identity), whatever idea it may be, is a simple, mounted image; and we made everything continuous and non-continuous and brought it into this existence by reason of an infinite and a finite effect, because nothing comes after the thing, and because the negation of a name is possible only after that name has come into existence; and the word "no" is not an idea but the negation of every idea.

This is part of associationism (shirk) and refutes the theory of monotheism. Al-Kirmani refers the student to his treatise *al-Rawda* and to *Tafsir al-Shari'a* by Imam al-Fatimi al-Mu'izz li-Din Allah.

3 — In the eighth chapter, the author of *al-Mahsul* says, in the section investigating the First Creator: that Allah Almighty created the world all at once — meaning that He created the intellect with a single sentence, and by His power and decree displayed images of existence within it, the ideas of the world and everything it contains, without indicating anything on its own. The intellect has seniority.

<!-- page 38 -->
Al-Kirmani protests against this statement because it implies and decides that the intellect works independently, relying on its own thinking; this is a great error, because the intellect is only a mediator in divine work, not another creator.

4 — The author of *al-Mahsul* says in the ninth chapter: Eternity and its causality is the unity of God, and the unity of God is eternal. We see al-Kirmani rejecting the idea of "eternal unity," viewing it as a mediator between God and the intellect. The second is itself the substance of unity for one primary cause; at the same time it is the first, the cause of the act of creation and of the creature, the perfect and the perfected, the eternal and the everlasting, existence and what exists.

5 — In chapter 11, the author of *al-Mahsul* says: The image is visible in the intellect from the unity of God; so the abstract image for it becomes eternal, because the intellect too becomes wholly eternal and not partial. Al-Kirmani also tends to speak of basic facts while he is thinking about the manifestations of the physical world, and suggests that this applies to the speaker-prophet's intellect, or to the foundation, or to the predecessor, more than it applies to the underlying matter. And this is "the nearest angel," or the invisible pen that writes upon the tablet of existence.

6 — The author of *al-Mahsul* says in the twelfth chapter: Since the intellect is caused by the eternal — which is called "the word of God" — and since there is no third between them, the intellect becomes similar to it. Al-Kirmani objects to this view, saying that the intellect and the word are one and the same.

7 — In the thirteenth chapter, the author of *al-Mahsul* says that the intellect has received the name "Complete" (Tam) because it has become the creator, and because creativity has been carried out by the perfect creator; therefore the creator is perfect, which is itself created.

<!-- page 39 -->
That is, "creativity" can only be complete.

We see al-Kirmani objecting: God has no attributes. The creator or originator imposes his superiority over the creature.

8 — In the fourteenth chapter, the author of *al-Mahsul* says: The intellect carries the ideas, and the loft that is its source — which is "the Word" exactly — is like the sun, which sends light through its brilliance and not by natural friction or matter. If the work of the intellect is the result of the power of the Word and its ability to convey, then it becomes clear that the Word is the cause of everything that comes from the intellect, and not the intellect itself; the second, which is the intellect, is only an intermediary between the Word and the limits below it. Here too al-Kirmani objects, saying: such an arrangement applies only to the intellects of the speaker-prophets.

9 — Finally, in the fifteenth chapter, the author of *al-Mahsul* says: The process of creativity is a medium between the creator and the created; the same is true of the agent who is entrusted with it. He adds: the idea of creativity depends on the agent who influences the image, which is transmitted from the creature to the creator.

This last section contains intermittent sentences referring to the same topic, drawn from different clues. As expected, al-Kirmani rejects these statements, saying: this does not agree with the belief of the monotheists, because the act of the creature is here considered independent of the Creator.

In fact, the idea of monotheism and understanding the knowledge of the limits (hudud) is extremely difficult; and by attaining its knowledge, souls survive, grow, become stable, and endure beyond the world of matter.

The author of *al-Mahsul* said: Creativity is a mediator between the creator and the created.

<!-- page 40 -->
It is the effect of the influencer upon the influenced; and he said: the image of creativity is intermediate between the doer and the effect, and its existence is on the side of the creative doer, so the effect of the image is on the side of the creative in the effect, and this image — I mean creativity — became in the creative from the Creative Almighty.

This is the book *al-Riyad* by the great preacher Hamid al-Din Ahmad al-Kirmani. This is the sum total of his presentation of the philosophical opinions that were the subject of controversy in that era — opinions which al-Kirmani highlighted, refined in his fine hand, and brought forth as some of the best philosophical texts in the field.

In fact, the author of *al-Riyad* deserves the thanks of generations and the appreciation of the Islamic world, because his book is a fruit of thought, a tall tree that grew in the shade of this vast world.

Manuscript and method of investigation

When I was seconded by the Ismaili Scientific Society in India, the great orientalist Prof. W. Ivanow — secretary of the Society — assigned me to edit the book *al-Riyad* and supervise its edition. He sent me the first copy of the original, which is symbolized by the letter (A) and was adopted as the basis for the investigation. After browsing it and finding it full of grammatical errors as well as missing and confused texts, I was prompted to write to him apologizing that it was certainly not possible to realize the project and reach the goal of producing a correct text unless it could be compared with at least one other copy. About a month passed, and then he sent me another copy on microfilm from Tehran, which is symbolized by the letter (B). This one helped me greatly because it came out more upright, more purposeful,

<!-- page 41 -->
and more correct than the first — although the first is the more beautiful in handwriting and arrangement, as the photographs show.

Copy history

At the end of copy (A) it is stated as follows:

The first day of the month of Rabi' al-Thani in the year 1354 of the Hijra, corresponding to the second of August 1935, in the handwriting of Adam ibn Sheikh al-Majid Muhammad Ali al-Surati from India.

Copy (B) is also Indian; at the end of it is written:

Its copies were branched on Wednesday the twenty-eighth of the month of Shawwal in the year 1355 of the Prophet's Hijra; the name of the scribe is not mentioned, regrettably.

Final word

Now is added to the series of historical and philosophical books which I have edited another book: *al-Riyad* by al-Kirmani. I can only record here a word of thanks and gratitude to Professor W. Ivanow, in recognition of what he has done and continues to do for the service of scholarship, and for the way he strives that the greatest possible number of Ismaili manuscripts may be properly edited for the world of being — all for a single purpose: to introduce the Islamic world to this cosmological philosophy that has occupied minds since time immemorial and has been the focus of attention of scholars, and the orbit of their excavations in the East and the West.

Aref Tamer
Beirut — Lebanon
1960

In whose sanctuary an idol still stood, where the landmarks of the Hanifi religion (1) were under threat, where the fire of disbelief and unbelief was burning, where the edifices of polytheism and idolatry (2) were being erected, and where the people had sunk deep into misguidance and were caught in the blindness of adversity — he rose up for God as a diligent striver, and he struggled, and he lamented for them what had befallen them in their veils of darkness. (1) the ruling Imam (2), the Commander of the Faithful, and his fathers, the Guardians of the Faithful — the family of Taha and Yasin, peace be upon them all.

To proceed: the most deserving matter for the believer in monotheism to attend to and devote his efforts toward is what leads to the unity of Allah Almighty and the knowledge of His limits — to an ordered creed of certain belief, calling for the dispelling of confusion (3) and darkness (4) from the souls of the believers, also touching on the unity of the Lord of the Worlds.

When I saw that Shaykh Hatim al-Razi (5) (may Allah have mercy on him) had set about correcting in the book *al-Mahsul* (The Harvest) (7) what was claimed to be corrupt, and that Shaykh Abu Ya'qub al-Sijzi (8) (may Allah have mercy on him) had defended the author of *al-Mahsul* and held his statements to be sound, and saw that what they disputed was not among the branches in which disagreement is permissible while the roots remain sound — I found that Shaykh Abu Ya'qub al-Sijzi (may Allah have mercy on him) was, in some of what he said, sound in the path he took to rebut [his opponent], and was unjust to Shaykh Abu Hatim (may Allah have mercy on him) in others, and that some (9) of their words came out in disordered fashion; and that nonetheless there remained in the book *al-Mahsul*, in its author's own words in the chapter on monotheism and the First Intellect in particular, apart from the branches, matter that it was obligatory upon Shaykh Abu Hatim (may Allah have mercy on him) (10) to correct and to speak to — and who is more deserving
