of correcting them than him, since his statements ran beyond the length of his own book. So he left the matter aside and neglected it, and that was the sum of it. He grows angry with the people of the Guiding Da'wa whenever they take a stand against him over a point of difference.

And in [matters touching] the paths of monotheism, and (1) the knowledge of the limits of imbalance, I judged that I should cite the sayings of each one of them — this from the book *al-Islah* (2) of [the author of] *al-Islah*, and that from the book *al-Nusra* (3) of [the author of *al-Nusra*]. We set their points against one another, and I speak of what must be said about them: what is meant by them, and the correct way to ascribe their meanings to him, so that (4) the sound side may be distinguished from the rest, the partiality of whichever of them is in the wrong may become plain, and (5) the meaning of what they differ upon may be discerned, so that one may then judge accordingly. Thereafter I speak of what I noted [its author] had neglected to correct in the book *al-Mahsul* — among foundational matters in which disagreement is not permissible — and I make the truth plain in pursuit of [divine] reward. I have done so, and gathered all of that in this book, which I have titled the Book of *al-Riyad* (The Gardens), on the adjudication between the two (6) — the author of *al-Islah* and the author of *al-Nusra* — because, in what it collects of their statements and what it quotes (7), it draws a separation between them: a clarification of what one of them persisted in misjudging and what (8) the other neglected to correct in the book *al-Mahsul*, fulfilling thereby the duty owed therein, drawing on the blessings of the saints of Allah upon them, peace be upon them; for it is (10) like a *riyad* — a garden of greenery for the eye and of fruit ready to the hand. Allah helps us, and He guides him in His land to bring forth the thing (11) from the traces of error, though we have no knowledge of it ourselves. The book gathers ten chapters (abwab), all of them comprising one hundred and fifty-seven sections (fusul). Allah is the Helper; He is the Protector and the Trustee.

**Chapter One**
From the Book of *al-Riyad*, on the adjudication between the two companions [the author of *al-Islah* and the author of *al-Nusra*], on what he had to say concerning the Soul, which is the first emanator.

**Section One**

The author of *al-Islah* said: The Soul is perfect (tamm) in itself because it is emanated from the First Intellect, and the First [Intellect] is perfection (kamal); for the emanation of the perfect proceeds from perfection, because the Intellect is itself perfection.

The author of *al-Nusra* said concerning the Soul: He does not know that perfection (kamal) is more excellent and more complete than the perfect (tamm), because (1) perfection is an attribute and the perfect is the described, perfection is the carried and the perfect is the carrier — and the described and the carrier are more excellent and more complete than the attribute and the carried, since the perfect is described by perfection (2) and bears it, and perfection is the attribute of the perfect, predicated upon it — as they say.

And we say: What the author of *al-Islah* intends, when he says that the Soul is perfect because it is emanated [from the First Intellect], is not whether perfection is or is not an attribute, or (3) whether perfection is described or

1 – In copy (a) it comes as if.
2 – In copy (a) it comes as (to complete).
3 – Inserted with (f) without (a) in copy (a):
- 53

<!-- page 56 -->
not described. Rather, what he means is that perfection (kamal) is like the cause by which the existence of the effect is brought about. This is because he held that perfection is the cause of the perfect, and that it is superior to it in rank — for were it not for perfection, the perfect would not be found, as is evident from the fact that if perfection is removed in supposition (al-wahm), the perfect is removed (1) along with its removal, just as with the sources from which the past, future, and subject [forms] are derived: if the source is removed, [those forms] are removed with its removal. There is no way for the emanated soul to be found at the level of the First Intellect, since the First Intellect was the first existent, and from it [the soul] came into being. So [the author of *al-Islah*] said: it [the First Intellect] is perfection — and the carrying-out of what he called the emanated soul is like the effect — and he said: it [the soul] is the perfect. And if the intent of the author of *al-Islah* was that the First Intellect is in truth perfection — and that it is in truth what the author of *al-Nusra* described in this way — then they have been unjust to the author of *al-Islah*.

**Section Two**

Then the author of *al-Nusra* said: Perfection is more excellent than the perfect — even though the words [are spoken] in this place concerning the First Originated, which is the First Effect and the First Emanation, and not concerning anything else. Nobility must be ascribed to the effect, not — perhaps because the perfect's being perfect amounted to no more than the perfection without which it would not be perfect at all; and if [perfection] is a thing, then that thing is its cause; and if that is (2) so, then perfection is the cause of the perfect and the perfect is the effect of perfection. And if perfection is the cause of the perfect, and the perfect is the effect of perfection, and yet [the author of *al-Islah*] gave the perfect honor and excellence over perfection — then it would follow that the effect must be more honorable than its cause, and this is impossible. For its existence is by [the cause's] existence: were [the cause] to be removed, its existence would be removed by that removal — that very existence by which it exists is inferior to it in honor and rank. And since that is impossible, the author of *al-Nusra* in what he said was unjust to the author of *al-Islah*.

4 – The conjunction (and) appeared in copy (A).
5 – Dropped in copy (A).
- 54

<!-- page 57 -->
**Section Three**

The author of *al-Nusra* said: perfection is an attribute, the perfect is the described; perfection is the carried, the perfect is the carrier — and this is an indication that the perfect is more honorable than perfection, just as the carrier is more honorable than the carried.

We say: This is not absolutely true with respect to our existence in terms of universes and emanations. The second [principle] is what is carried, and is more honorable than its bearer — as is the case with the vegetative power, which is (1) the proximate cause of the existence of the developmental power, and is its carrier, while the developmental power is a carried form within it and is its perfecting [complement] — and the developmental power is more honorable than the vegetative; and likewise the developmental power, which is the proximate cause of the existence of the sensitive power, and is its carrier, while the sensitive is a form carried within it and is its perfecting [complement] — and the sensitive is more honorable than the developmental; and likewise the sensitive power, which is the proximate cause of the existence of the rational power, and is its carrier, while the rational is a carried form within it and is its perfecting [complement] [and the rational is more honorable than the sensitive]. The honor of *this* is not like the honor of *that*. Therefore it cannot be the case, from the standpoint of origination and the First Emanation, that the First Intellect be the carrier and the Second Intellect emanated from it be the carried — or that it [the First Intellect] is the perfect, which is an attribute of the perfect that is the Second Intellect. And if that is so, then the inference of the author of *al-Nusra* in the rebuttal of what he mentioned was unjust to the author of *al-Islah*; and the meaning of what he said in it is not sound — unless it is applied to natural things far removed from what (3) the author of *al-Islah* is speaking about, namely originative and emanative principles.

2 – In version (B) it comes as and the powers.
3 – In copy (b) what he is talking about came up.

<!-- page 58 -->

The statement of the author of al-Islah is correct with regard to the world of origination and emanation, and the statement of the author of al-Nusra — even though the author of al-Islah is mistaken in his own framing — is correct with regard to the world of bodies and their accidents.

## Chapter Four

Now consider what we are about to say.

Perfection is a name that applies first to the First Thing, which is the First Cause — and which is the First Perfection. The first effect is completed by adding its existence to that which is above it. Its existence and its completeness are realized by the addition of itself to what is above it, as in the case of origination. According to this scheme, origination is not something other than the originated, but is what has its very being from the Originator. It is itself the same entity: when added to what is above it, it counts as an originated thing; and when added to the character of the originated event itself, it counts as origination.

The First Intellect is therefore perfection, and is perfect in truth. Yet it still requires that which holds back from non-existence — the perfect being that which has nothing of its kind outside itself, just as existence (2) is in nature. So too in origination: "body" is a name that applies to that which is perfect in its three dimensions, with nothing of its kind outside it that is like it; whatever is like it is from it. There are few such things: the sun is complete, and there is no other sun like it in its essence and outside it.

In sum, the saying that the existence of the First Intellect, in addition to itself, is imperfect — that deficiency does not lie in itself; it lies in its addition to what is above it. All things are perfect in

> 1 — In copy (A) it is rendered "and its stature."
> 2 — In copy (B) it is rendered "as it exists."
> 3 — In copy (B) the reading is "what was…"

— 56 —

<!-- page 59 -->

themselves, because their existence comes from the perfection that is the First Intellect, and there is no deficiency in it. Were it deficient in itself, its existence would be impossible to ascribe to it (1).

It is also impossible that something else stand to it as deficient, because the deficient is impossible — that there be anything above it that needs the perfection of itself. Likewise, the existence of something for which there is no possibility of being so is impossible. Rather, deficiency is obligatory for it only when added to what is above it in rank, not in itself. For the self (2), insofar as it is the subject of one thing — like numbers, which are perfect in themselves while their decrease arises by their addition to others — is exactly like all the beings of the world: each is complete in itself, with no deficiency in it (3) so long as they are taken as objects in their own right. They become incomplete only in terms of their order within the array of forms, where some images are more honorable than others, such as (4) the human being, who is more honorable than all the images.

So the human being is in itself as perfect as any other, and it is deficient only in addition to those things above it. If this is the case in the physical (5), then the way the bodies relate to one another mirrors this same scheme of rank. Therefore Allah said:

> "We will show them Our signs in the horizons and within their own selves until it becomes clear to them that it is the truth."

(The verse: Quran 41:53.) — meaning that we will establish for them the imams and the bounds in the pure and the subtle, to serve as proofs of the condition of the observed bodies, and of the souls whose actions sustain their existence. For what they have missed of the knowledge of the bounds of Allah — which is the truth — becomes clear (6) to them through the image (7).

## Chapter Five

The author of al-Islah said:

What needs perfection is the action of the soul, not the soul itself, because the action of the soul can only be carried out in time, and the soul's revival is itself in time — just as the First Intellect and time are one. The First Creator is creativity, time, and perfection (1) all in one; the Creator created all essences at once. The soul, even if it needs to benefit from the First, is not thereby deficient in itself. The First was not deficient even though it was in need of being commanded, for completeness and deficiency (2) appear in the action of the soul, not in the soul itself.

The author of al-Nusra said:

He did not realize that the intellect does not need anything in the way the soul needs to benefit from the intellect. The state of the intellect with respect to matter is not like the state of the soul with respect to the intellect: since matter, from the standpoint of the intellect, is nothing other than the intellect — their identity is one identity — it is impossible to say that a thing needs what is itself. Need arises only when one thing needs another, and only if it is genuinely something else. So they said.

We say:

The saying of the author of al-Nusra is an answer to what the author of al-Islah obliged — namely, that the intellect needs only matter. And since the talk turns on principles drawn from outside the natural world, the author of al-Nusra in this matter has taken precedence over the truth more than the saying of the author of al-Islah. It is worth saying that the soul does need perfection, and that this can only be realized in time, because the soul is an emanation that emerges into the natural world, not from outside it.

> 1 — In copy (B) it is rendered "not."
> 2 — In copy (B) the reading is "the soul."

— 58 —

<!-- page 61 -->

These are the souls that do not attain the degree of perfection — that is, the elevation to the degree of the Second Perfection — except in time, since their existence is the existence of emanation. If the statement of the author of al-Islah is carried in this way, then it is correct.

## Chapter Six

The saying of the author of al-Islah — that intellect and time are one, because the Creator is creativity, time, and perfection all one — is not correct, because time is the number of the movements of the celestial sphere: the imaginary duration between the movement of the first degree of it and the movement of what follows from the next degree to where the first was. Origination, which is the intellect, and its perfection precede the celestial sphere whose degrees give rise to the existence of time. The existence of time is therefore subsequent to the existence of the First Intellect.

His belief is contrary to what is established in the law of the Guiding Mission, and we have shown the relation between the universe, time, and their order of existence — and that the existence of the universe precedes time — and indicated all this from the law of the Prophet ﷺ in our book known as *Rahat al-'Aql* (1). There is enough in it for the seekers.

## Chapter Seven

The author of al-Nusra said:

If the intellect did not deny its own divinity, the Following One would not have exceeded its knowledge from the level of the intellect to the Originator. What deficiency is more evident than this — if it does not know the Originator except from the First side?

We say:

> 1 — *Rahat al-'Aql* is among the books of the late preacher Sayyidina Hamid al-Din Ahmad al-Kirmani (Hujjat al-Iraqayn). Edited by Drs. Mustafa Helmy and Mohamed Kamel Hussain and published by the Ismaili Cultural Society in Bombay, India.

— 59 —

<!-- page 62 -->

It is not permissible to hold the view stated above, because the power deposited in all the honorable beings in the world of unity gives them the knowledge that they have a Creator. Were it not for that power, they would neither exist nor endure — they would vanish. For this reason every thing is described as Oneness; and even what is multiple in itself owes its share to that power. The power is unseen and the justice of it is manifest. It is impossible to imagine a single human being who does not know that he has a Creator, or who fantasizes that he has no Creator.

As for the Great Limit, we say: he grasps by his very existence what means he is the True Idol of the truth — even if the truth is in him and he is its owner. So if we call the First Intellect by what the author of al-Nusra has mentioned, and call the Following One what he said, then it is obligatory that the Following be the basis of the Natiq's establishment in the world of the body, just as he is the Following in the world of intellect. For were it not for the Natiq, the people would not have known that they have a god.

This is not the case, however, since each of them has its share from Allah — but the Natiq takes precedence in rank because he is closest to the First and is after Him. That is why Allah said:

> "It is not for the sun to overtake the moon, nor does the night outstrip the day; each swims in an orbit."

(Quran 36:40.) All (1) are equal and yet at different ranks.

## Chapter Eight

The author of al-Nusra said:

A further sign of the lack of deficiency is that all the First Ones place themselves in this order. They said that images are always longing —

(2) longing for matters, and the matters have no survival except through forms.

We say:

His statement that the soul is the image of the truth, and his statement that the intellect is like a body and matter — since the talk was on the First Intellect — was prejudice against the author of al-Islah,

> 1 — In copy (B) it reads "and all."
> 2 — In copy (B) it reads "and I longed."

— 60 —

<!-- page 63 -->

addressing him by this terminology. "Intellect" is a common name, and what he said is not true in the paradoxical case of one of the first originated and emanated intellects; but it is true in the world of nature — of the other intellects: of the intellect in potentiality, the intellect by acquired aptitude, and the intellect in act. If the words were paradoxical, he carried what was said of the natural onto what was paradoxical, and his contradiction was prejudice.

He said: "Images always long and yearn for materials, and materials have no survival of anything other than images." This is not correct, because images do not long for materials (1). If materials did not exist for the sake of images, they would not be what materials acquire. Rather, it is materials that yearn for images for their own existence — for the sake of images, which acquire the materials, the materials being added to images of vice (2) and to the most honorable image (3). And if that is so, and the soul has conducted the course of the image, and the intellect is the stream of matter, and it has been ruled that matter has no survival except by images (4), it is as if he has made it necessary that the existence of the intellect lies in the soul. How then is one to repel his belief that what exists is a creative existence, or how is one to imagine the existence of intellect and soul, given that the talk is on the paradox that emerges from the world of nature?

It is known that the Following One exists in the past, not the previous one — its existence therefore comes after. This is a clear fallacy, and it was only invoked to lead him to believe what is said about the naturalists. As for what holds in the world of creativity and the first emanations, the matter of the principles (5) is not from the household of nature: it comes from the intellect and the soul, which is like the second emanation. Except for what he mentioned, his saying is correct from this point of view.

## Chapter Nine

**Casting**

The author of al-Nusra said:

The intellect is the first medium, the soul is the second medium, and the first medium is closer to the cause than the second medium. Proximity and the rank of one thing to another can only arise from perfection and honor, and distance can only arise from deficiency and absence of honor.

We say: (2)

This statement is not true with respect to the creative and emanated intellects. First, because a medium requires two parties, and it is not permissible to say that the First Intellect is a medium unless its existence is in such a state that it has a party above it and a party below it. Since it is in a similar state of existence as both, how could the First of the First Ones (3) be thought of as a medium when there is nothing ahead of it in terms of its existence? So how could it be intermediate between two when it is not?

It is impossible for the First Intellect to be imagined as a medium, because its existence is the First Existence, and what is a First Existence is not called a medium. Even a medium deserves to be called a medium only by being preceded by something of the kind of its own existence, so that an occasion of likeness exists between them — and by being succeeded by something also of its kind that suits it in some respects, that suits the first from one side and the second from another. Such a thing has two sides and is intermediate between them. As for the First Intellect, there is no occasion of likeness between it and its Creator, glorified is His mention (4), because it is not of the kind of His existence. So it is not

> 1 — In copy (B) it reads "medium."
> 2 — Dropped in copy (A).
> 3 — In copy (A) the reading is "atheism."
> 4 — Most of his mention has been omitted in copy (A).

— 62 —

<!-- page 65 -->

a medium between Him and what is below it. And when there is no occasion of likeness between it and its Creator — as for what lies between it and its Creator that brings it into existence, that is something else — it is also not a medium between Him and what is below it. The mediator between it and what is below it is the very eye of creativity, and the cause that is said to be creative, established on what we have shown in *The Illuminated Epistle* and in our well-known book *Rahat al-'Aql*.

If that is the case, then there is no sense in the inference of the author of al-Nusra in what he said — he himself having earlier conceded that there is enough in deficiency and honor. Deficiency is necessary in addition to the First Intellect, not in itself: it is a stature that lacks nothing and has perfection, and it is in addition to its predecessor that it suffers the lack of rank — like the Natiq and the Asas and the sun and the moon. Each of these, in the case of the Asas and the moon, is perfect in itself with no deficiency in it (1); and if added to the other that is its form — the Natiq and the sun — it is deficient in rank only relative to it.

So the author of al-Nusra is obliged to admit the following: deficiency arises by addition to the previous one. And the author of al-Islah is obliged to admit that completeness arises in oneself because it is emanating from the perfection that is its cause. Saying this in his presence is correct, and saying that is also true on his face — but the author of al-Nusra wants the author of al-Islah's words from where the author of al-Islah did not mean and not from his purpose.

## Chapter Ten

The author of al-Nusra said:

If he says, "I do not say that the author of al-Islah means that the intellect is like the soul in the chapter on perfection," we will say to him: If it is better than it or less than it, then he does not say "thanks to him" — he should say that it is lacking.

We say:

The author of al-Islah may not say what the author of al-Nusra took as his counter-payment. He said: All things are perfect in themselves, and nothing is worth

> 1 — In copy (B) the reading is "in it."

— 6X —


There is no deficiency in a thing except in relation to what is higher than it in rank. What does not possess this additional element above it cannot be deemed deficient; and if such a thing is compared against what is higher, the comparison itself amounts to unjust prejudice against it.

## Chapter Eleven

The author of al-Nusrah said:

One of the clearest signs of the soul's deficiency and the intellect's perfection is the words of Allah:

> "And of His signs are the night, the day, the sun, and the moon."

He pairs night and day as two foundations, and the sun and the moon as two foundations — with the sun corresponding to the first foundation and the moon to the second. There is no dispute among the people of knowledge and wisdom that the moon's light is less than the sun's — diminished in radiance — and that the moon derives its light from the sun while the sun grants its light to the moon. This is what he said.

We say:

It is clear that the moon has less light than the sun and that it receives its light from the sun. But this diminution stands only by comparison to the sun — it does not imply a deficiency in the moon's essence itself. For if the moon were incomplete in itself, it would not have been able to receive light from the sun. Precisely because its essence is complete, it can receive from another. The words of the author of al-Islah concern the integrity of the soul in its essence, while the words of the author of al-Nusrah concern its deficiency in its accidents and the signs of perfection that accompany it — there is a clear distinction between the two statements. The author of al-Islah has been treated as one wronged: he has been opposed in matters that are not part of his purpose nor part of what he intends to say.

## Chapter XII

The author of al-Nusrah said:

A further indication of the soul's deficiency is that the wise have likened the intellect to the soul of the soul (1), and have made the soul stand to the intellect as the body stands to the soul. They did this only because they recognized the soul's deficiency and the intellect's perfection — they likened the soul to the imperfect thing and the intellect to the perfect thing.

We say:

His statement here confirms what we have argued: the deficiency lies not in the soul itself, but in its relation to other things. Even if the meaning of his words is restricted to the universe of the second emergence, the benefit applies only to those souls present in the world of nature — souls needing to reach their second (2) perfection. The soul in itself (3) is wholly complete, but it is incomplete in one respect: it is one of the senses through which (4) the soul's stream runs from the body. The second perfection — which belongs to the first emergence — does not apply to these souls, because their power and completeness were given to them at the beginning of their existence, not through time and accumulated benefit, but through a diminution from what precedes them in rank, in priority, and in posteriority.

## Chapter XIII

The author of al-Nusrah said:

If there is no deficiency in the soul except in the fact that nature borders its horizon, then the soul has two extremities — one toward the intellect and one toward nature — whereas the intellect is not the same: (its two extremities are toward the Word on one side and toward the soul on the other) (5). Nothing of dark nature has ever clung to the intellect, because of its perfection and completion, while nature has clung to a part of the soul because nature falls on its horizon — as anyone who reflects upon it can see.

> (1) Dropped in copy (A).
> (2) In copy (B): "kamma."
> (3) In copy (B): "itself."
> (4) Dropped in copy (A).
> (5) The whole sentence is omitted in copy (B).

The reflective observer (1) thus arrives at the soul's deficiency and the intellect's perfection. This is what he said.

We say:

This statement is disordered. The fact that nature occupies the horizon of what he calls "the soul" earns the soul no deficiency; on the contrary, it indicates the soul's completeness in relation to nature. Nature's movement is incomplete relative to the soul, while the soul is, relative to nature, complete and noble — by virtue of the soul's competence in the very capacities that nature lacks. I mean that the soul and nature themselves are the two perfections insofar as they emanate from a higher perfection. He said: "One [extremity] is toward the Word, the other toward the soul." But the intellect is nothing other than the Word, nor is the Word anything other than the intellect. Rather, the self is one and the essence is one (2); the names that scatter answer to their meanings. We have already discussed the use of the term "intermediate" in this sense; that mention is not concealed.

He further said: "Why is it that nothing of dark nature clings to the intellect because of its perfection and completion, while nature has clung to a part of the soul because nature falls on its horizon?" But it is not the soul that clings to nature; rather it is nature that loves and clings to the soul — moving toward the soul for communion with it. To believe and perceive that the soul has clung to a part of nature is mistaken. The best way to dispel the apparent meaning of his statement is to read it charitably as saying that the soul has overflowed onto nature, so that this overflow might be called "clinging."

## Chapter Fourteen

The author of al-Islah said:

What is incomplete is the action of the soul, not the soul itself; the soul completes its action, not its own essence.

The author of al-Nusrah said:

> (1) In copy (B): "the consideration."
> (2) In copy (B): cited with addenda.

This statement is contradictory, because if the action is incomplete it must be so from one of three sources: either from the deficiency of the agent itself, or from the deficiency of the matter from which the action proceeds, or from the deficiency of both. If the deficiency of the action stems from the agent itself — and he holds that the soul is the agent — then the soul is deficient. If instead it is from the deficiency of matter, then he has affirmed matter as an eternal substance, and that is a departure from the doctrine of religion, leaning toward the school of (Abu Zakariyya al-Razi) (1) and others who held matter eternal. I imagined that the author of this book (2) inclined toward this position, because he posits time as eternal alongside the intellect — that is the kernel of his doctrine. Even if the action's deficiency belongs to both sides at once, he has himself said that the soul is the agent; hence he has affirmed the soul's eternity. Whenever he affirms that, he has said it. This is what he said.

We say:

The statement of the author of al-Islah, on the terms of his stated purpose regarding deficiency and perfection, is admissible. The statement of the author of al-Nusrah in his reply is fallacy, prejudice, and a departure from the limits of knowledge. First, after dividing matter into the three divisions named above, he claims that if the action's deficiency comes from the agent itself, and the soul is the agent, the soul is deficient. But the author of al-Islah has clarified that this deficiency is not necessarily attached to the soul itself: the deficiency is in the action in the sense of its effect — and so this objection falls away. Second, he claims that if the deficiency comes from matter itself, this proves matter eternal — since the two perfections, the first and the second, are eternal once their primal form is proven eternal; how then could matter be deficient? But this misses the point: the diminution is only (3) in the substrates that receive perfection, not in the perfection that is received — as is well established in natural philosophy. The diminution arises on the side of the receivers, not on the side of what is received from them — for instance, heat: the sun's heat is accepted by objects that yield to it like wax, which softens; while other bodies (1) refuse to accept it, such as stone and iron; and still others react only slowly. We have established by direct observation that the heat is one and the same; the discrepancy lies in the receivers, not in the heat itself. Third, he claimed that the author of al-Islah leans toward the school of Abu Zakariyya al-Razi along the lines he described. This is a departure from the limits of knowledge and a transgression (2); I will not dignify it — neither in the structure of the division nor in what follows from his two claims regarding diminution and the eternity of al-hayyuli.

## Chapter XV

The author of al-Nusrah said:

As for his argument that the soul is complete because it emanates from the First Intellect, which is itself perfect — and that what emanates from perfection can only be perfect — this argument does not hold. The soul does not have to be exalted simply because it emanates from the perfect intellect. For if it did, then by the same necessity what emanates from the soul itself would also be complete — namely the form and the form of the form — and the same would hold of every subsequent emanation until one arrives at the indivisible singular individuals, all complete. The consequence would be that the whole world is complete, with no deficiency in it or in any of its parts.

We say:

The conclusion of his argument is that if the soul were complete by virtue of emanating from the First Intellect — the perfect whole — then the form and the form of the form emanating from the soul would also be complete, and the same would hold for everything emanating from all the forms and images he claims to be individuals: everything in the world would be complete, with no deficiency at all. But we have already shown above in what sense the author of al-Islah holds the soul to be perfect. The statement of the author of al-Nusrah in this chapter (3) is no real investigation, because the soul does not emanate from the soul or from the individuals as he claimed. The soul is itself indivisible; the individuals are fragmented into their parts, and the hyulic substrates (al-hayyuliyyat) are emanated together with the soul that precedes them in existence, not from the soul itself. For the soul that is second in rank does not possess primary existence; there is nothing that exists with it that is not above it in rank (1) except the first, which is the First Intellect. The soul's existence is a second-order existence, and what possesses second-order existence cannot exist unless there is something whose existence it shares in kind and quality. What I have established — that this second-rank soul is a thing whose primary existence is not preceded by its own kind, and is one in its existence — is true only by the grace of Allah, exalted is He, who created it out of relation, addition, and multiplicity. Everything described as a thing through its subdivisions exhibits this kind of relation. The attributes in the active causes themselves act as causes, because more than one thing is asserted of them; the cause is one, and yet many features attach to it according to what its causes and effects bring into relation with it.

When Allah is exalted far above having any other before Him or beside Him, or (2) being differentiated from any in attributes or relations — and He is the Almighty, the Creator of the universe, the Cause who has no cause — it is impossible, given His soundness, His holiness, and His exaltation above multiplicity, that anything should come into existence directly from Him except a single one. This one — which is the First Intellect — when it stood in two proportions, one being that it is the cause of what is below it, and the other being that it is itself an effect (since it is created and originated) — produced what came to exist as a duality in keeping with those same two proportions. From the first proportion came the Second Intellect; from the other proportion came the orbits of the heavens and the rest of the creatures of the corporeal world. Thus from the One, nothing comes into being in the universe except two — for nothing can come after the rank of the One in existence except two, nor after the singular except the pair, which is multiplicity. If the first thing in the order of foundations is one, then what follows it in the intelligible order is two — the pair, or the multitude. So we have two: one of which is what we call (1) the soul, and the other is what we call (2) the body that contains the heavens, the planets, and what is below them.

## Chapter Sixteen of Chapter One

Then, when (3) the basis of religion and its branches was set upon the scales of the foundations and their correspondences — so as to serve as an indication of their order, mapped onto the foundations of the unseen — the existence of the Book and the *shari'a* came forth on the side of the Speaker-Prophet (al-Natiq), and the establishment of the Foundation (al-Asas) came as inspiration from his side. This was proof that all that exists proceeds from the First Intellect — who is in his world like the Speaker-Prophet in the world of the body — yet it is rather the soul that is next, together with al-Hayyuli, that bears witness to this, just as the Book and the Foundation, which exist on the side of the Speaker-Prophet, bear witness to it. And this is borne out by what the Prophet did in joining the Book and the 'Itrah on his own behalf, when he said:

> "I leave among you the two weighty things: the Book of Allah and my 'Itrah, a rope stretched from the sky to the earth — one end of it in the hand of Allah and one end in your hands. Hold fast to them and you will not go astray (4) as long as you hold to them. I have asked my Lord to grant me to meet them at the Pond."

He brought his two index fingers together along with the rest of his hand and said: "Not like these two" — gesturing with his index fingers and the middle finger of his hand, where one precedes the other. "Their helper is my helper, and their forsaker is my forsaker." And he likened them in existence to his fingers.

> (1) In copy (B): "the so-called."
> (2) In copy (B): "the so-called."
> (3) Dropped in copy (A).
> (4) In copy (1): "you do not hold on."


<!-- page 73 -->
The two index-fingers — for (1) one of them does not precede the other — and so he has acted within the realm of bodies.

The first course of the Intellect is in dar al-Ibda' (the realm of origination); he has set forth what he stated from the book, together with those associated with him from his 'Itra (the Family) — who is the Foundation — and the other Imams, the prayers of Allah be upon them.

(2) The manner in which what proceeds from the First Intellect comes to the Second, and the Intellect's resting upon what stands within it, is something we have already mentioned in Kitab Rahat al-'Aql. Thus al-hayula does not display its nobility, nor the good form which its power yields (3), except through the power of the Soul, which is the follower — and the Light shines through them. Just as the Book and the Shari'a do not declare themselves, and the wonders of their governance do not appear within and beneath them except through the ta'wil of the Foundation and the one who stands in his place — so its existence is from the First Intellect together with the Second.

Chapter Seventeen of Part One

Then we say: We (4) have said that the First Intellect does not have alongside it anything of the same kind as its existence — such as a duplicate of itself. For its existence is not extended to anything else except the existence of the First; and the First is not multiple, lest the paths of monotheism be disrupted. If it had a likeness in its existence, that likeness would also have to share with it. And if their existence were simultaneous, then there would be no account for their existence; (5) and this is necessitated by what precedes them. The meaning of their existence is that which is independent in its existence.

The existence of duality is a multiplicity of essences, and the multiplicity of essences does not subsist by itself but by the multitude of souls. Rather, its existence is from that which precedes it, which is one — one in particular from one side, and full of meanings from the other. The one in particular, when multiplied in meanings, ...

(1) Dropped in copy (b).
(2) In copy (b) came "Salam".
(3) In copy (b): "the forms are presented."
(4) Fell out in copy (a).
(5) In copy (a) came: "and required us."

<!-- page 74 -->
... has its existence not in itself but in others; while that which is not multiplied is multiplied neither in meanings nor in itself.

Thus it becomes clear: if the First Intellect had alongside it something of the kind of its existence — such as two accidents borne by one preceding thing — then the existence of that one [common thing] would be such that it is folded back into meanings, not in itself, but in what precedes it and makes it one. And since there is nothing above the Intellect, it is not possible for it to have a likeness of its existence. It is therefore established that it is one.

As for the proof that the Intellect is one: there are two things present within it — namely the Soul. If the existence of the Soul and the hayulanic things together rests upon what we have shown, then it is manifest that to claim the Soul appears [as independent] is wrong.

Chapter Eighteen of Part One

This is what the author of al-Nusra is forced to maintain by his statement concerning the deficiency of the originated things and of Form, and the deficiency of all that is in the world.

We say: Form and the formed are perfect, and the world is perfect. Its parts are themselves perfect, as is evidenced by the fact that the perfect is that outside of which there is nothing of its kind of existence. The world is the totality of bodies, and Form is the composition of the parts of body. Body in itself is perfect in its three dimensions; nothing is needed to bound it unless that bound lies outside of it. If it is bounded, it is complete; and if it is complete, then the author of al-Nusra is forced [to concede] that the world is complete and that imperfection in it is impossible.

And if his position is drawn from his own words, then his pronouncements are self-contradictory. It would have sufficed him to say, in his book known as al-Ikhtisar, in the Chapter on the Command, what already indicates the world's perfection — for there he said: "And the form of the world's governance in its entirety is nothing else, and the wisdom suffices with what is in the world's totality, being rich and perfect." I do not know how this escaped him. Perhaps he classified Kitab al-Ikhtisar after Kitab al-Nusra.

As for the disparity that exists among the parts of the world — by which the author of al-Nusra is forced to allege deficiency — this is rather by the addition of some of them ...
- 72

<!-- page 75 -->
... to others, with respect to their forms and conditions, which are the first perfection of what exists in some parts of the world. They have been given their first perfection, and then a second first; and to some of them has been given their second perfection, which is the final of the acquisitions. The discourse then arrives at the deficiency of which the author of al-Nusra speaks — except that this deficiency lies in the later states of the natural essences which appear when they are added to one another. And the perfect of which the author of al-Islah speaks is (1) the essence whose existence is foremost.

Chapter Nineteen of Part One

The author of al-Nusra said: And if it is necessary that the Soul emanate from the Intellect, which is the perfect — the Soul being [its] equivalent in rank, since it is also necessary that the Soul emanate from the Intellect, which is the first intermediary and the first creator — then [the Soul] must be an intermediary like the First, and creative. So the Soul would attain, of the virtues, what the Intellect attains; and so would the whole world. This is what people say.

We say: It has already been advanced that it is impossible to say the Intellect is the first intermediary. In sum, what he has mentioned is a corrupt issue. For if a thing is the cause (2) of another thing — in the sense that one of them is cause and the other effect — it does not follow that [the effect] must share with [the cause] in all of its conditions, such that its essence would be its essence and its rights, and that nothing would be said of [the effect], when [the cause] deserves to be described, except what is said of that particular essence. And when I found further at the root, and when it was then said that [the effect] is found in him as he is, [it is clear that] he must not share with him in all his conditions; he must share with him only in some of his conditions. For if he does not share in some ...

(2) Fell out in copy (B).
- 73

<!-- page 76 -->
... of his conditions, then — since he is the cause of his existence, and gave him existence, and if such is the way of the existence of things in multiplication — he must share with him in some respect. And if he must share, then the Following must be a Soul of all things, emanating from the First Intellect, the cause of its existence, so as to be like it in one respect and opposed to it in another. And if [the Following] must be a likeness of [the First] in a respect contrary to its own face, then it has been shown that it is not necessary — given the existence of what is called the Following Soul as drawn from the Intellect (1) — that it be like the Intellect in all its states; nor is it necessary to say of the Following what is said of the Intellect in respect to that reason. And this rejects the statement of the author of al-Nusra: that if [emanation] were obligatory, [likeness in all respects] would be obligatory.

Chapter Twenty of Part One

The author of al-Nusra said: This has many analogues in observable instances — such as Thought and the partial soul. The partial soul that is in us is like the Intellect, and Thought is like the Soul; except that, even though Thought issues from the soul, it may still be said to be like the soul, because Thought grasps what it perceives part by part and time after time, while the soul-as-totality (2) grasps what it perceives without time. This is what he said.

We say: The soul — I mean the soul of human beings — has actions, each one of which has a name proper to it. If it requires the realization of something, it is called Thought. If it observes what has formed within it of the forms of information by returning to you what it has stored, it is called Memorization. If it engraves within itself the marks it gathers, whether from the side of inference or from the side ...

(4) Fell out in copy (B).
- 74

<!-- page 77 -->
... of the senses, that pattern is called Perception. And if it knows that there are things outside of it which it desires to conceive, that is called (1) the will to incline. And if these are its actions, then Thought is one act of it which does not exceed (2) its own self, such that Thought would have an existence other than its own existence. So how is the analogy of Thought correct (3) as analogous to what is called the Soul, which has an existence other than the existence of the Intellect? It is said that [Thought] perceives part by part — but perception is nothing other than the Soul itself acting, which act is called Thought in itself. Or how is it correct that the Soul — I mean the soul of human beings — perceives as a totality without time, when it has no way to gather knowledge except from the side of the senses, and from inference proceeding from the side of their parts to their totality in time?

Furthermore: if it is not permissible to say that Thought is like the soul, then it is [equally] obligatory that the Second Intellect, which is called the Universal Soul, is not like the First Intellect. For if Thought from the souls of human beings does not [serve as the analogue], [then] the Second Intellect — the second existence of origination, self-subsisting, though existing at less than the First's rank — does not (4) have an existence of its own apart from the soul; rather, it is the very same as the soul, with no existence of its own. Every act of the soul has a cause for which it requires action — namely that it is in the world of nature; and if it is separated from this world, those actions are voided by the voiding of their cause. What remains is only the percipient essence as pure intellect. He then set forth the Second Intellect, called the Soul, as proceeding from the First Intellect, and Thought as proceeding from the Soul — yet the existence of Thought was no existence (5) [other than] the existence of the Soul which he likened to the Intellect, nor did Thought have a perception of its own [apart from] the Soul's. It is as if he denied that the Second Intellect, called the Soul, has an existence ...

- 75

... other than the existence of the First Intellect, nor a perception other than the First's perception, and that it is impossible for the Following to have an existence other than the existence of the First, and yet to have a perception of the First as one. Nor is it correct to be said that the Following is creative just as the First — for the First does not deserve to be said to be emanated. Both are pure intellects, each of which has encompassed itself and preserved itself in itself; they resemble each other in one respect and do not resemble each other in another.

If this is so, then there is no point in likening Thought to what he likened it to; nor is it sensible to make the human soul [analogous to] the First Intellect; nor to give Thought the rank of the Universal Soul. The nobility of the human soul, and its passage from potency to act, comes only through acquisition; and acquisition is only from the senses as work, and from the side of perception as knowledge. If the nobility of souls lies in the acquisition that is conceived through thought and reasoning, and if Thought from among the souls is honored only to that degree, then it is as if he made the cause of the First Intellect's nobility to be the Second Intellect, called the Soul. And this is an inverted matter — to derive the nobility of the Following and rank it by what is ahead of it, namely its existence which is prior — because the nobility of the prior is itself arranged by what is behind it. The ruling on what has another existence in completion and perfection, if it is contrary to what exists in the First, does not run in the course of the First in that respect. This is not the way to proceed, even if they share on one face. And the author of al-Nusra, by what he has ruled to be the first existence, makes it the case that [the First] has something whose existence is another existence; and that is impossible.

Chapter Twenty-One of Part One

The author of al-Nusra said: One of the clearest signs of the Soul's deficiency and the First Intellect's perfection is ...
- 76

<!-- page 79 -->
... that the First [author], and the author of al-Islah, all said that action belongs to the Second, and they did not attribute anything to the First. For action is always a sign of deficiency and need, while the abandoning of action is evidence of perfection and self-sufficiency. That is because action is only for the sake of drawing a benefit or warding off a harm.

Abu Zakariyya said: The Initiator is already the Soul by way of governance (1) and tampering, and the people of religion do not call it true; so there remains nothing but the drawing of benefit or the warding off of harm. When this is found, there is a deficiency. The Soul, in its action, seeks to draw the benefit, which is its reaching the purpose for which it is destined — so it initiates the action. But the Intellect, when there was no rank above it (2), and it was not made [further] perfect, did not move to action in any way. And the Soul, when it found a level above itself, remained moving and remained in its quest. By this method, we say: as to the deficiency of the Soul and the soundness of the Intellect, this statement is clear, praise be to Allah.

The upshot of his statement in this chapter is that the Second is the doer, and that action exists only to draw a benefit or to ward off a harm — and that the drawing of benefit (3) and the warding off of harm exist only for imperfection — and so the Intellect is not deficient by refraining from action. The reason for its stillness is that there is no rank above it for it to seek; and the Soul has no rank above itself to which to move and acquire.

We say: the words of the author of al-Islah, were they about the soul that is the soul of human beings, would make the saying of the author of al-Nusra the better said. But since his words were about what is called the Soul [the Universal Soul], the saying of the author of al-Nusra fails. His inference from the state of human souls — that they perform action only to draw a benefit or to ward off a harm, or by way of tampering — is the falsehood of his statement, and his ruling concerning the universal Following, perfect in its totality, is a clear injustice. As it may be said in a sentence what is said (4) ...

(1) In copy A: "request" came.
(2) Fell out in copy (B).
(3) This sentence is completely omitted in copy A.
(4) In copy B, "what was" is mentioned.
- 77


<!-- page 80 -->
As for its being first in existence in terms of completion and perfection, one cannot then say that what is not being is another existence, nor can one say that its existence was an existence firstly. Therefore, the statement of the author of al-Nusra concerning action and its divisions is not correct, for not every action is for drawing a benefit, paying off a harm, or for futility — an action may proceed from an agent in such a way that what benefits is the object of the action, not the one who produced it. The evidence for this is that the heat from the fire is an act of the fire, yet this act does not return to the fire, nor does the fire gain any benefit or suffer any harm from it; rather the benefit and the harm reach whatever the heat acts upon. Likewise the action of the sun in illuminating (1) the world: human beings are able to act by their will, and by the sun's rising and the existence of the universe, plants, animals, and minerals derive benefits — benefits that accrue to others and not to the sun. The sun does not benefit from this action, nor is it harmed by it. So too the whales that live in the sea of Oman, and the creatures called "dolphins" which, if an ancient sailor is drowning in the sea, help him to escape it and push him toward safety: that action returns no benefit and no harm to them, rather the benefit reaches the one acted upon. And if this is the case in the natural world — that such strange actions issue even from creatures deficient in their own state and dependent on others in their actions — then it cannot be denied that, from the pure abstract minds, which are granted their perfections in the first stage of their existence, there proceed actions greater than those that emerge from the natural realm. These actions, in accord with the proximity of the ranks of these minds and the dignity of their grades, are noble and not for the sake of (2) drawing a benefit to themselves nor warding off a harm from themselves, but rather for others. These actions are therefore acts of wisdom, not acts of drawing benefit, paying off harm, or futility. No: the perfect and well-articulated act, which proceeds only from the perfect, issues from such a source. And accordingly, the one whom the author of al-Nusra called "the soul" is not, as he claimed, moving — for it possesses what is necessary in the natural order.

5 – In Version B (*Dia'ah*).

1 - It fell out of Copy (B).

- 78

<!-- page 81 -->
This movement belongs only to the natural bodies in their own natural forms, and the soul is of a higher rank than nature, and below what we have explained in *The Book of the Repose of the Intellect*. It has become clear from what we have mentioned that the protest and objection raised by the author of al-Nusra against the speaker is not correct, and that the position of the author of al-Islah — that the consequent is more complete than the precedent — stands, in accord with what we have already explained. It is therefore not permissible to say that what is in the realm of creation, from the first and second intellects, the higher letters, is subject to motion and stillness: we have shown that motion and stillness belong only to what is a body, or to what is in a body and contained within it.

**Chapter Twenty-Two of Part One**

The author of al-Nusra said:

The result of the saying of the author of al-Islah is that the action of the soul issues from something other than the soul itself, even though the soul is complete in him; and so the action of the soul becomes (1) a soul. This is forbidden by manifest evidence, because actions, in everything we have ever witnessed in the world of sense (2), do not themselves become subjects of action. For example: the writer and what is written, the builder and what is built — writing does not become a writer, nor does what is built become a builder. Rather, the man who is a writer in potency, and a builder in potency, becomes a writer and a builder in actuality. So we say, in the case of the soul and the intellect: the intellect is already perfect, the soul is imperfect, possessing a measure of potency. If the soul were already to exist by drawing on perfection, but only became perfect by acting, this would be impossible. We say:

The intent of the author of al-Islah, when he says that the action of the soul is acted upon and not the soul itself, is this: that the second perfection is attained from the soul's side, from which the stream of what overflows pours upon it.

So the soul passes from potency into actuality, and if the intent of his saying is that the action of the soul

2 - In Copy (A) what is mentioned is *yudrak* (to be grasped).

3 - In Copy (B) what is mentioned is *al-shahid* (the witness).

- 79

<!-- page 82 -->
is what passes from its overflow (1) from potency into action, then the second perfection is obtained, and nothing passes the limit of potency into action except by virtue of something that already exists. So there is no denial here: if what was in potency passes into actuality and attains the second perfection by acquisition, then it stands as something existent in what was already granted of perfection in the first existence. The two thus share in the sense that they already exist, even if each of them has received something — they participate in it, though not from the same side from which the other received it. Nor is it impossible for them to be two parallels in respect of their commonality, since it is known that every collective and every other thing that is a meaning among meanings is, in that sense, like another, by the witness of our saying that everything which passes from the limit of potency to the limit of action already exists, and everything that already exists has had the second perfection occur to it, and everyone who passes from the limit of potency to action has the second perfection occur to them. And if the realization of what already exists is the second perfection, and the existence of what was established in potency, so that it became already established by the second perfection in it, then they are common and similar in that they attained the second perfection, even if (2) from one side they differ from another. And if what we have mentioned is neither impossible nor wrong, but obligatory, then the statement of the author of al-Nusra is nothing but mere prejudice (3).

**Chapter Twenty-Three of Part One**

Then (4) the author of al-Nusra said:

This is a manifest objection, because actions have never been seen, in the world of sense, to become themselves the subjects of action, as he has indicated — by the matter of the writer (5) and of construction. The evidence that testifies

to the abstention from what has been said — namely, that actions are themselves the subjects of action — is this: the writing that he mentioned, which is the act of the writer, is the very thing that addresses its reader and speaks to him. If the writing in its forms and arrangements were not effective upon its reader, it would not be as though it were speaking about its subject, translating concerning it, by virtue of the forms engraved in it. When its reader knows what is in the soul of the writer, and the image that the builder has created is the one that captures the viewer for its image by its squares and its layout, in order that he may conceive of it — yet if the form of the building does not already exist (1) until it is acquired — then the very same scenes are obtained in which their pictures depict what they portray. So too the striker and the one struck: the act of striking is what produces the hurt, and the hurt afflicts him, as we have mentioned in our two well-known epistles, *al-Rawda* and *al-Idah*. And the saying of Allah Almighty is evidence that actions are themselves verbs, as most who say it acknowledge: "There is nothing that does not glorify Him in praise" — the glorification is the act of the Almighty, and although it is a verb, it is what glorifies and praises Him. And He says (interpretation of the meaning): "And We sent the winds" — the wind is one thing, and what stirs it is another, and the explanation of this is clear. It thus becomes clear that the protest of the author of al-Nusra against the author of al-Islah in this chapter is untenable. The purpose of the author of al-Nusra in saying that we have not seen actions in the world of sense becoming themselves verbs at all, is that the very same actions did not insist upon being the very subject in the world of sense — the writing did not insist on being its own writer, nor did what is built become the very builder. That is a correct statement, as he said, because the action does not become identical with the one from which it issued; rather, it is an actor like the actor in respect of the existence of the action with regard to it. Even if the ranks are different, and the reasons are different, the author of al-Nusra wished to defend those whom he represents by prejudice against the author of al-Islah — and may Allah sanctify their souls — and there are many confusions (2) that need to be unraveled, layer upon layer.

1 – In Copy (B) the reading is *ka-anna* ("as if": "indeed").

2 - In Copy (B) the reading is *arada* (he wanted).

*The Book of Riyad* [6]

- 81

<!-- page 84 -->
**Chapter Twenty-Four of Part One**

The author of al-Nusra said:

He likened the intellect and the soul, in their perfection and imperfection, to the man and to the sperm, because the sperm is the image of the man in potency, on the truth, and it becomes a complete man like the man. Similarly, the soul is the image of the intellect, on the truth, and it becomes perfect like the intellect, if it benefits from the intellect and its benefits accrue to it. This is what he said.

We say:

His statement that the sperm is the image of the man in potency (1), on the truth, and likewise that the soul is the image of the intellect on the truth becoming complete — this is not correct. The sperm is not the image of the man, nor is the soul that which by potency is the image of the intellect. The sperm is matter prepared for being given form (2), free of images, so that images may be acquired in it in stages. Likewise the soul is a natural preparation, devoid of images, which would acquire the images of knowledge, so that the soul becomes self-fulfilling through that. And even if the soul were an image of the intellect, on the truth, then the intellect would have to possess an image of what it itself is, so that they would be one thing; and if the picture has an image, it is something else — and how can it be true? Whatever its existence, it is a creative existence, and otherness in it does not exist except by addition, while the essence is one. No, this kind of talk and analogy applies only to what is in the natural realm, except for what (3) was outside the separation of the bodies.

**Chapter Twenty-Five of Part One**

The author of al-Nusra said:

Also, the status of the speaker in the corporeal world (4) is like the status of the intellect in the world

of the spiritual; and the status of the foundation is like the status of the consequent. So as it may be said that the soul is as perfect as the intellect, it may be said that the foundation is as perfect as the speaker. And this is not correct, because the rank of the foundation is below the rank of the speaker, and this has been explained in *The Meaning of Prayer*. This is what he said.

We say:

The second is like the first, even if it is in a lower rank, and the foundation is like the speaker, even if he is in a lower rank, and that second (1) in its existence has nothing left for it in the way of any degree which it will attain later and which would gain it completion; just as the foundation is perfect in its rank, and no degree remains for it to rise to and acquire. It is not, in itself, incomplete. And it suffices, by way of witness, to point to the Prophet's act and his indication of what we have said — namely, that it is obligatory to perform the slaughter on camels, and to perform the sacrifice (2) on others — because slaughter is the delivery of the iron blade to the head of the *fawad* (3), from the wound, so that no blood is spilled which would be distorted upon the degree of the report, the gazing upon the secrets, and the departure of doubts and suspicions (4) in the ranks, while the sacrifice is based on taking the covenant and the release in the call, and the slaughter is based on the next. It is as if the Prophet, peace and blessings of Allah be upon him, had displayed this deed and had informed the Umma that follows him, on the one hand; and the wisdom of his role is that the foundation in his place in supplication is that he did not establish it in the inner call which is the continuation of the worship of God leading to the unity of God and the knowledge of His limits, unless I instruct him in what is in it of perfection, and inform him of what is in its perfection. So the highest degree is in his teaching, and his path is in attaining perfection and completeness — not like any other in his Umma from whom he obtains (5) communication and completeness
