finally, after taking the covenant first and the teaching second. Peace did not do that fundamentally, except to indicate that the second, which is the foundation in the world, the intellect, has attained perfection first; and there is no leverage in taking the covenant first upon the sons of the call, then informing them of the degree of perfection. The communication is only for the purpose of pointing out and indicating that the natural souls, which are below the second, are first in potency, then they pass out by acquisition into the last that is already, which is their perfection and their fulfillment — to believe in each of these things what is worth believing in. And then it is not confined to indicating that it is indicative of completeness on the other side: as in the two rak'ahs of the dawn prayer, which are upon the first and the second. In the first rak'ah, which is the first rak'ah, it is obligatory to recite the praise. In the second rak'ah, it is obligatory to recite *al-Qunut* in place of the praise that is in the first rak'ah, so as to be in the same state as the first from the standpoint of perfection. Their distinction can only be made in terms of rank in existence, and this is the first of existence like the first rak'ah, and this is the second rak'ah like the second of existence.

**Chapter Twenty-Six of Part One**

Then we say in response to what he said:

If (2) it is permissible to say that the soul is as perfect as the intellect, then it is permissible to say that the foundation is like the speaker. And this is not right — and how could it not be? The foundation is the one who stands in the place of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and his family. Even if he falls short of standing in his place, or is not as perfect as he is, when Allah obliges obedience to him — how does Allah Almighty render obedience binding to one who is, like others, deficient in either of these two matters? It is not permissible, in wisdom, that the Prophet should be a messenger to all who are in his time and to all who are born after him,

1 - In Copy (B) the reading is *yatba'u* (he follows).

2 - It fell out of Copy (A).

- 84


…and an excuse for the rest of his creation, to lead the community as he leads his descendants. There is no ancestor like him, so the proof stands against them: the Foundation is in the same position as the Speaker who comes after him on one side, and the Speaker is on the other side. It is enough proof that the disparity between them in rank is not in their essences — for the rank of the Foundation is lower than the rank of the Speaker only because he cannot claim that rank himself. From what we have said, it is clear that the position of the author of *al-Islah* is the one that should be accepted against the opposition of the author of *al-Nusra*, who said that the second is incomplete; even on the view of the author of *al-Nusra*, what should be said about it is what has been stated.

## Chapter Twenty-Seven of Part One

The author of *al-Nusra* said, in his argument:

That bowing is to be on the Foundation, prostrating on the Speaker, and that the supplication in bowing — "Glory be to my great Lord and praise Him" — is the praise of the righteous one from the side of his Originator, the greatest, which is to follow (which is the Prophet and the Great), while his Educator is the greatest one followed. And in prostration, "Glory be to my Lord, the Most High, and praise Him," is the praise of the Speaker who is the Prophet from the side of his Originator, who is the second, and the highest is upon the prior side, since his Originator the highest is the prior. The Speaker, peace be upon him, mentioned this in the revelation. The meaning is that when he says it, then he says, it means that the Speaker is here standing in the rank of the Intellect, so the Foundation depends from him, and it is at the corner — they are the two originals — or that the lower means the preceding one. But the Prophet, peace and blessings of God be upon him and his family, reached the highest degree of the prophetic rank — to a level where there was no veil between him and the prior. The prior is undifferentiated from the Word, and the Foundation has not reached this level and station.

⸻

Similarly, the next did not reach the rank of the prior, so he remained below it. This is present in every pair: males are never anything but more complete and more honorable, and females are inferior — like the heavens and the earth. The sky stands in the position of the male and the earth in the position of the female; the sky is more honorable than the earth, and the earth is more deficient than the sky. This is what he said.

We say:

The author of *al-Nusra*'s statement that the rank of the prior is higher than that of the second is a true saying, and it is exactly what the author of *al-Islah* said. The fact that the rank of the second is below the rank of the prior is not a deficiency in his very self. As for his saying that the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him and his family, reached the rank of the prior in perfection without the Foundation reaching it — this is hardly true. For the Foundation, even if he did not reach that degree by himself, reached the level of perfection by means of the Speaker, through his instruction and his making him aware of the blessings sent down to him. By accepting it completely, the Foundation himself was illuminated, just as the moon is filled with light on the fourteenth night of the month. This is testified to by the conduct of the Prophet, peace be upon him and his family: offering the sermon on Friday before the prayer, and delaying it on the Day of Eid al-Fitr. Because God made Friday a face — an interpretation upon Himself, as it is one of the seven days — the Prophet's ascent to the pulpit for the first sermon and then the prayer signifies his attaining the degree of perfection and completion, and his rising to the highest ranks of honor from the side of the originals. He was first, then he called others to his Law, accepting its revelation, obeying its Foundation. Making the Day of Fitr's prayer come first, as a day among the days of God Almighty in obedience to Him by which the religion of God is completed, and then offering the sermon afterward on the Foundation — under the Foundation's obedience and the acceptance of His command and His Law — signifies that the Speaker first attained perfection and his elevation to the ranks of honor, and through his mediation the Foundation was acquainted with the degree of perfection. The successor is complete in himself, even though he attains the degree of perfection through the Speaker, so his rank is below the rank of the Speaker. And the existence of the female is not a deficient existence — though one may invoke its support what is supported with it — but rather its existence is a complete existence. If its deficiency were due to what it executes that is specific to the male, then the existence of the male would likewise be deficient with respect to what is specific to the female; and so, removing the male, his existence would still be complete. This is impossible. Therefore the male is complete in himself and the female is complete in herself, and by adding one to the other their rank becomes uneven, so the female ranks below the male. It is sufficient proof that the disparity is in their ranks, not in their selves. And his saying, "the second did not reach the rank of the prior" — he said:

The Foundation has not reached this level — so the statement of the author of *al-Islah* is correct: that the second is complete.

## Chapter Twenty-Eight of Part One

The author of *al-Nusra* said:

It is a sign of the soul's deficiency and the intellect's perfection that many speaking souls are subject to nature, are drawn toward it and toward what it commands them, and incline to its desires and pleasures, forgetting their own knowledge, light, and splendor. Such a soul desires the world of nature, its forms and effects, and remembers nothing of the splendor of its own world and the light proper to its essence — which would be the proper object of its longing — because of its lack of desire in nature for those desires and pleasures, and the absence in their world of any asceticism toward the pleasures, goodness, and splendor proper to it. The intellect, by contrast, does not forget the pleasures of its own world, its agency, its beauty, and its perfection. It does not incline to nature, does not desire it for its own sake, does not yield to its desires, and does not look at it; or if it looks at it, it looks at it from the side of pity, mercy, and contempt for what nature has. The intellect has informed it of itself, telling it that nature is something little if it is attributed to what the soul has in its own world — that if the soul were like the intellect, steadfast in what it has witnessed in its own world, it would not want nature when it is troubled by it. This is what he said. He said that many speaking souls are subject to nature, are drawn to do what it commands them, and incline to it because of their deficiency.

We say:

The submissive thing that is drawn toward selfishness, pleasures, and the following of natural desires, and the abandonment of worship, is the sensual soul of a person — not the speaking soul to which what we have attached should be attached. The sensual soul is only persuaded — and only when persuaded does it bring forth ugliness and vices. If it is affected by the intellect within the bounds of education, the intellect prevents it from committing what it would otherwise commit. These are not the successor-souls that emanate from the First Intellect, on which the author of *al-Islah* speaks. We have already advanced our argument about this — that the successor is distinct from these souls, and these souls are other than the successor — and that the successor is perfect and complete in a state where nothing remains in himself that he has not attained, such that he could be said to be deficient as is the case with these souls. The successor-souls exist in the first perfection, and they have a second perfection which they attain in time and through acquisition.

## Chapter Twenty-Nine of Part One

Secondly, we say: the successor is perfect, and the one who gives him his perfection and completion at the very beginning of his existence — all at once, when he exists — and with him and through his overflow, the deficient catches up so as to accept the degree of perfection. The proof: the beings in the natural world exist first by potentiality and then by act, and there is no passage of what exists by potentiality to act except in the presence of what already exists in act. And if there is no passage of what exists by potentiality to actuality except by the existence of what already exists in act, then external to act there is something that already exists, for the existence of what already exists is a necessary existence. If that necessary existence is undisturbed in its existing, then it is either the First Intellect or another. It is invalid for it to be the First Intellect, refraining from existence-in-act except in the presence of another and needing in the mind what exists with him outside of himself so that he could act upon it. The First Intellect is too self-sufficient to need anything else in his acting, for there is nothing in him other than himself; he is the one whose perfection has its being from the One who exists, the Almighty, who is beyond him. He arose as a single existence which has no parallel in his existence, and there is nothing else present with him for him to do anything to it — for if there had been, that other would have become the worshipped god alongside the Almighty. We have explained this at length in our book known as *Rahat al-'Aql* (Peace of the Mind); what is in this position is that, in his isolation, there is nothing other than himself to act upon, and there is no need for any other in his acting except by acting in himself. He acts in himself, and his acting is what brings out the existent-by-potentiality in him, and by his overflow in others. This invalidates the claim that what brings out the existent-by-potentiality to act is the First Intellect; and if it is not the First Intellect, it has been proven that it is something different from him, and that is the second. This testifies to the validity of our statement: that the Prophet, peace be upon him and his family, did not need someone other than himself for the greatness of his rank and integrity in order to complete his act — which is revelation and Law — and that he, peace be upon him, in that uniqueness of himself and from himself, issued what he obtained according to what he benefited from what reached him as a degree. He found what he had read and prescribed it perfectly, conducting himself in a bodily house upon the descent that is the First Intellect's first stream — within the same creativity — so that his actions and his rank might lead to contemplation of the truth of the belief, in knowing what is hidden from the senses.

⸻

The First is among the principles of creativity and revival; so the path of monotheism is invariant. As for the Foundation, even though he is at the highest degree of perfection, he has not attained that degree at which he could be unique in his action — in the matter of the mission of preaching, interpretation, teaching, and guidance — by himself. He has not dispensed with what he found with him, namely the revelation and the Law. By this I do not mean that the Foundation dispenses with this, that is, with revelation and the Law; nor is this the case of the Speaker, who is not dispensed with from something existing with him in the world of the body, which he uses for his action like the Foundation. Therefore the course of the successor is that the one upon whom the matter rests proceeds in what we have mentioned, as proof of knowing the rank of the second. The Prophet, peace be upon him and his family, is on the path of the First — the perfect — and is self-sufficient in his action, not using anything other than himself. The Foundation is on the course of the second — the perfect — but his action is not from revelation and Law in the manner of the Prophet, which he did not dispense with except in respect of the existence of any other deed of his. The subordinate community is conducting a subordinate orbit, of the natural world, in which its beings come out of potentiality into act by the flow of the second. The souls that are in potentiality need what they obtain — the degree of communion and perfection — from the Foundation's call. Just as the second is the one who supports those who are ready to accept, in addition to what comes from the House of Nature, likewise the Foundation, in its place in the call, is what makes the one who is willing to obey reach the degree of communion and perfection. That is the form: that what they were from the world of nature was not their perfection, and the sublime degree was not from the side of the First Intellect but from the side of the Second Intellect first — just as none of us reaches the degree of communion from the side of the Speaker, but from the side of the Foundation's call first. They proceed from those above them just as we proceed from them — as it is said, "the heel follows the heel" — so the perfection of pronunciation and its officials are in their place, and we have perfected them by being completed by them, with the favor of God upon them. The result is that these souls are not the successor.

<!-- page 93 -->
So whatever exists from among its conditions is evidence of its deficiency, and the protest raised by the author of al-Nusra against the author of al-Islah is therefore an untenable protest.

Chapter Thirty of Part One

He said: (1)

The next one is incomplete because it forgets its world and does not remember it. But how can this be sound? Forgetting takes place only after knowledge. And if these souls had existed previously while they were knowers, it would have been impossible for them to come down into the abode of nature after being in majesty and attaining perfection — which is intellectual knowledge — since the existence of what exists in the world of origination and emanation, deriving from the intellects, is an existence resembling that which is fixed in the world of body, which is not subject to the impossibility of what was bound to exist. Such existence is therefore eternal, and what is eternal cannot become subject to change — having the conditions for the possession of knowledge at one moment and the removal of it at another — since the abode in which it dwells is not an abode of impossibility, as he himself has affirmed when he uses the word "forgets" (2).

I say: if these souls had reached these persons from that world, and had come to learn (3) and to study the sciences, they would not have been free of an initial state of ignorance prior to their existence in this world — whether that ignorance belonged to them by their own essence or by accident. If their ignorance were essential to them, then they would be barred from knowing anything at all, since ignorance would be intrinsic to them, and that is impossible — for we find that they do learn after not knowing. Even if the ignorance were accidental, that accident must have come to them either in the higher world or in this world. If it occurred in that world — which is the abode of eternity and perfection, not the abode of impossibility

<!-- page 94 -->
and deficiency — then it is impossible for an impossible accident to befall it. And even if it had a right to that world, its end is to know and to return to the world from which it came — and that is followed by impossibility, since it is impossible within wisdom that it should remain in its first state, in which by hypothesis it is impossible for it to suffer such accident, then return to that state again. This is invalid as a case of accepting accident casually, whether the ignorance be specific or general (1) in this world, despite the fact that it exists without such an accident at all. If its very existence is not from this lower world, that too is impossible — for the perfection which yields benefit is the perfection that is among the blessings of that world, "belonging to the people of (2) knowledge," not of this world. And if this is so, and it is impossible that its ignorance be essential to it or that the accident befalling it have its source in that world — and if such an accident "having its source in that world" (3) be necessary for it to be said that it forgot its world — then the claim collapses.

Chapter Thirty-One of Part One

Then, if its arrival is from the world of origination, then its existence in the first place is on the side of origination and emancipation in the best proportion, and what so exists is already actually existent; and what is already in act is barred from accepting any accident — since acceptance of accidents follows the division of things that exist potentially, the latter being suspended between potency and act in the abode of body. That which exists in the abode of origination has its perfection and completeness conferred from the very beginning of its existence, which is eternal. Hence it is impossible for it to accept an accident or for what is impossible to occur to its existence. And if this is so, and yet on the view of the author of al-Nusra it is supposed that

3 — Dropped in copy (B).
— 92

<!-- page 95 -->
it forgot — an impossible accident — then it cannot be said that it forgot its world.

Chapter Thirty-Two of Part One

Then we say:

Forgetting befalls souls that are prepared, from within the natural world, to receive knowledge by entering into accidents on the basis of the causes of their existence; and there occur to them states proper to their being among the noblest souls — if they ground themselves by the prescribed religious rituals — and the things prior to them in existence have been linked back rationally to the First Principle, which we call Intellect and the Second Revival. The Next is the First Resurrection, as we have explained in our well-known book *Rahat al-'Aql* (Peace of Mind). And it is impossible for these souls to come from the world of intellect — a claim for which there is no evidence whatever. It suffices to point, for the refutation of what the author of al-Nusra said in his well-known book *al-Maqalid* in its forty-fourth Euclidean proposition, to his own assertion of the impossibility of these souls coming from the world of intellect — a clear sign refuting what he said elsewhere. I do not know how that escaped him. And as far as I can judge, *al-Maqalid* was composed after the book of al-Nusra. May God seat him with our pure loyalists in the House of al-Quds, and seal our religion and our worldly life with goodness through him.

Chapter Thirty-Three of Part One

Then he said:

That the act, in its perfection, does not forget its world, does not incline toward nature, and does not desire it — this is a correct statement. If it be said of the souls that have acquired knowledge and works, and whose perfection is complete, that they have become attached to the abode of nature and have come to resemble the intellects that have gone forth in perfection — like the souls of the speaker-prophets and the foundations (*natiqs* and *asas*) that stand in their place, the prayers of God be upon them — and the emanated intellects that are the First, the Second, the Preceding, the Following, the Originative and the Emanated,

— 93 0

<!-- page 96 -->
the First is loftier and further than to possess any portion of the natural world, and is likewise above inclination toward it; but its overflow remains connected to it, in order to complete what is yet incomplete.

Then he said:

If the soul were as perfect as the intellect, it would remain steadfast in what it saw in its own world; it would not desire that effect by which it has been relieved of its state — for the first state is not what is fixed upon it. This same sense has been expressed elsewhere as well.

And he said:

It would not desire it; when it is the agent producing the effect, it is an affirmation that nature is its effect. But how can it be sound to say that nature is the effect of souls, and to say that their existence proceeds from people who belong to the natural world? Or how is the universe of the heavens, the planets, the natures, and the engendered beings to be conceived as bound to people whose number can be enumerated? Or how is it sound that the perfect should desire its state in what is below it? That is impossible. And if it is impossible, then necessarily the existence of the natural world stems from the First Emanator, although (1) the perfect assets in it are abundant. To explain this is a chapter in itself, manifesting the prejudice of the author of al-Nusra against the author of al-Islah — even though the words of the author of al-Nusra are sound on the construction we have set out, not in the way he proposes in his rebuttal.

Chapter Thirty-Four of Part One

The author of al-Nusrah said:

The wise man mentioned in logic that all things exist in four modes: either in the very substance of things, or in the idea, or in the speech, or in writing. The things-in-themselves are those that the intellect has encompassed, and the intellect has not failed to encompass itself, for there is nothing that has no essence; and if a thing is not that which has an essence — which is what survives within the scope of the intellect — for the intellect, once it has encompassed it, gathers

(1) Dropped in copy (b)
— 94

<!-- page 97 -->
its wealth into itself; but the soul has encompassed only what the idea has admitted of being encompassed. And if it is among the things that have their own essence, and the idea is not present so as to bring it before us, then the soul has not gathered its own properly within itself, and so it remains incomplete relative to the level of the intellect, since it has not descended to the gathering of what has an essence. The intellect, by contrast, has encompassed everything that has an essence, and there is nothing that has no essence — for this is one of the clearest signs of the intellect's perfection.

This is what he said.

We say:

The discourse in this matter is not well-ordered, and what the wise man meant in saying it — if he said it — was to show that what exists is divided into four parts in the very nature of things. What he means is that some things exist outside the soul and have a meaning in themselves there: like the body, which is itself outside the soul, and whose meaning in itself is that which it is in itself — namely length, breadth, and depth.

He said:

"In the idea." What he means is those things that have no essence outside the soul, but whose presence is in the mind — like genus, place, and the circles that are drawn within the soul concerning the movement of the imagined creature, and so on.

He said:

"In speech." What he means is those things that exist in speech — like propositions in affirmation and negation concerning subjects, in which the subjects do not themselves change.

He said:

"In writing." What he means is those things that exist in writing, and the ruling on their existence in it is like the surplus divine matters from the world of intellect upon the speaker-prophets and the helpers, the prayers of God be upon them, which (1) — if they are not fixed

(1) Dropped in copy (b).
— 95

<!-- page 98 -->
in written letters and the forms are not deposited as a whole — the soul has no perception of them; whereas the affirmation of the nullity of its existence, and the use of it, [follows]. The wise man had restricted what exists to these four divisions, although upon investigation they diminish and decrease, with sub-classes falling beneath them. So it is allowable that the wise man meant to say what indicates that writing stands in place of (1) speech, and that speech stands in place of the soul in what one wishes to display, and that the soul likewise represents those things that lie outside it by picturing them — and in its place it attaches to them in the sense of being the same — by way of the acquisition of meanings, by which they are what they are. So in the words of the wise man there is no proof of what he wanted to support him in, regarding the speaker's failure to attain that great rank. And if the words of the author of al-Nusra are taken to apply to what is in the natural world — of the souls that have risen in rank and become already-existing intellects called a Second Emanation, which still remains a list of forces below the First Emanator — then in the world of al-Quds, this would be sound.

Chapter Thirty-Five of Part One

The author of al-Nusra made it incumbent, in his statement explaining the words of the wise man, that this is not sound if his words are construed on the next condition that he must affirm — that the author of al-Islah speaks rightly, since thought, for the souls that exist in the natural world, is a power for them to acquire what they do not yet possess the form of; and the end of this power is for it to be dispensed with — proof that the soul does not need that which it has already encompassed and whose form it already possesses, having already received it, in order to think; but it does need thought regarding things to which it has not descended, and whose form it has not acquired — so that, by thought, it actually attains them, reaching the degree of perfection and self-sufficiency. And if the soul becomes self-sufficient with regard to thought about something else,

(1) In copy 2 it reads: "about."
— 96

<!-- page 99 -->
We say:

What is meant by the "next" is the overflow and blessing upon the existents of the world — and I mean the engendered beings — to which He has given His power first, enriching them by what we have indicated in the words we have stated, for the sake of His perfection which He attained at the beginning of His existence and by which He became richer. And if this is so, then the prejudice of the author of al-Nusra against the author of al-Islah has been exposed in his discourse against him, and the truth of what he said has come to light — namely, that this is natural, not what is contained in the abode of al-Quds.

Chapter Thirty-Six of Part One

The author of al-Nusrah said:

As for one who said that the deficiency lies in the act of the soul and not in itself — because the act of the soul can occur only in time — this is wrong and mistaken, since what was done in past time need not be deficient. And if the problem lies in the deficiency of things in time, then it must be supposed that it lies not in a longer time being deficient; and the shorter the time, the more complete it would be. We see otherwise in the witnessed world (1), because things that may be more perfect, taking place in a shorter time, would then be lesser things. If the matter were as the author of al-Islah described, then it would have to be against him: time is not the cause of the deficiency of things nor of their perfection. Rather, deficiency in a thing arises from deficiency in the doer, or from deficiency in the substances, or from deficiency in both together.

We say:

The author of al-Islah did not claim nor say that the act is in time. He said that what is implicated as a meaning in it is attained through time, and that the duration is the lesser; and if it is obtained, it is complete. Everything in

(1) In copy (b) it reads: in the spectator.
— *Kitab al-Riyad* [7]
— 97

<!-- page 100 -->
the witnessed world has a perfection, and once it has received that perfection, it is perfect. It has been advanced above in saying that what is meant by

he said: the deficiency is in the act of the soul and not in itself — namely, that one means by it that the soul performs what enables it to attain the degree of perfection from its side and to acquire the form which (1) so that it is completed by its overflow. And what the author of al-Islah said — that the deficiency lies in the act of the soul, not in itself — is meant only as an indication that the deficiency in the capable self is from the soul which follows the overflow, and which can accept any payment only in time: in days, months, and years. And if this is so, then the statement of the author of al-Islah was sound from the outset in what he was discussing, and the statement of the author of al-Nusra, if construed regarding the natural [soul], is sound — though he wanted to be an advocate for his teacher. May God bless them and bring us together with the loyal ones, the prayers of God be upon them.

Chapter Thirty-Seven of Part One

The author of al-Islah said:

Al-Bari Almighty originated the intellect and time together in one fell swoop. The author of al-Nusra said: It is known that time is the measure of motion, and motion belongs only to the heavens, so it must be said that the heavens, and the time generated from their motions, came into being with the intellect at once; and it would equally be permissible to say that the powerful and corrupt beings came into being with the intellect at once; even if it were permissible to say (2) that intellect and time came into being together at once, then

this analogy holds: time is deprived of the virtues and lights given to the intellect, like what the intellect obtains, so that it is perfect — and that is impossible. Although it may be said that intellect and time came into being together at once, they cannot fail to be either two things or one thing. If they are one thing, then the plural

(1) Dropped in copy (A).
(2) See the introduction.
— 98


It does not fall under the same designation, because it is impossible to say that the stone was with the rock, or that the tide was with the knife, and that they were two distinct things. The intellect did not bring its own self into being without a time, nor along with a time; rather the intellect exists in itself without time, for it was, and time was not, and likewise it is not the case that the intellect was of itself in time, with time preceding it. What the intellect is preceded by is not time, but rather eternity and perpetuity.

We say: The conclusion of his statement, even if it is incomplete in its evidence and indication, is that the existence of time together with the intellect is impossible, and this is true on our own doctrine, and it follows as a corollary from the position that it is impossible for anything to be together with the first intellect. That is sufficient as a demonstration. The matter, however, is otherwise than what the author of al-Islah has mentioned in this chapter, except that the author of al-Nusra said that what precedes the intellect is not time, but rather the eternal and the everlasting — for there is nothing that precedes the first intellect. We have explained this in the statement above in this book and in the book *Rahat al-'Aql*. What is correct is that the law of belief and doctrine binds those who maintain that the world of origination is eternal and everlasting to what we have set out in the treatise known as *al-Riyad*, and in what we have mentioned in it, which need not be extended further here.

## Chapter Thirty-Eight of Part One

The author of al-Nusra said:

(1) By way of arguing that the intellect became perfect because it was not subject to anything other than the Almighty, while the soul became deficient because it was subject to nature:

(1) See the introduction.

We say: The philosophers, including the foremost among them, although they were eminent in their own time, were nevertheless prone to error in many of the intelligible matters of which they spoke. Had they lived in our time and witnessed the springs of blessings that overflow from the House of Revelation — that things are in truth as the Alawite call declares them to be — they would, by their own diligence, have done justice to themselves (1) and to many of their beliefs in which the witness of the senses falls short. With the traces of the One in whom God has placed a light for unveiling the mysteries of things, they would have been convinced. But in their times, they followed their own opinions and intellects in matters of this kind, so that some of them ended up holding beliefs whose implications align with the position of the literalists of the Hanafi school when these latter rely on their intellects alone to follow the paths of religion. Their opinions diverged; among them were some who were closer to being correct, and some who were more in error. He who relies (1) upon what the balance of religion attests to, in the horizons and in the souls, has been spoken of in the discussion above, where the falsity of any belief in such a position has been shown. There is no need to dwell on this at length, except for what the author of al-Nusra has mentioned in his rebuttal. And we ask God to illuminate our intellects, and to conclude our affairs in happiness, and to grant us good outcomes and good deeds, by His existence, His kindness, and His generosity.

(3) Omitted in copy (B).

## The Second Treatise of the Book of al-Riyad

On the adjudication between the two disputants concerning what they discussed about the intellect, which is the first originated being.

### Chapter One of Part Two

The author of al-Islah said:

We say: Motion and rest are two effects that the first and the second share in common. For when the first was originated, it was united with origination, so that origination produced in it two effects necessarily. We say "two effects" because origination and the originated are two — one of them is origination itself, and the other is the originator itself. The originator and the originated, even if they are two, may be understood as one essence, which is the whole. These two effects exist in the first by potentiality; then the second is emanated from the first, and so the emanation gives the intellect two effects: one of them is the emanation itself, and the other is that in which the two origins are united in the act. One of the two effects is motion, which is higher than the other, which is rest; and the one from which it is, which is the first, is higher than the other, which is the second. The motion of the first is what benefits the second of all the essences in general, and the rest of the second is what receives the benefit of the first of all the essences in general and what is contained within it.

I do not say that motion and rest are produced from the soul by potentiality which it has learned from the intellect; rather I say that they are two effects of the soul by means of the power learned from the intellect in matter and form, so that matter and form became the foundation of this world.

The conclusion of his statement in this chapter is: that motion and rest are two effects shared by the first and the second; that they exist in the first by potentiality; that one of them is higher than the other, namely motion; that motion benefits the first, and rest is the acceptance by the second of what the first conveys; that the soul receives these from the power learned from the intellect in matter and form; and that when the first was originated, it was united with origination, and that origination and the originator are two, even though they are one essence.

The author of al-Nusra said:

This statement contains some confusion, because motion is only on account of seeking. As for the motion of bodies, it is like the motion of fire upward to seek its place, and the motion of earth downward to seek its place, and the motion of souls to seek benefit. When seeking (1) is established (2), then need is established, because seeking arises only from need. It is impossible to say that the intellect needs anything for which it would move with a motion. Even if it were permissible to ascribe motion to the two origins, ascribing it to the soul is more fitting than to the intellect, because the soul needs to benefit from the intellect, moving with the motion of longing and the motion of effect toward cause. If it is established that motion belongs to the soul, then rest belongs to the intellect — for the intellect rests in what it receives of the virtues and lights bestowed upon it by its cause, and it is not a seeker of increase, because its cause has overflowed upon it from the lights and illumination that the intellect cannot know in any further way through motion in seeking it. The intellect has not discharged all that overflowed upon it onto the soul, but only some of what overflowed onto it, so that the soul might dwell upon it and grasp it when it is set forth.

(1) In copy (B): "it is established."
(2) Omitted in copy (A).

For some seek to move in their seeking. The conclusion of the statement is: that motion arises only from need, that the intellect is not in need of motion, that ascribing motion to the soul is more fitting than to the intellect, that motion belongs to the soul, and rest to the intellect; and that the intellect has not discharged everything it has upon the soul except in part, so that this becomes the cause of the soul's motion in seeking the remainder of what it has.

We say: As for the statement of the author of al-Islah, that motion and rest are effects shared by the first and the second, and that in the first they are by potentiality, and in the second they are by actuality — this is to affirm that the first is at rest by actuality, and moving by potentiality, since it is moving and resting in itself by potentiality, and that the second is moving by actuality, and that the second is moving and resting in it by potentiality, and that the second is at rest by actuality, since it is moving and resting in it, and that the second is moving. This is impossible in several respects.

As for the statement of the author of al-Nusra — that the intellect is not in need, and therefore does not move — it is correct, because motion in all its varieties is only found in natural bodies. Yet the first and second intellects, being non-corporeal beings, cannot be either moving or stationary, on the proof that the body must (1) be a substance, and the need of either of them in existence for the other prevents its existence from being from God Almighty as the first existent. For what exists from God is the first existent in itself, and is nothing other than the very act of which it is the first existent. The Almighty and Most Great — His same act has one subject, not two. If the existence of one of them were not the first existence, it would have to be preceded in existence by another, in which case it would be a thing preceded in existence by another. Nothing precedes the first intellect in its existence, since it is the very act in which origination resides, behind which there is only God, the Creator, the Almighty, whose existence is indicated. Since it is excluded that anything precedes it in its existence, it is invalidated that it should be a body. If

(1) Omitted in copy (B).

it is invalidated that it should be a body, and there is no motion except where there is a body, the intellect has no motion. And if it is invalidated, then the statement of the author of al-Islah is also nullified — namely, that the intellect moves by potentiality.

### Chapter Two on Chapter Two

The author of al-Islah said:

We said "two effects" because origination and the originator are two: one of them is origination itself, and the other is the originator itself. Origination and originator, even though they are two, are not one whole; this is an image in which it should not be believed, so it follows that origination and originator are contrary to what he mentioned — namely, that they are two, that origination is the originator, and the originator is origination. They are one only when added to that whose existence began through being his act, and through being the same act. If added to itself, however, it is the principle, namely that it is an object of another whose existence is from it.

### Chapter Three of Part Two

On what the author of al-Islah said, that motion and rest in the first are by potentiality, and in the second by actuality:

We say: What is first in existence is only mere act and the agent of the act. Even if it were permissible for motion and rest to be in the first by potentiality, then the ruling of the second would have to be more honorable than that of the first — because the ruling of what is in actuality is, in honor, not like the ruling of what is by potentiality. And it is impossible for the second to surpass the first in honor. What he mentioned is among the qualities of that which comes later in existence, which requires action for its outward manifestation, and that which already exists. The first intellect is purely intellectual, and so too is the second, and what follows is paradoxical for the substances of the intellects. It is not permissible to believe that what necessarily exists from the House of Origination is specialized in matters that lie outside the higher limits.

### Chapter Four of Chapter Two

The author of al-Islah said:

Motion and rest are effects of the soul by the power learned from the intellect, in matter and form.

What is meant is that the bodies which exist in matter and form are moved by the natural power present within them, which derives from what is outside, namely from the first and second intellects — the motion of longing (1). He made the two effects come about in matter and form. The form is its mover, and that it should itself be movable is impossible. This is what is "learned from the intellect," in the sense that its existence is from it, and it is correct in this manner, and it is the better of the two ascriptions. What does the meaning of what he said amount to?

(1) In copy (B): "the eastward direction."

### Chapter Five of Part Two

The author of al-Nusra said:

Motion belongs to the soul and rest to the intellect, so it is not permissible to believe that these exist within those transcendent limits.

We say in summary:

The world of origination is the totality of before and after, and the pure intellects, which are the highest paradoxical substances of all things, are above any object of supposition that could be ascribed to them — whether motion or rest. For motion in what moves is that in which the thing in which it resides is something other than it,

(1) In copy (B): mentioned as "the eastward direction."

and possesses an existence of its own. If motion is in what moves, the thing in which it resides is other than it, and it possesses an existence of its own and what it is. Where existence of self exists in the presence of different things, the self is deficient and in need. The second, which he called "soul," from the world of origination, is specialized in perfection and completeness; and that which is in no way deficient and in no way in need cannot be different in itself. If it is excluded that there be in itself something other than itself, and if it is excluded that what is not itself be in it, then it cannot be moved. And if it is excluded that motion be in it — for motion is that in which the thing in which it is located moves away from its first state to a subsequent state, whether in the self or in its states — and that which follows, which he called "soul," whose existence is eternal because of its arising by way of origination from the second, is intellectual; and if it is eternal, then it is impossible for it to be transferred from the state in which it is found first — then it is invalidated that motion should be in it. To speak of rest is like speaking of motion. The first intellect has no rest, nor does the next, which he called "soul," have motion. Rather, what the author of al-Nusra wanted to say is what he said concerning those souls and intellects which are within the House of Nature, where a secondary emanation is emitted, and the being is moved according to what we have explained in our well-known book *Rahat al-'Aql*: that motion and rest are specialized in the world of body and its contents, because they are among the causes of its perfection, not in what is specific to the House of Origination. But he transferred the meaning to confusion in what he said by way of supporting his sheikh, through misconceptions. May God ennoble his standing, and may He gather them and us together with our pure followers (God's prayers be upon them all) (2).

### Chapter Six of Part Two

The author of al-Nusra said:

Motion arises from need, and the soul is more fitting than the intellect for this, as has been shown from what was said above.

(2) Omitted in copy (A).
(1) The sentence is omitted entirely in copy (A).


<!-- page 109 -->
It may be said that movement and stillness are properly attributed only to what is a body or what resides in a body. The First and the Second, of whom we are speaking, are not embodied, so any talk of their movement or stillness is to be set aside, in the sense of movement and stillness that we have already explained in our well-known book *Rahat al-Aql* (Repose of the Intellect).

As for the claim that this is true of what was originated by way of re-emergence: the Second was not creative, and its existence consists in the first emanation, not in any movement.

In every instance where need is invoked for what he mentioned, the movement in question is in a natural thing — as one of the causes of its perfection, like the motion of the heavens, which does not move because of need in itself for something it lacked at the moment of its origination, but rather because that motion *is* its perfection. So too with the movement of the speaker-prophet, and his striving to gather people to worship, and his exertion in matters of governance: this is among the perfections that necessarily belong to him, because by it something proper to him is brought about after he has attained perfection — namely, his rising to the rank of prophetic utterance — even though he already exists.

So the author of *al-Nusra*'s statement that movement for the sake of need is not established is, in this absolute form, not verified. Rather, his ruling about *some* natural things is correct, on the basis that need is inseparable from them, as we have mentioned.

## Chapter Seven of Part Two

The author of *al-Nusra* said:

What does he hold concerning the soul, other than that some… What he meant by this saying is that the First is distinguished by the rank of priority and the highest station in what is not proper to anything other than itself — what he called "soul" — which is the noblest thing that may be attributed to it by way of interpretation.

Otherwise, if the matter were as he described, and each of the *hudud* received its existence from what is above it by way of overflow, then the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) would have been wronged: the blessings that reached him from the world of *al-Quds* (Holiness) would have flowed onward, leaving no trace of them upon the *wali* (guardian) and his followers, and he would not have communicated anything of them to anyone — which is plainly invalid. The consequence would be that the *wali* (peace be upon him) would be made doubtful in his perfection and merit, and his standing in the place of the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him)
- 107

<!-- page 110 -->
and his family, and the obedience due to him, would also be cast into doubt and suspicion. From this it would follow that it is not permissible to give credence to the speaker-prophet, nor to the guardian, nor to the *hujja* (proof), nor to the *hudud*.

Rather, we say:

The blessings overflow from the side of every rank. The reason that those blessings by which perfection is attained do not reach every being, and that the *hudud* do not hold them, is the incapacity of the receivers to accept them — corresponding to the strength of the one who is able to receive. Some receive the blessings in full; some receive only part of them. Some are by nature unteachable; some are compassionate teachers; some are willing learners. It is not permissible to limit the *hudud* in such a way as to withhold the benefit of the benefactor, so long as the receiver is entitled to it when it is willed for him — lest he should ascend to a rank that was his before his spiritual birth.

The Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) said: "Do not kill your children for fear of want." And whoever did so has sinned and overstepped his station and fallen
(1) from the rank of the virtuous. How then can it be permissible to lock away the spiritual wealth — which is to say the sciences and the radiant blessings — from their rightful owners, when the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) commanded that such wealth be paid over to them, as he said: "If you find them to be of sound judgment, then hand over their wealth to them, and do not consume it in extravagance and waste"? No: the blessings do overflow, but only to convey them to those who deserve them, and only by report from those properly situated, in the manner we have explained in our hermeneutic letters and our Basran and Baghdadi *majalis* (councils). God — and a company of our brethren — has assigned us the duty of delivering this trust to its rightful possessors and to those who are faithful to its conditions. The author of *al-Nusra* may perhaps, by way of analogy, have transmitted what he said.
(1) Dropped in copy (A).
- 108

<!-- page 111 -->
## Chapter Eight of Part Two

The author of *al-Nusra* said:

Furthermore, action is the essence of encompassing — it surrounds things — and this is not the condition of what is moveable, for the moveable does not surround things; rather it stands in need of something (1) to abide in a state of stillness, so that by its stillness it may encompass. Even when the intellect moves, it surrounds something; but the soul does not surround itself with what it would benefit from. When we found that an ocean gathers things while remaining itself unmoved and surrounds them — whoever encompasses what he obtains, gaining from its light and virtues — we know that the intellect is utterly still and that no notion of movement attaches to it; whereas the soul only moves in order to reach the threshold of the intellect, and then to abide there, and it does not move in order to surround. By drawing benefit from what it stands in need of encompassing — and so long as the soul is in motion — it does not surround, and it does not attain the virtues and the lights, until it abides by drawing benefit from it, and then it surrounds things. The soul thus moves a movement of subsisting that comes to rest by drawing benefit from the one that is settled. This is what he said.

We say:

It might be said that the intellect is not moving with respect to what does not endure, and not that the intellect, if it is not moving, must therefore be at rest in the way of bodies (which are not free (2) from being either at rest or in motion), because its existence is not like the existence of bodies that are never free from these. The arguments already given are sufficient, and they indicate that movement and stillness do not properly apply to it. Therefore it is required to say that, so long as the soul is in motion, it
(1) Dropped in copy (A).
(2) Dropped in copy (B).
- 109

<!-- page 112 -->
does not encompass anything and does not attain virtues — but this is not a sound meaning, however the case is parsed. It is not differentiated from the Second in the manner we have indicated; nor does it apply to what is not differentiated; for souls residing in the house of nature acquire only by being moveable and embodied, employing the senses, deductions, and utilizations, and the matter exists at the establishing of the evidence for it. This much is sufficient to make plain that the error in this point persists.

## Chapter Nine of Part Two

Then he said:

The soul is moving in a movement of subsisting which comes to rest by drawing benefit from the one that is at rest. He spoke truthfully when he gave a meaning to this; he spoke falsely on the face of what he says. To speak truthfully is to take "soul" here as the natural souls, in which case it is said that the soul is moving, because the natural souls, which exist in potency, move toward acquisition; and his calling it "a movement of subsisting" is correct because such acquisition leads them into subsistence. His saying that "it comes to rest by drawing benefit" is correct, because by acquiring and drawing benefit and hunting down knowledge — virtues both in knowledge and in action — the soul goes out of potency into actuality. As for the one with whom it comes to rest, he is the one from whom the soul already takes its being. The reason he is in error if his statement is carried at face value is that the Second is not a moving thing, and the intellect is not a resting thing in the sense in which we apply rest to bodies; they have no need of movement, stillness, and the rest. One ought, then, to occupy oneself with knowing the levels of the *hudud*, so that the matter of perception is not confused — for things grasped only through their accidents lead to corruption of the essences of souls. God is the light of our intellects by means of these *hudud*, and we are made happy by following the pure stages of the divine sciences and the treasuries of the lights of *al-Quds* (Holiness) — may God's prayers be upon them.
- 110

<!-- page 113 -->
## The Third Chapter of the Book of *al-Riyad*

On the judgment among the disputants concerning what is spoken of in regard to soul and nature: whether they are like the First or not.

### Chapter One

The author of *al-Islah* said:

Concerning what the author of *al-Nusra* has established: we do not say that nature and darkness are born of the Second in the way that the Second is born of the First, because that world is the world of nobility and virtue, while this world is the world of darkness (1) and (?) the world, and the meaning of the Second's being born from the First is that the Second resembles the First. Nature does not resemble the condition of *al-Hayuli* (prime matter / hyle).

The author of *al-Nusra* said:

This statement is wrong. If that world were light with no darkness in it, and this world were dark with no light in it, then there would be no link between this world and that. Rather we say that the soul has two extremes: one of them toward the intellect, and it is in the intellect's horizon —
(1) In *Nissan*, (3) reads "umbrella."
- 111

<!-- page 114 -->
this is the luminous, noble side; and a part of the soul lies toward nature, with nature in the soul's horizon. There is some darkness in this side, after the manner of a circle, because nature is in the soul's horizon. We likewise say that nature has two ends: one toward the partial soul, in which there is some light because it lies in the soul's horizon; and the other side facing the elements, which are the darkness in which there is no light. By making these distinctions we speak of the soul, of nature, and of their darkness and their light; and we do not say that this whole world is the world of light with no darkness in it, nor that this whole world is a world of darkness with no light in it — for if it were as he described, there would be no conjunction between the two worlds, nor any mating.

He said:

It is true that the natural beings are born of the soul, even though they do not resemble it, just as the soul is born of the First even though it does not resemble it. It has only fallen into a state of weakness and darkness because of its distance from the First Cause, in order that it may be born from the soul; just as the falling-off in the soul itself occurred because it is beyond the First Cause — I mean beyond what came before it. This is what he said.

We say:

The wording of what the author of *al-Islah* said in his book differs from what the author of *al-Nusra* said. The kernel of his saying, in answer to the statement of the author of *al-Mahsul*, is this: that the Second is born of the First yet is not like the First. He said: I do not say so. Rather we say that the Second is born of the First, and is not like the First, and is similar to it — even though the First is of the highest rank and most honorable station; as with male and female in every species and substance: the male, though higher and nobler than the female, is nevertheless the counterpart of his companion and her like.

Then we say:

The Second is more honorable and lofty than that its relation to its like should be like its relation to the First. The First and the Second are two counterparts, the very source of honor, perfection (1), and elevation. There is no honor above them; God has made them the source of perfect honor, of perfect light,
- 112

<!-- page 115 -->
of perfect elevation, and of perfect knowledge — and there is no ignorance there, nor revolving, nor darkness. Yet they founded the higher world, which is the mineral of purity, of *al-Quds* (Holiness), and of life. *al-Hayuli* (prime matter) is a trace of the Second, which is the essence of this world — the essence of a darkness that cannot be described by mercy or by power. Yet the Second has manifested *al-Hayuli* with the strength learned from the First, and then it has brought forth from it the image which is nature itself.

Then the author of *al-Islah* said:

It is not permissible to say that *al-Hayuli* (prime matter) is born from the Second; rather we say that *al-Hayuli* is an effect of the Second. Therefore *al-Hayuli* does not resemble the Second; and the Second is born of the First and does resemble the First, though the First is of higher rank. This is what he said. The kernel of it is that the Second emanates from the First and is like it, even though the First is higher in rank; and *al-Hayuli* is an effect from the Second, its essence being a dark essence overcast in gloom — because the path of the divine relation toward the Second is not like the path of the Second toward the First. The First and the Second are counterparts; *al-Hayuli* is not similar to the Second, because *al-Hayuli* is a trace of the Second. If this is the upshot of what he says, then what the author of *al-Nusra* answered is no answer to him; and the author of *al-Islah* has not committed himself to saying that this whole world is light upon light. He spoke describing the condition of the First and the Second: that God Almighty has made them the source of honor and elevation — not that he has proved that nothing exists which is not like them. And in his account there is sufficient indication that *al-Hayuli* is not generated from the Second. To press the speech further with what we might think on our own behalf is not our intention here; the matter rests in the foregoing summary (1).

We say:

*al-Hayuli* is not similar to the Second, nor to the First, for its existence is by way of emanation from
(1) Dropped in copy (A).
(2) In copy (B), the sentence …
**The Book of al-Riyad [8]**
- 113

<!-- page 116 -->
the First, at the lower proportion that we have shown in our previous words and in our well-known book *Rahat al-Aql* (Repose of the Intellect). This is sufficient.

## Chapter Two of Part Three

The author of *al-Nusra* said:

This saying is wrong, because if that world were light and there were no darkness in it, and this world were dark and there were no light in it, then there would be no link between this world and that world.

We say:

This statement, as it stands, is established against the author of *al-Islah* unless he can be cleared of error. In terms of what he himself has said, it is in fact correct: he is from the world of origination and re-emergence in such a way that what is shared in him befits what is above him by way of being emanated, and what is below him befits him by way of being a body. He has two aspects, neither of them like the First, from which he derives his existence. The Second resembles the First on the one side and does not resemble it on the other: the side from which he resembles it is that he is emanated; the side from which he does not resemble it is that he is a natural body — and that nature, when it was added to the world of origination and resurgence, was a life that proceeds valid from that world without it, and gave it from itself what it has to receive: backwardness, and perception. And when it was added to this world, it was the principle of movement, and it became the very thing in which it exists by way of self-attribution, not by way of accident; and so it became nature — even though, in its existence, it is emanated, or a medium between the two emanations of the First and the Second.

## Chapter Three of Part Three

The author of *al-Nusra* said:

The soul means the Second's two sides: one side of it toward the intellect, in the intellect's horizon,
- 114


<!-- page 117 -->
which is the noble luminous limit; and the other part is toward nature, in nature's horizon, in which there is some darkness.

We say:

This is not correct. The proof is that what has sides is what has diameters measured by area. As for the Second — which we have named "soul" by what we explained above — if it is non-corporeal, then it is no body; and in that case one is obliged to say that it has a luminous side that lies in the horizon of the intellect, and a side in which there is some darkness that lies in what borders on the nature apparent to the intellect. The first of these accords with the description of nature already mentioned, and it is impossible for the second [side] to have what the first denies. For its existence originates from the noble proportion within the First — namely, that the First is cause; and we have already said that the First is, in that it exists, and is not in itself a cause. The effect [*ma'lul*] is a cause in addition to what is below it — which is the lower proportion within the First; and the effect, by addition to itself (which is the lower proportion within the First), is from its own intellect: it is a cause that emanates something like itself, since the cause has its like as its effect. And from its own intellect — in that it is *malaisa* (non-being) — it emits what is not like itself, by being an emission from the lower proportion within the First, namely the proportion of being effect; because the proportion of effect is more honorable than the proportion of cause. So the cause obtains the higher [aspect] from what arises about it, except that what lies below the effect (that exists about the cause) testifies to what we have mentioned: that [the foundation] is, in this respect, the parable of the Second in the science of the body. The body, being invisible to the speaker [*natiq*], is all that was originally there; it is present in the speaker, from whom the foundation took its existence. And likewise, not everything that the speaker has is there for the foundation, because the speaker has what the foundation does not share — namely the visible, in which the foundation does not participate. So [we have established] that the foundation is one in which the speaker participates, and that there is nothing in the speaker that the [foundation] does not indicate; and if it does not indicate it,
- 115

<!-- page 118 -->
then it is the very parable by which the existence of the Second is inferred by its rank — and its rank was, for the Second, not made manifest in the First. And if it is not indicated, then its existence — as we have mentioned — is in the horizon of nature, which is impossible.

## Chapter Four of Part Three

The author of *al-Nusra* said:

*al-Hayuli* (prime matter) fell into darkness because of its distance from the First Cause, so that nature might be born from the soul.

We say:

The fact that *al-Hayuli* is not like the First, and the Second is not [made *al-Hayuli*] because of distance from the First in proportion: this is the case of the First's effect, which combines two proportions relative to its being cause — the proportion of being effect, and the existence of something that issues from the proportion that is effect, which is unlike the existence of something that issues from the proportion that is cause; for the cause (1) is more honorable than the effect in every respect, and prior to it in rank and nature. Because that of which it is said to be a cause, and that of which it is said to be an effect, are one and the same self: it acquires these proportions only by way of addition.

## Chapter Five of Part Three

The author of *al-Nusra* said:

The decrease occurred in the soul because it is beyond the First Cause — I mean the one preceding it.

We say:

There is no deficiency in the Second. What he called "deficiency," as we have mentioned, is not
(1) Dropped in copy (A).
- 116

<!-- page 119 -->
that it is far from the First, but that it is like it; and the decrease is necessary, not in its very nature, but it became incomplete — not from the side of its own self, which is perfection, but it became incomplete from a side in which the Second's perfection is acquired through its receiving benefit.

## Chapter Six of Part Three

The author of *al-Nusra* said:

The proportion of the birth of nature from the soul is no greater than the generation of the soul from the intellect. The soul is less than nature; so that the soul, although it is permissible for it to be generated from the intellect with [the intellect's] perfection and honor and its closeness to the First Cause of the soul, is itself permissible to give birth to (1) nature — for nature more closely resembles the soul in all its states, in its actions and its powers, than the soul resembles the intellect in its actions and powers. For nature, among the ancients, is labeled as the principle of movement; and some of them said that it is the active force. If you look at nature from these two limits, it is the best fortune and the best installment, because the soul among the early thinkers is the surviving moving essence, and is nevertheless labeled with mind — not in the sense that "mind" is one of the characteristics that delimit the soul. The soul also seriously challenges the intellect: if the limit of the intellect is that it is the first of things, that it is the Creator, that it is the first effect, and that it is the first mediator — then it is not permissible to say that the soul is the first thing, nor that it is creative, nor that it is the first effect. So it is permissible for [the soul] to be born from the intellect with [the intellect's] perfection and honor and closeness to the Word — like the soul that does not bear the limitation of the intellect — and it is not denied that it be permissible for [nature] to be born from the soul, since nature is similar in its limits to the limits of the soul. And this is not to be denied, even if one looks at the matter from the standpoint of [bare] consideration — this much of the knowledge would not be hidden — even if the author of *al-Islah* stood upon
(1) Dropped in copy (A).
- 117

<!-- page 120 -->
the wonders and wisdom of nature, the splendor and honor in its actions, and the beauty from which the partial soul desires to incline here and follow it. For you know and are sure that [nature] is born of the soul, even if it does not resemble it; because effects never desire to govern themselves with respect to their causes — and from this the partial soul comes to desire to follow nature. And [you know] that all goodness, splendor, honor, and adornment is from the higher noble world. If nature were not born of the soul, [the soul] could not tell of beauty, comeliness, and adornment at all; but in order for [nature] to be generated from the soul, [the soul] tells what it saw in its world of beauty and honor as an incomplete story — a story without [completing] the soul. So know what he said.

We say:

If there is any precedence in speech over the intellect, the soul, nature, and the existence of the above — its existence over others — and otherwise [if not], it entails the correct statement. I quote it without needing to elaborate with certainty on what carried over the error in perception. In this there is sufficient.
- 118

<!-- page 121 -->
## The Fourth Chapter of the Book of *al-Riyad*

On the judgment between (("the hunters")) and the discussion concerning the soul — whether it has parts and effects.

## Chapter One of Part Four

The author of *al-Islah* said:

According to what the author of *al-Nusra* has established: as for the parts that are in us, they are traces of the First Essence, not parts of it. Our union [with it] is by way of the effects of the First Essence, not by its parts; and the union of the First is not by its parts, because the First is united by the very same Word. The parts that are in us, even though they are not parts of [the First Essence], nor united with it [by way of parts], are nevertheless united with it by its traces — as we have shown of the union of its handiworks with our traces in it. And the union of the First Essence is likewise by the Word, because the First Essence is united by the very Word — until [the First] and the Word became one thing. This is what he said.

The author of *al-Nusra* said:

This statement is confused and apparently contradictory, because he said
- 119

<!-- page 122 -->
that the parts that are in us are traces of the First Essence — but how can they be parts and at the same time relics, while also being essences in themselves? (And the effects do not (1) have power by themselves.) And if he says that the parts that are in us are traces of the First Essence after applying the name "parts" to them, then he must indicate that they are parts of an essence; for if they were not parts of the First Essence, they would not be called parts of an essence. And once it has been established that they are parts of an essence, the proof of this is that they are traces — because the parts of a thing are not traces of others. So he said.

We say:

The statement of the author of *al-Nusra* — that the souls (2) are jewels existing by themselves after acquisition, and that effects cannot exist by themselves — is true. But the statement (3) of the author of *al-Islah*, in what he said and in his refusing to give the souls the name "part," is along this line: he saw that what are integral are bodies that are inseparable from the nine accidents and are aware of the five senses, whereas the soul is not a body, nor is it overflowing with object [in the manner of bodies]. So he did not see fit to give it the name "part," lest it be run in the course of bodies — so that nothing should match what we have said of it. When he did not assent to that, he said "traces," and named [the souls] by the fact that they are an essence and remain. What is meant by this is that the essence and survival of these souls is by way of the effects of those paradoxical essences which reach them from the side of the *hudud* (limits), who are the mediators — peace be upon them. If this is so, then it appears that the intention of the author of *al-Islah*, in saying that the effects of what runs in the soul is the running of the image, and what is done by it is essential, and by it you will remain if you accept it, is what compelled him to say so. As he says: "But we have said,
(1) The sentence is omitted in copy (A).
(2) In copy (A), [reads] "the soul."
(3) Dropped in copy (A).
- 120

<!-- page 123 -->
honor and merit belong to these worlds because I have accepted (1) the effect of that noble essence in full, and we were prepared to accept it"; for it is true that the recipient wills what [the giver] wills and accepts what [the giver] accepts. And what the author of *al-Nusra* means by saying that it is part of what runs in the soul is the running of *al-Hayuli* — which is acceptable. This is an analogy and an approximation for [the sake of] the understandings.

## Chapter Two of Part Four

The author of *al-Nusra* said:

That the souls are parts of the universal soul rests on no proof. If [the universal] is the totality of those parts, then the whole is divided into two parts — [and] all these parts [together] — he has been obliged [to say] that the souls are parts of the universal soul. The souls in the abode of this world are sustained by the evidence that has been given before and after. The Second is what is named "soul," whose existence is not in the house of the body — which is the house of the world. And if there is no [universal] in this world to be the Second, [containing] both classes of these souls, and if it is not possible for there to be a whole of these souls, then these are not parts of it.

## Chapter Three of Part Four

Then [the proposition] positively that they are parts of the universal soul is not correct, given his intention that the universal soul is the Second. So the matter does not escape [these alternatives] in the case of the soul, if it is parts of the Second: that they be either all of them, which is the Second in the house of the world; or some of them in this world and some in the higher world; or [parts] not in the sense of "part" in this world. It is invalid that they be all that is Second in the entire house of the world, because in this world [the soul] is based on potency, and there is no way out for it toward actuality except by what already exists
(1) In copy (A), "we said."
(2) The sentence is entirely omitted in (B).
- 121

<!-- page 124 -->
— and [there is] the refusal of the First Intellect to be that by which the existing souls are brought out from potency to actuality, because of the regress this would require, which is impossible by God Almighty's standard, based on what was said in books other than this one. It is also invalid that some be in the world and some in the higher world; because if some of those in the higher world were like those in the world based on potency, [they would lack] what their existence requires. This is invalidated by the evidence that the existence of the Second is an already-existent existence, not like the existence of the abode of the world, which is a standing potency. After the invalidity of these two divisions, it has been proved that the Second is not part of the world, and it is invalid for the souls to be a part of it.

## Chapter Four of Part Four

After [the discussion of] the parts, [the author of *al-Islah*] gives what he gives to them all together. These souls were parts of the Second — and we found them neither knowing nor wise; not all of them are parts of this category, and a belief like that, to that extent, is great unbelief — we seek refuge in God. For if it is known that the substances of the Messengers (God's prayers be upon them) [come] from it, which is the closest cause of the overflow upon the world below, the souls also (1), if they are not parts of [it], then since these souls are not parts, we say: the souls are of the nature of those paradoxical things whose existence is creative, [whose] appearance and existence are only among the natural and phenomenal worlds. It is a natural force that acquires what is gained from the *hudud* (limits) through obedience and worship (2). So it emits from itself an abstract subsistent image that is like the abstract things in the house of the Hereafter on the one hand, and the opposite of it on the other.
(1) Dropped in copy (B).
(2) Dropped in copy (A).
- 122


<!-- page 125 -->
Hence we say: this is the second origin, and our treatises on the Ma'ad include the mention of it, God willing.

Chapter Five of Section IV

The author of al-Nusrah said:

Likewise, the effects bound to us through our crafting do not accept further effects from us once we have finished our work; nor do they accept the products that are ready to be received from us, except by introducing corruption into them and changing them from their states, (1) because they are not parts of us, nor are they subsisting substances; (2) they are transient accidents. This is what he said.

We say:

What is meant by this is that bodies are limited in what they can accept. They reach a point where they accept nothing further alongside it — like iron heated in fire: once it is fully red, that is its limit; it does not grow hotter. Likewise, a black body does not grow blacker; its blackness may reach a point beyond which it will not be increased. This is part of [the meaning of what he claims], and he is the first to accept what his own statement requires: the effects are not what bring forth, but the receiver — that is, the body subject to the succession of accidents and effects.

Chapter Six of Section IV

The author of al-Nusrah said:

But the rational souls in us continue to gain from the light of the world above — the Most High, the Most Noble — and each soul grows in honor within itself without any change in itself, and without any corruption being introduced into her by the [universal] Soul wishing to benefit her. This being so, she is true (1) [to her nature] — (2) [as is mentioned] in itself —

(1) In copy (b), the same is stated.
(2) In copy (b) it is mentioned in itself.
- 123

<!-- page 126 -->
parts and substances of the soul upon which no effects fall.

We say:

The truth is that the soul is infinite in what it can accept of what he described, and this is one of the proofs that the soul is not embodied. The matter regarding the soul being parts of the Second [Emanation] is not as he held it to be in what he said, claimed, and embraced.
