The best that can be granted to what he said is this: for those who wish to call them parts, these souls are effects insofar as they are rational. As such, the [universal] Soul and what she has produced are one thing — she is what is hers, distinguished from her offspring only by rank. So it is like the part of a thing in which the whole already exists; in all of them, their nature is in themselves one and the same from this aspect.

Chapter Seven of Section IV

The author of al-Nusrah said:

Also: to say that the rational souls existing in this world are not parts of the universal Soul nullifies prophethood, imamate, guardianship, revelation, and the laws. For it is not permissible for the soul to perceive its influencer, encompass him, or benefit from him; and yet the prophets (peace be upon them) have claimed that they saw the noble upper world, stood upon the spiritual ranks, and benefited from what was sent down upon them in light and manifestation from those above them — the Origin and the three branches — and that they saw the angels. If their pure souls were merely effects of the universal Soul, they would not have been able to grasp what was inspired in them, nor stand upon the spiritual entities that are parts of the universal Soul.

We say:

It has already been said that the souls are not parts or effects, but a second emanation. What is meant by those who say they are effects is that the actions performed through them
- 124

<!-- page 127 -->
are essential to the souls and grant them survival. As for those who say they are parts, they are — in their own rational nature — like the things that have brought them forth, and prophethood need not be nullified on this account, nor the imamate, nor anything else, for it has been established that these ranks are not invalidated by what he claims. The prophets (peace be upon them) were able to perceive these things absent to them from the upper world after they had reached the level of perfection: they came to stand at the limit at its end, and when they returned to themselves they realized that the transcendent selves are one thing in respect of wholeness, with no difference between them except in rank. We have shown in our well-known book *Rahat al-Aql* what suffices on this matter and brings healing.

Chapter Eight of Section IV

The author of al-Nusrah said:

Rather, it is necessary to say that the Intellect is creative through the Word, and the Soul is the image of the Intellect; Nature is born of the Soul, and the rational souls are parts of the universal Soul, so that by this order tawhid, reward and punishment, prophethood, imamate, shari'a, and revelation are upheld.

We say:

The validity of tawhid, reward and punishment, prophethood and imamate, the messengership and the laws drawn from revelation, is not proved from the standpoint that the Intellect is creative through the Word, or that the Soul is the image of the Intellect. It is proved through another aspect of the Intellect that we have explained in our letters and treatises about them. As for the Intellect, it is not permissible to say that it is creative through the Word; the Word must be presented to the Intellect because of the imbalance in its modes (1) [regarding]

(1) In copy (b) the matter is stated [differently]:
- 125

<!-- page 128 -->
tawhid, according to what we have explained concerning speech. Then we say: the Intellect is nothing (1) but the Word itself, and the Word is none other than the Intellect itself; therefore it is correct to say what Maulana Imam al-Mu'izz li Din Allah (peace be upon him) said in the book *Ta'wil al-Shari'a*, where, speaking of intelligibles, [he said] that the Word is the Intellect, and that these names properly belong to it. Our explanation and statements concerning the Creator, the Intellect, the Word, and what is said about world-creation and the First Emanation — in the treatise *al-Munira* and other letters — are sufficient for the inquirer.

But the Soul and Nature both emanate from the First Intellect, each according to its own proper measure, so that one of them is nobler than the other — and that is the First Emanation — and the rational souls are the Second Emanation. What occurred in the first of them is recapitulated in the last of them. Our treatises on the Ma'ad alone contain what is needed of this discussion, beyond which any further mention would carry what is not required of ta'wil and amount to extravagance in speech. Likewise, in every restricted matter, a hint suffices the seeking student; others abandon it because it is difficult to attain and hard to know. May Allah aid the believer who comes to know this according to his capacity in devotion to Allah and to His awliya, and help him if he is sincere to His face and to Him and His awliya. May Allah make us among the followers who fulfill their covenant — and we have been blessed with His praise and glory.

- 126

(1) In copy (b) it is not mentioned.

<!-- page 129 -->
Section V of *Kitab al-Riyad*

On the judgment between the two disputants in the discourse concerning whether the human being is the fruit of the natural world.

Section V, Chapter One

The author of *al-Islah* said:

The human being is the fruit of this entire world, and it is for his sake that this whole world has been created — from its principles and foundations: first, the principles of the simple individuals brought into being from them by motion and stillness; then the individuals that are the heads of corporeal bodies; then the composites that are the generated things of this whole world; and the natures from which is constituted the human form, ready to receive the three souls: the vegetative, the sensitive, and the rational.

The author of al-Nusrah said:

To say that human beings are the fruit of this world is also the doctrine of the Dahriyya (the eternalists), who do not affirm the soul's survival after its separation from bodies, because (1) if the human being were the fruit of this world, this world would have held him and would not let him move on to another world. But we would say:

(1) Omitted in copy (b).
- 127

<!-- page 130 -->
the human being is the fruit of the highest world — the noble, illuminated world — and for this reason the prophets (peace be upon them) came warning humanity to provision themselves from the house of mortality for the house of survival, and from this world for the abode of the Ma'ad, because they knew that this world is not his home, nor is he the fruit of this world. This is what he said.

We say:

Both speak truly in one aspect of what each has said, and both speak falsely in another. The aspect in which both speak truly is that each has said of the human being what is rightly his. The aspect in which both speak falsely is in denying what is true of him — that he is the fruit of all the worlds, because the human being is brought forth from between the two worlds. From this world he receives his first perfection, and from that world the second perfection: for the end of the natural world is to give to his existence its first perfection, which is the production of the physical form by the circular bodies, the luminous planets, and the causes that bring the form to the state of accepting what is proper to it — so that the human being has the capacity to receive, and is the fruit beyond which there is nearly nothing further. And the end of that [higher] world is to give what comes before the existence of the natural world: the second perfection, which is the perfecting of the spiritual form (2) — the natural precursor — intrinsically, and abiding [through] the knowledge of the oneness of Allah Almighty (3) by means of all the hudud (peace be upon them), and obedience to them in both acts of worship — in knowledge and in deed. This is its fruit, and it corroborates what we have said in that statement of Maulana Amir al-Mu'izz li Din Allah Ma'add ibn Isma'il (may Allah's prayers be upon him) in the book *Ta'wil al-Shari'a*, where he says that the human being is the purpose of the Second [Emanation] and the end of the action of nature.

Section V, Chapter Two

As for the statement of the author of *al-Nusra* and his objection that if the human being were the fruit of this world,

(1) Omitted in copy (b).
(2) Omitted in copy (a).
- 128

<!-- page 131 -->
this world would have held him and would not have let him move from it to another world — by this he has merely shown his opponent how to turn the same argument against him. For [his opponent] could say: and if it were as you said, that the human being is the fruit of the higher world, then that world would hold him and would not let him return [to be] among these people — so that, by your own reasoning, what you wrote and asserted would be impossible. Thus this statement does not establish what he intends to prove in the unqualified sense, nor does what he said about the prophets sending their communities to be provisioned serve as proof that the human being is [solely] the fruit of [either] world. The human being is in truth a human being because he is constituted by the meeting of the overflow of the worlds: from the world of nature comes what flows in the stream of bodily existence, and from the world of the intellect comes what flows in the course of form — and this happens through intermediaries.

Section V, Chapter Three

The author of *al-Islah* said:

The Soul emanates from the First Intellect in completeness; Nature emanates from the Soul in completeness; and form emanates from prime matter (hayula) in completeness — and all of these are complete.

The author of al-Nusrah said:

If the matter is as he said, with the Soul as perfect as the Intellect, then what is it that is spread out upon the figures, the form, and the composites? This is invalid; it does not stand. It is permissible to suppose that these products come only from the pairing of two — one rich in perfection, the other deficient in need — such that a coupling takes place between them, as we observe in the visible world with the sky and the earth. The heaven is like the male, perfect and rich in all its visible cyclic motions; the earth is deficient, in the position of the female, and her need [for the heaven] is established (1). For this reason a marriage took place between them, and from them appeared the various kinds of metals,

(1) In copy (a) it appears as "established."
[Kitab al-Riyadh 9]
- 129

<!-- page 132 -->
along with the various forms, plants, and animals — none of which were actually visible in the heaven or the earth, nor in the elemental mothers; rather, they existed in potency. When [the heaven and the earth] were wedded, all of them appeared in act, even if it required, as we mentioned, the deficiency of one and the perfection of the other; this is what they then produced. This is what they said.

We say:

As for the statement of the author of *al-Islah* that the Soul is the second [emanation] emanating from the First Intellect in completeness, it is correct, in accord with what has been said above. But as for his statement that prime matter (hayula) emanates from the Soul, we have already shown that its existence is not from the Soul — for the Soul is the Second [Emanation] from the First Intellect, and neither of the two is presented to the other as a basis. Neither of them exists abstracted from the other except in the mind as a supposition; outside the mind, they exist only together.

As for the statement of the author of *al-Nusrah* that one cannot suppose the products come only from the pairing of two — one rich in perfection, the other deficient in need — so that there may be a coupling between them, and his likening of this to the heaven and the earth: he himself refuted it by what followed in his own words, namely that there appeared from them the various kinds of metals, forms, plants, and animals, none of which were actually visible in the heaven, in the earth, or in the elemental mothers, because they existed in potency. And this means: if some of these things — I mean the generated things — are not actually visible in the heaven and the earth because they exist in potency, then the heavens and the earth are equal in respect of their being in potency. Neither of them shows any preference over the other in this regard, contrary to [his claim] that between two existing things one must necessarily be complete and the other incomplete. We have shown that deficiency is found in things not in their essences, but in their ranks when they are compared with one another, and in their actions, which do not occur except in something other than the thing that performs them. Each thing's existence is perfect in itself; for if its essence were not perfect in the existence proper to it, no act could proceed from it, either by itself or by another. What is witnessed in the state of created things bears witness to this — for the heaven, which he singled out for perfection and artistry,
- 130


<!-- page 133 -->
It is incomplete in its acting, since the action is performed only by what is being acted upon, and the earth is specifically such a recipient. With its diminishing and its need, its deficiency is not in itself but in its action; the action is performed only by the one for whom it is sought. The same applies to the animal and to newborns: if this is so, then the statement of the author of *al-Nusrah* — namely his report of the saying of the author of *al-Islah* — is a contradictory and prejudiced statement.

**Chapter Four of Section Five**

The author of *al-Nusrah* said:

He did not answer what was said at the end of this chapter concerning the name *al-Tamamiyyah* (perfection) being applied to the Creator Almighty, so as to apply it instead to complete creativity.

We say:

The author of *al-Islah* did not mention any of that. The statement he quoted from the wise men in the *Book of al-Islah* — that the perfect creative is a complete creativity, and that what is perfect is perfect by virtue of nothing other than itself — is a saying than which none is more correct. It follows that what is most perfect and complete is the Creator, the Originator Almighty, and that the name *al-Tamamiyyah* belongs first to the Creator (swt). His statement does not contain anything otherwise, and the assertion of the author of *al-Nusrah* on this point is a prejudice against the author of *al-Islah*.

**Chapter Five of Section Five**

The author of *al-Nusrah* said:

We all say that creativity is the effect of the Creator, and that He and the creativity are, from the side of the Creator, one thing — namely the perfect; and the creative through complete creativity is nothing other than the perfect. Nor do we need to say that the Creator is perfect, or that He is glorified and exalted; the Creator and the creative are one thing.

- 131

<!-- page 134 -->
We say:

That is a correct statement, but we are not saying that the Creator is complete creativity and nothing but perfect, because creativity is perfection. What we are saying is that the creative who is the perfection is nothing but perfect, and that the Creator is the creativity, He is the cause and the effect, the perfect and the complete. The names are synonymous with respect to additions, because of the plurality of essences, so the meanings of these names apply to all of them. It is one thing only. We have already mentioned this in this book and in others, so let this suffice for length.

**Chapter Six of Section Five**

The author of *al-Nusrah* said:

If it is established that this world is the act of the Soul, and is nevertheless highly ordered and arranged, with perpetual circular motions, then we know with certainty that by acting she sought to reach the noblest rank before she actually began. For if this were not the case, there would not have been in the world any noble being capable of receiving the effects of the Soul from her. We also know with certainty that human beings are the fruit of the Soul's reaching her end; this is estimated by way of her cause — so understand this.

We say:

We have already said earlier what implies that the universal Soul, in her action, did not seek to reach a rank she had not yet attained. The universal Soul — which is the second — rather pours forth upon this world, not because she is drawn toward it for something not yet hers (for God enriches her by what is with Him), nor by seeking something she has not been given. Rather, her action is to establish wisdom by bringing what is in potency into act, and to make that

- 132

<!-- page 135 -->
which is virtuous a mean, and her existence is not delayed by what is above her. If one meditates on this, one sees that the benefit accrues to what proceeds out into action, and to the one who is under the burden of being content with perfection.(1) There is no one of whom action is too costly, nor anyone in need. And whoever believes that in that great expanse there is fault or sin — if that were so, then human beings are of two sides: one part natural, one part divine, for the human being is not the fruit of this world alone, nor the fruit of that world alone, but the fruit of all the worlds. The human is the last in the universes, united in eternity with what has been acquired by time. Therefore it has been said that the last of them was already in the first of them. Whoever knows the stations of the prophets, the executors, the imams, and the righteous — may God's blessings be upon them — knows this; for he is ready for salvation. May God gather us together with our pure loyalists in the Abode of Holiness.

**Chapter Seven of Section Five**

As for the saying of the author of *al-Nusrah* — that every agent who performs an act does so in seeking something he wishes to attain, especially if the act is from the agent in motion and in order, with affirmation — this is not a necessary condition for the First Creator, the Originator. For action there is in the way of metaphor, not in motion, nor in order. The First is the last; the beginning of Him is the middle of Him is the end of Him; there is no order in Him and no sequence. Rather, the systems and the orderings in what follows benefit from the lights of what is not in order or system — namely the First Intellect. The Soul too may resemble the Intellect closely: if you look at her, you perceive the powers of things that are not in order or sequence, but you perceive things in a single grasp. This belongs to her if she is united with the Intellect and pushes back the monstrous things of corporeal(2) dark nature.

(1) In copy (b) it appears as: "in attaining perfection."
(2) Fell in copy (A).

- 133

<!-- page 136 -->
We say:

How can what he said be correct — that arrangement and order exist only from the Intellect — when general things are made preferable only by the Intellect, and the Soul is not affected by the effects of the Intellect, with the result that she and the animals would be alike? On that view, God would have lifted knowledge from the Soul, so that the effects of the Intellect would not act upon her. We said above that the issue does not lie in the agents: it is not the case that every agent seeks by his action to attain an end or a purpose that he estimates by saying. Rather, the First Intellect itself is not a vehicle for ordering and arrangement, for the Intellect perceives only the truth.

- 134

<!-- page 137 -->
**Chapter Six of the Book of al-Riyad**

In it he speaks of motion, stillness, and form.

**Chapter One of Section Six**

The author of *al-Islah* said:

As for the discussion of bodies and form, they are with respect to motion and stillness like body for them. The first *hayula* (prime matter) is illusory motion in the vehicles, and the *hayulanis* (matter-like things) in a single statement of three. What has been said of that in this book contains much confusion. I say, in brief, that motion and stillness are to *hayula* and form as form and *hayula* are to body — for motion and stillness are gentler than body, and body is denser than soul; likewise motion and stillness are gentler than *hayula* and form, and *hayula* and form are denser than motion and stillness. To represent the gentler with the soul and the denser with the body is appropriate, but to represent the denser with the body and the gentler with the soul is too early. This is clear, and the proof goes with it.

The author of *al-Nusrah* said:

The situation of the Soul and the body, and the comparison between them in terms of gentlest and densest,

- 135

<!-- page 138 -->
is not as he has described. For if it were as he mentioned, it would have been required that the tangible essences be objects of the symptoms — since they are denser than the symptoms — and that the symptoms be souls for them, since they are gentler than the essences. Rather, what is required is that we look at the limit of body and soul, and then liken to the soul what resembles the limit of the soul, and to the body what resembles the limit of the body. We say: we have found that the body is what accepts the three dimensions — length, width, depth — and we have found that the soul is what has parts resembling each other. Likewise we have found that motion is divided into three sections — comparable to the divisions of body — such as motion from the center, motion toward the center, and motion around the middle. So motion must resemble body: when it is divided, it divides as body divides into length, width, and depth. *Hayula* is not like this, because the parts of *hayula* resemble each other; and because motion resembles body if they are alike in the kind of division, and soul if they are alike in the parts, the first thing that motion resembles is soul — that is, the one motion which is the undivided body.(2) The resemblance of *hayula* is that it is not divided, while form is the body that is divided. This is what they said.

We say:

In this chapter the two have departed from likeness and simile, and between us and both of them stands the fact that they have built upon a corrupt root upon which the proof rests; neither the simile nor the analogy by which they represent it is true. The author of *al-Islah* has held that motion and stillness are to the soul as

The mover is the act of the engine, and the engine is what we call the soul. So the soul, and stillness as well, is the engine's letting go of moving

(1) Fell in copy (A).
(2) Fell in copy (A).

- 136

<!-- page 139 -->
what it moves. If the engine is the soul, then to represent motion — which is her action — by her is to represent the impossible. The same holds for stillness; and if it is in motion and stillness that this is invalidated, then it is also invalidated that motion and stillness are *hayula* and form. The path of *hayula* and form is therefore not the same as the path of motion and stillness, because motion and stillness, even when they are actual, are two opposites that do not meet in the same place at the same time, whereas *hayula* and form: their existence together cannot dispense with the other, on condition that one of them is an agent — which is form — and the other is an object in which the action occurs — which is *al-hayula*. Our book known as *Rahat al-Aql* (Repose of the Intellect) contains an explanation of this.

**Chapter Two of Section Six**

Then he required himself to say that the soul is gentler than the body — because the soul is a body, and that is impossible: the soul is not embodied to have density or gentleness, for density and gentleness are specific to(1) that which is a body, just as air, which is a body, is specifically gentle, and earth, which is a body, is specifically dense. The soul is the soul, and the evidence is established that it is not embodied. Once the soul is not embodied, then to say of it that it is gentler or more dense, or to make a likeness on that basis, is impossible.

**Chapter Three of Section Six**

The author of *al-Nusrah* then came forward and undertook to give the limit of body and soul. He set the limit of body as that which accepts the three dimensions, and the limit of the soul as the unity of parts in resemblance, and provided this with a flow of water, fire, and air — each of which is similar in its parts. It is impossible

(1) Quotation marks are present in copy (A).

- 137

<!-- page 140 -->
to go beyond saying that the soul is not embodied. Soul and self are two names synonymous for one thing, and that thing is not a body. Were it a body, it would have been required of it the nine qualities and symptoms of which body has need, and it could have been perceived by a sense; but since it is not perceived by a sense, it is not subject to illusion. It has nine symptoms which do not exist in it; and since the physical symptoms — which are the very mark of being a body — do not exist in it, our well-known book *(With Lamps)* contains what heals this point.

**Chapter Four of Section Six**

Then he made motion divisible into three parts resembling the body, on the grounds that the body is divided into length, width, and depth. This is impossible. The three sections into which the body is allegedly divided — namely the three dimensions — each one of these three dimensions is not embodied, and is not said to be a body. The motion of each of its sections is itself motion, and is said to be motion. So how is it likened to the body, which is divisible into nine sections, as we have shown in our well-known book *(Milestones of Religion)*, by a motion that is divided into three sections, or six sections? That is far off. And the discussion in this chapter on the part of the author of the harvest, the author of *al-Islah*, and the author of *al-Nusrah* is irregular disorder.

We say:

Motion is that by which a thing's selfsame state changes — either by place, or by quality, or by corruption, or by increase, or by decrease, or by impossibility — and its occurrence is from the mover which is the principle in the thing in which it inheres, naturally and not by width. *Al-hayula* and form are the first emanations following in proportion as emission. The second is regarding the noble proportion in it. There is no existence of the soul outside the soul in the first place,

- 138

<!-- page 141 -->
together with the form, including the heavens and the earth and what is between them. God Almighty created all of this through the First Intellect, which was created by God and not from anything — a great elevation above.

**Chapter Five of Section Six**

The author of *al-Islah* said:

The First Cause, which is creativity, is the word of the Almighty and Exalted. Its form is *kun* (Be), which is two voweled letters (kaf and nun), and the unvoweled letter — the kaf and the nun — of the word stand in the position of body; motion and stillness stand as the soul. This is the example of motion and stillness with the word. The first cause, which is the word, will be the whole world, for example. It must therefore be said that motion and stillness are in all the essences of *al-hayula*, and form is below it in all the worlds, and that the whole of the discussion concerns the three worlds. The first beings are illusory: they are motion and stillness; they are *al-hayula*. Their form is the second *hayula*, which are individuals and the begotten of the first *hayula*. The third *hayulas* are the four composite mothers from the second *hayulas*.

Then we say:

The Houthi and its form are the act of the command, which is creativity, as we have said. *Hayula* is illusory, and so creativity is imaginary; it is shown only in the first creative.

The author of *al-Nusrah* said:

As for what the author of *al-Islah* has raised about *kun* — that he said that the kaf is movable and the nun is stationary, that this is like the body, and that motion and stillness

- 139


<!-- page 142 -->
As for the Self itself, it is not established for Him that these two letters are the words about which speech here applies — because at this level there is no kaf, no nun, no motion, and no stillness. One cannot say of what is above the First Effect that it is in motion or at rest, or anything like such expressions; rather, what is there is pure ibda (creativity) and complete causation. This is what they say.

We say:

The claim of the author of al-Islah — that the First Cause, which is ibda, is the word of God, and that its image is two letters — is a remote analogy in the sense he intended, and it scarcely holds. If we say "image," it entails something that has an image, and the word is not a picture. If we say "picture," meaning what is above nature, we mean the abstract image; and if we say "the image in question," we mean the image that subsists in matter, in the natural world. And when he says that ibda — which is the word of God Almighty and His command as expressed by it — has two letters, one movable and the other a consonant, indicating the very union of two attributes, this points to the inference of the noble Essence and its attributes.

He said:

It combines two states, just as the word "Be" combines motion and stillness; so the two states are attributed to that Essence in respect of two honorable relations, one of which is its being a cause, and the other its being effected. The motion that terminates in stillness corresponds to the relation whose cause terminates in an effect, and the stillness whose end is motion corresponds to the relation in which the effect terminates in the cause — for there is no motion or stillness here except by way of analogical understanding. Otherwise the First would have been a cause only incidentally, in which case the speaker would have to affirm that He is the agent of His own Essence. — 140

<!-- page 143 -->
Section Six of Chapter Six.

Then he said:

The First [Effect] is imaginal, and He made it motion and stillness; then He made it motion, stillness, and hayula. The image is not these hayulas, but the motions and the stillnesses are the supports, and the image is an essence emanating from the First upon what has been discussed above — including the lower world. Motion and stillness have their existence in bodies, upon their principles concerning which the hayulas and the image appear. Together they constitute the body as a shape and a picture in the body.

Section Seven of Chapter Six.

Then he said:

When he made the First Hayula correspond to the matter that is ibda, the First Hayula is imaginal just as ibda is imaginal, and it shows itself only in the First Creator. He said that this must hold in the presence of the Almighty, because of His pride in that: since ibda had not yet appeared, He became Creative through ibda. This is a grave error, because the doctrine and belief in monotheism is that what is other than Allah Almighty — and He is the Creative One and the Originator — had no existence at all; and even if it had, ibda is nothing other than the Creator Himself. We have built upon this the demonstration sufficient to our argument.

Section Eight of Chapter Six.

1 — Missing in copy (A).

The author of al-Nusrah said:

What the author of al-Islah said, and the points on which he differed, is correct, except that what he said — 141

<!-- page 144 -->
that above the First Effect there is motion and rest — affirms them positively for the First Effect and for what is below it. We have shown that the First Effect is not subject to motion, so motion does not befit it; nor is there stillness for it, because it is other than a body.

Section Nine of Chapter Six.

The author of al-Nusrah said:

Contradictions occur in bodies, not in spirits.

We say:

Bodies are bodies because they are objects; there is no differentiation among them and no contradiction between them in that respect. Difference and contrast lie in the images by which what animates them is animated — not insofar as they are bodies.

He said:

And because what is in it is similar to what is already there, the matter follows what he said, if the very speaker were such.

— 142

<!-- page 145 -->
Chapter Seven of the Book of al-Riyad.

On the judgment between the two.

So speak of it in the sections on the world.

Section One of Chapter Seven.

The author of al-Islah said:

He says: the entire world is composed of four jewels, four structures, and four divisions of human beings. It stands upon three jewels apart from human beings, and the fourth is human beings. The knotted and the dissolved are all one essence. We do not say that the pair of the human in form is the horse; rather, the horse's pair and its form belong to its own essence — just as gold and silver are a pair, and as ruby and emerald are a pair, and as the horse and its form make pairs among animals: horses, elephants, falcons, lions, and so on. Because they are all animals, it is not permissible to say that the horse is the pair and form of the human being — for the human is alone in possessing the rational soul, and he has no counterpart in any of the parts of the world or their offspring. Rather, he is the counterpart of the world as a whole, because he gathers all the jewels of the world. For this reason he is called the small world (microcosm), because he counterparts the whole that gathers its parts. — 143

<!-- page 146 -->
We say:

The "whole of human beings" is the result of his saying that the world is divided into four parts. The horse is not in the form of humans, and humans have no counterpart among the parts of the world; rather, the human is the equivalent of the whole world.

The author of al-Nusrah said:

The state of pairing is not merely the state of [biological] sex; the state of pairing is the state of acceptance.

We say:

This dense lower world is the pair of the subtle upper world. If you look between them, you will find a distance beyond the distance between the horse and the human, for the horse is not of the human's species — they are joined only in being of the genus of life, and the horse shares in that. He made this dense world the pair of the spiritual world because of the connection between them in the chapter of bestowal and reception of benefit: the forces of the higher world are connected to this world, and this world receives those forces from the upper world; so he made this world the pair of that world. Likewise the human and the horse — he made the horse the human's pair because the horse accepts instruction from him more than the other animals accept it. The result of his saying that the horse was made a pair for the human is that he accepts instruction from him, and no animal accepts it like that acceptance.

We say:

The investigators in what they discuss have not relied upon sound research — for instance the author of al-Islah, who divided the world into four jewels, four structures, and four sections together with human beings. That is impossible, because the divisions of the world are more than what we have mentioned, as we have said. The word "world" refers to many things, from the center to the ocean: the sphere — 144

<!-- page 147 -->
and the planets, fire, air, water, earth, and the offspring [of nature], according to what he estimated and inferred. It is clear from his words that he intends the offspring of the world, not the world together with the offspring; for they are three genera, with no fourth, and each of them has many species under it. The first of these is the nearest of the four arcs, the foremost of which is the metals — all that comes to be in a fixed place of the earth without growing in its parts, and which combines what dissolves and what does not, and what breaks and what does not break. The second is the plant — all that grows from the earth, and this combines low growth and trees. The third is the animal — all that moves and has species, combining what flies and what does not fly. The offspring of the world do not exceed that. The human being who is named as the fourth shares in the genus of the animal, with a kind of conscious awareness.

Section Two of Chapter Seven.

He said:

The human has no counterpart and no pair, and the horse has no pair. That is impossible, on two grounds. The first is that the human is a species of the animal genus, and inasmuch as he is a species, he is the pair of the other species of his genus — since all species form each other within the chapter on quality. If human beings are a species, then they are a pair to others among the species. The second is that human beings would not have been likened in their structure, morals, actions, and all their conditions, motions, and natures to all that they encounter in the universe of existence — according to what he mentioned, and on account of which it is said that he is the small world — unless he were a pair to all cosmic things according to occasion and circumstance. If this is the case, then human beings, if they are good, virtuous, wise, religious, knowledgeable, and pure, are paired with the angels on account of the occasion between them; if he is courageous, brave, strong, and helpful (1), his pair is the lions; if he is unjust, cruel, and murderous, then he is paired with the wolves and the deception of foxes; if he is a treacherous thief, he is paired with the crows and the hawks; if he is deceitful, cunning, and overpowering, the tiger is paired with him.

(1) Missing in copy (1).

Kitab al-Riyad [10] — 145

<!-- page 148 -->
If he is an adulterer — of immorality, amusement, and play — then he is paired with the monkeys. According to whatever a person is satisfied with — whether by his actions, or his morals, or his customs, or in some portion of them — he will have a pair of like kind. If all of this is established, then what he said in the chapter — that there is no pair for human beings — is not sound; human beings have many forms and many pairs in many respects.

Section Three of Chapter Seven.

As for the author of al-Nusrah, he said that what was correct concerning the meaning of common genus is what he referred back to. He said that the horse was made a pair for human beings because the horse accepts the teaching of human beings. But if it were on the basis of acceptance and instruction that it became his pair, then the horse has no priority over others — for in the animal kingdom there is one who accepts education and refinement and is not the mare; and if the criterion is qualitative, then the mare is not such either, to be a pair. He has a priority over all other animal species, since he has many pairs rather than one pair.

In sum, we say:

In truth, the pair of human beings can only be the angels. What we mean by that is human beings of all virtues — the prophets, the guardians (awliya), and the imams (may the prayers of God be upon them) — and those who follow in their footsteps, establish their obedience, and worship Allah with both modes of worship. Not the others, from among the people who are in the form of human beings but whose souls are themselves the souls of beasts and predators — as we have said and shown, like monkeys, donkeys, foxes, the wild beasts, and the wolves. And those who follow the traces of the Imams (may Allah's prayers be upon them) and above them, from among the prophets and guardians (may Allah's prayers be upon them) — their diligence in obedience, in both modes of worship, and their adherence to command and prohibition distinguishes them from the beasts, so they resemble them. Peace be upon them; they will be saved in their entirety, and none among them except: ("and who will take care of them from among you — 146

<!-- page 149 -->
he is one of them"). May Allah make us among those who follow their party and spread it together with the prophets, the guardians, and the pure Imams (may Allah's prayers be upon them all).

Section Four of Chapter Seven.

The author of al-Nusrah said:

He mentioned Bandhaqlis [Empedocles], who said:

The bestial soul looks toward the rational soul out of love; and the rational soul, if what comes back from her is good, will be kind to her with benefit and aid. Her image is desirable, and there is no better image among the bestial souls than the image of the horse, and none more eager to accept the influences of the rational soul than the horse. So he made the horse her pair, and this is clear.

We say:

If the author of al-Nusrah were drawing inference from the Book of Allah, the Lord of the Worlds, and from the words of our followed pure Imams (may Allah's prayers be upon them all) concerning what he wants regarding the command of the soul from the standpoint of interpretation, that would be richer than the words of the Prophet [taken alone in this regard]. If he and his like had lived in our time, they would not have failed to draw from the seas of knowledge of the People of Revelation — knowledge which overflows from the side of God's reservoir on His earth, namely our pure clients (may Allah's prayers be upon them all) — and they would then have spoken on much that they took to be hidden from the senses. They would have spoken from the intellects and their ranks, and from the soul and its principles, and would have come back and believed otherwise: namely, that what Bandhaqlis intended is what he said, not what the author of al-Nusrah went toward in interpreting him — that the bestial soul is the horse and that the rational soul is the human being. If he were to return to the balance of consideration and take the face that is true, then, since the sensual soul, which is bestiality, would accept the ruling of the rational soul if it is not hindered, and the rational soul, if it sees the sensual soul as midwife to — 147

<!-- page 150 -->
its ruling, would have its judgment kindled toward her by virtue of its own discernment — we say: what he means by "the sensual soul" is the one who is in the form of human beings, the one who would accept education, discipline, and reason — not the horse. And what he means by "the rational soul" is the teachers, who are the prophets, the guardians, and the imams (peace be upon them) and their followers — those who would be kind to those who are in the image of human beings, and teach them their interests in their religion and worldly affairs, and benefit them according to their senses, and raise their grades according to their acceptance — they being in truth the mothers of human beings. Indeed, everyone agrees with what is indicated by the interpretation of the words of Allah Almighty: "the reassured soul — return to your Lord, well-pleased and well-pleasing" (the verse), and we carry his saying on the face that is correct. It is, in the first place, a statement on the face that is not correct: the horse is not more receptive than those who are in the form of humans, nor is its image better than their image; nor are the guardians, imams, and their followers — with the least of their sympathy for those whom they take up — less in worth than some people on account of the training of the horse and its rectification. On the contrary, they are greater and greater and greater still. If this is the case, and there is no clear statement that constitutes any evidence that there is no better image among the bestial souls than the image of the horse — nor was the matter, regarding the existing animal image, more than the goodness of the horse's image — nor is it the case, as he said, that the horse is to be the pair of human beings on the side of education, good image, and invocation — that is impossible.

Section Five of Chapter Seven.

The author of al-Islah said:

Human beings are the counterpart of the whole world, and they are preferred to it by the noble essence which is not of this world; (1) his body is contrasted with all bodies in their dumb genera.

1 — Missing in copy (B).
2 — Missing in copy (A).
— 148


<!-- page 151 -->
…and solidity, (3) and dissolution — for within his body there is something corresponding to each kind: bone, flesh, nerves, veins, cartilage, (4) and all the liquid substances (5) that flow from his body, such as blood, mucus, sweat, and what flows from the eye, the ear, the mouth, and his other parts. All of these belong to the essence of bodies and their kinds, which correspond to the kinds of the essence that is one of the three essences of the world — namely, the essence of bodies, which is not unified by anything of the three breaths.

His growth, in turn, corresponds to the growing species — the various kinds of trees — which belong to the second essence, unified by the vegetative soul. His sense corresponds to the animal in its various kinds, which belongs to the third essence, unified by the sensitive soul (the soul of beasts). With this, he meets the three jewels of the world; and his uniqueness comes in the fourth rank, where he becomes the fourth essence: the essence of the speaking (rational) soul, in which he stands alone.

This is the substance of the author of al-Islah's saying: "The human being is the counterpart of the whole world. His body stands opposite all the bodies of the world in their differing kinds; his growth opposite the plants; his sense opposite the animal; and he possesses something that is preferable to all of them, in which he is alone — the speaking soul that is his."

The author of al-Nusra said:

As for his statement that the dissolved and the contracted are a single essence — this is not warranted. Rather, the dissolved, the knotted, the growing, the sensitive, and the speaking are all one essence at the level of the genus, and what divides them are chapters and properties; the contracted essence is not the same as the dissolved essence, nor is the dissolved that is not coagulated identical with what is coagulated. They are distinguished only by the place at which they separate, not by the place at which they did not separate. I needed to separate them because he made the human being a mediator between the dense physical world and the gentle spiritual world.

3 — Fell in copy (B).
4 — In copy (B), "the extremities" is given.
5 — In both versions, "the inanimate" appears and is meaningless.
— 149

<!-- page 152 -->
For in him is everything that is in the world, so that everything in the lower world has a likeness in him; he leaves nothing of it out. Thus he set bodies opposite his body — and his body is knotted, like the contraction of bodies (6) — and then he set the dissolved bodies opposite his growth, because of the dissolution that is in them; for dissolution occurs only through the movement of the jewels that dissolve. Growth, likewise, is achieved only through movement; so, when these resemble one another in the chapter of movement, each is almost the same as its companion. He set bodies opposite his sense, and he set the sperm of the animal opposite his sense — gathering in him everything that the rest of the world holds of knotted and dissolved bodies and of the developing and the sensitive — and he placed him above the other essences.

This is the substance of his saying: the human being so resembles the world that nothing of it has been left out. His body is among the bodies that are contracted, even as it stands opposite the dissolved bodies; and he feels as the beast does, even as he stands opposite the developing bodies; and he speaks, in relation to the animals; and he surpasses, through the rational power, all other jewels.

We say:

The dispute between the two parties in this chapter is this. The author of al-Islah set, opposite the human being's growth, the plants of the great world that grow and develop. The author of al-Nusra, by contrast, set in this place the dissolved bodies. The author of al-Islah set, opposite the human's sense, the great animal world; while the author of al-Nusra set, opposite his sense, the developing bodies. And the author of al-Islah set the speaker of the human being — that which singles him out, preferring him over the world — as the counterpart of the animal, while the author of al-Nusra made the speaker the counterpart of something else. The position of the author of al-Islah is closer to what the analogy actually resembles, and is more in conformity than the view of the author of al-Nusra. In general, the human being is not only a counterpart to this world; he is a counterpart to all the worlds, since he is the collector of everything that preceded him in existence, and there is in him what corresponds to each of them. The matter is built upon this, not upon what he said.

6 — Fell in copy (B).
— 150

<!-- page 153 -->
Chapter Six of Book Seven

We have said that the human being — meaning man — is the counterpart of the worlds because he is the collector of much of what exists between the first creation and the second resurrection. He then became one, parallel in himself to the creative and emancipative world, and the collector of bodies parallel to the physical world. The two worlds, then, are like this, and the world is wholly contained in him.

A body may be of two kinds: gentle — that is, high — and dense — that is, what was. The human body, likewise, is gentle and dense; the gentle is not the highest. In the world there are planets and stars around it; and in the body of the human being, likewise, there is the skin that surrounds the whole body, and so on, covering the bones. There are openings that run the course of the planets, such as the ears, eyes, nose, and mouth. In the human being there is also a moving driver, which is the self; and in the world the four cardinal directions; and in the human being likewise the four natures. In the world there are rivers, valleys, and waters of different tastes, mountains and minerals; and in the human being likewise — as the author of al-Islah has it — there are bones and cartilage, flesh and blood, what flows from him and what comes out of him. In the world there is what grows and develops without feeling; and in the human being likewise there is what grows and develops without feeling, like hair and nails — and just as he grows and increases from his youth to his full stature. In the world there are sensitive animals; and in the human being likewise there are strong sensitive powers. The whole world is composed and integrated, and is supplemented by what exists in him — this is the analogy to the world. All of what we have mentioned is a summary detailed in our book known as "The Repose of the Intellect."

What corresponds to this in the world of creation and emancipation: that world is the world of the intellect, the world of praise and laudation and sanctification, the world of monotheism and divinization, the world of virtues, the world of goodness and merits. The human being — likewise meaning man — is the collector of these virtues: intellect, praise, monotheism, divinization, virtue, existence over others and within oneself.

— 151

<!-- page 154 -->
That world has imprinted itself upon him, and his body has been marked by this world; and in this respect there is no differentiation between what has been brought into being before him except by rank only. May God place us among those who follow the traces of His guardians, and may He not deprive us of the intercession of His friends through their pain and their suffering.

Chapter Seven of Book Seven

The author of al-Islah said:

The human being is preferred over the world by the noble essence that is not of this world — not in the sense that the essence has been forbidden by his saying that it is "of this world." Rather, he is from that world — I mean the upper world — according to what has been revealed; and even when he is only from that world and not from this world, we say that he is from all the worlds, since he exists in the state of existing between the two worlds — the state of the child in his coming-to-be between his parents. It is not permissible to attribute (1) him to one of them to the exclusion of the other, because he is from both of them, and from both of them he came to be. From this it is permissible to attribute to this world that which has, from it, the course of matter — which is the receptive — and to attribute to that world that which has, from it, the course of form — which is what is received — as in the case of the child, who on one side has his parents (the highest, most honored form) and on the other side the one who carried that matter, the midwife. There are many proofs of this in our book known as "The Repose of the Intellect."

May God Almighty guide us to the abode of His religion, and grant us blessing through the intercession of Muhammad and the Imams of the pure house. May God's blessings be upon them.

(1) In copy (B), "the latter of them" came.
— 152

<!-- page 155 -->
Chapter Eight of the Book of al-Riyad

On the Adjudication Between the Two Disputants

(He spoke about this in the chapter on Decree and Destiny.)

Chapter One of Chapter Eight

The author of al-Islah said:

As for saying that judgment (qada) is the prior and destiny (qadar) is the subsequent — this is incorrect, because destiny is before judgment, and the prior is before the subsequent; it is not permissible that the judgment, which is what comes after destiny, be the prior to that which is the subsequent. "Judgment" and "destiny" are two words whose meanings are known from common speech: destiny is estimation, and judgment is detail. There is no detail except after estimation, and the first is before the second by the farthest distance. The proof of this is from the Book of God (peace and blessings be upon him) where He says, "The matter concerning which you consult has been decreed" — meaning, has been separated and finished; and his saying, "When you have decreed the prayer," meaning, when you have finished it. This is like a garment that the tailor estimates: he estimates it before he separates and cuts it. It may be increased or decreased, widened or narrowed; but once it is dismissed — finished — it is over, and no increase

1. In copy (A), "his protection" is given.
— 153

<!-- page 156 -->
or deficiency is possible, just like estimation and decree. This is the substance of his saying: "Judgment is the end of destiny." It is not said of the prior that it comes after destiny, nor is destiny said of the subsequent, because before judgment, destiny is estimation, and judgment is detail. So long as the thing in estimation can be increased, decreased, widened, or narrowed, after that it is not possible to increase it or decrease it.

The author of al-Nusra said:

Judgment is not detail, because the standing of the eye of detail in judgment is not less than the standing of the eye of the separated. The eye of the separated is fixed in its form by virtue of a judgment that obliges it; just as the separation itself is proven by what has been decreed for it. As for destiny, it is estimation by what he said — but there is no estimation except by a judge who obliges that estimation. As for the evidence he cited from the Book of God, that judgment is emptying-out, this is not warranted; for the wise have said: when God created the intellect, He emptied of it the [forms of the] worlds, because it is the gathering-place of the forms of the two worlds, and the forms of the worlds had not departed from the intellect — they are one thing, and that is pure emptying-out. But the author of al-Islah has erred in likening destiny and judgment to the garment that the tailor estimates: he made his estimation like destiny and his elaboration like judgment, and this analogy is not straight. He ought to have made the form of the sewing — which is in the very soul of the tailor — like judgment, because it is spiritual; and the estimation by which the tailor estimates the sewing is like destiny in respect of what it represents, and it too is a spiritual matter that has not stepped beyond the boundary of reason — this is the meaning of the form of the originals. Then the elaboration that he separates after estimation is the physical boundary that emerges from the first and the second. If the matter is as we have described, then judgment precedes destiny, just as sewing precedes estimation, and by that sewing the tailor is prepared for the estimation as he estimates. It is true, then, that judgment is the prior and destiny is the subsequent. This is the substance of his saying:

— 154

<!-- page 157 -->
"Judgment is ahead of destiny, and there is no estimation except by judgment, and the elimination of the prior — destiny — is what follows."

We say:

The interpretation of the two disputants concerning judgment and destiny, in the way each of them has invoked it — even though the meaning of the words "destiny" and "judgment" is, on the showing we have given, an impossible interpretation — in any case yields that judgment and destiny are the prior and the subsequent. This is so because the author of al-Islah committed himself to saying that destiny is estimation, and judgment is detail, and to the analogy he mentioned about the garment and the form of the sewing. By this account, destiny is what was established by potency and could be otherwise, and judgment is what was established in actuality, having gone out from the door of possibility — which is potency — into actuality. If the meaning of destiny and judgment is this, it is impossible to say that the prior and the subsequent are reversed; for neither in actuality, in some respect, nor in any other respect, is anything of the kind made to come out into act except according to what we have established from the evidence. So it proceeds.

Chapter Two of Chapter Eight

The author of al-Nusra said that there is no estimation except by a judge who obliges that estimation, and that the judgment is represented by the craft of sewing for which the destiny is destined — making it the subsequent, on his submission that the meaning of destiny is what the author of al-Islah holds it to be: the existence of an existent through potency. But it is not permissible to say this of the subsequent; for it has been established from what we said above that it is already in place. And if it were permissible, it would not be destiny in respect of the subsequent. If destiny were not for the subsequent, the elimination of the prior would be first — better that it not be — and if it is never in potency, then it goes out into act. So what his statement performs corresponds to what is on it in the prior state. And it would have been impossible to say that the decree and destiny are the prior and the subsequent. Once it is impossible, we say that their saying

— 155

<!-- page 158 -->
is correct in the way it has been explained, not according to what they have said — because judgment has things in common in its meaning, and these things are all of them from the world of the body, not from the world of creation, and likewise destiny.

Chapter Three of Book Eight

As for judgment, it indicates the speaker who was forced to it by the support of God Almighty; and likewise those who take his place — peace be upon them — who have arrived at the level parallel to the first resurrection. All of them, in their act, command, prohibition, and judgment in the already-existing call, having been in potency, are identical with the meaning that "judgment" indicates. This is what the author of al-Nusra opposes; and therefore it is valid to say it of him, and to present it to the judgment.

Chapter Four of Chapter Eight

Secondly, the elimination of what comes out of the potency of revelation, and the sacred sciences that are not yet expounded in them — they being in them by potency, awaiting exposition and act — is the act by which the souls are taken out of the bounds of potency to the bounds of act. This is what the author of al-Islah means by his interpretation of the word "judgment."

Chapter Five of Book Eight

Thirdly, the elimination of what comes out of act, and the latency of the events,

(1) Fell in copy (A).
— 156


<!-- page 159 -->
And the impossibilities estimated in the world — the things that move the moving and the stationary consonants and that admit no reversal — are what is meant when it is said of an event that occurs in the act of one who commands a matter not subject to God's decree: these are the things that must be made up for.

Section Six of Chapter Eight

As for "the one who stands" (al-qa'im), he is first of all the promised Qa'im, the one promised by Adam (peace be upon him), and by the Prophet who safeguards his call, and by the Imams and Messengers (peace be upon them) in concealment. He extends the force that, by what is destined, must come forth from potency into act and into existence. His existing call, considered from the standpoint of the limits of the Prophet's role (peace and blessings be upon him and his family) in the concealment that follows the course of the Qa'im, runs by potency, not by the manifest course actually existing — and for that reason the interpretation of Laylat al-Qadr (peace be upon him) and the references the shaykhs make to him in their sayings have come to be called "Qadr" (destiny).

Section Seven of Chapter Eight

And destiny is, secondly, what the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him and his family) brought to him from revelation: the Sharia, the ruling, and the parables — which carry the force of the sciences and the gnosis that the soul needs in its passage to maturity and to act, by being assembled there in full. For that reason, no knowledge of it stands on its surface alone; only by interpretation. The statement of the author of *al-Islah* on that point indicates this meaning of destiny.

Section Eight of Chapter Eight

And destiny is, thirdly, the world together with its parts, by virtue of which it has the capacity to issue forth into the universe<!-- - 157 -->

<!-- page 160 -->
and into act, through the passage of days, months, and years, and through the events and the cosmic affairs whose occurrence necessarily follows from it in the constant motion of the movers below it. Because it is a decree, it is said: "What God has decreed will come to pass" — for it is predestined in respect of the necessity of its existence, and if its arrival is delayed for a long stretch it is, in respect of necessity, as good as already existing. Hence God said: "They see it as far off, but We see it as near." There would be no estimation if, where it exists, it were a "judgment" (qada'). If the word "qada'" indicates that what was in potency has come forth into act, then these are the meanings of qada' and qadar.

And if you consider the words of the author of *al-Islah*, and his saying that qadar precedes qada', the proper sense of his statement is realized only with respect to what the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) brought — from the revelation and the Sharia that precede the qada' (which is the interpretation), as he himself did, and the original mercy of God upon him. And the saying of the author of *al-Nusra* that qada' precedes qadar, if charitably read, holds its meaning only with respect to the Speaker-Prophet (peace be upon him) — i.e., the already-existing one — which is the prior bringing-forth of what came down from revelation and Sharia, which is destiny.

Section Nine of Chapter Eight

The author of *al-Nusra* said:

A report from the wise scholars: when God Almighty created the intellect, He finished the worlds with it, because it is the compendium of the images of the worlds.

We say:

The intellect that holds the images of the worlds is not the first creative intellect, nor is it the second intellect — the second emanated being — but rather the intellect that rises out of the world of<!-- - 158 -->

<!-- page 161 -->
nature by acquiring works and knowledge corresponding to the first emanation, so that a second resurrection arises within it. Thereby it becomes a compendium of the images of the worlds, despite their multiplicity, and the very same images may be impressed in it in the form of all their underlying realities, as in the case of the pronounced ones — the friends of God and the sinners alike — God's prayers upon them.

The first intellect is exalted above this kind of multiplication, since its existence is creational, and creational existence multiplies only in the respect that the intellect has of its own self: that it is a cause and is caused, and that its existence is what it receives from what is outside itself, and nothing more. The remaining beings — the intellects that emanate after it — multiply according to what I have stated of their own selves and of what their existence has received from prior to them, namely the first intellect. Their existence is, all the same, only from what they have been given by what is outside themselves; they are later in existence, and they multiply not in the manner in which the first multiplies in existence, and their need to be multiplied is the need every being has, while what is separated from them — the first intellect — is fully prior in existence. Once this is granted, it becomes clear that the one that is a compendium of the images of the two worlds is not the first intellect, but rather the intellects that issue from the abode of nature, the second.

Then we say:

The first intellect does not need an intellect below it, nor does it apprehend such, because it is in its own self, from eternity, complete, enduring, and one. It refrains not because of some attainment, but for the sake of what is found. And though it is altogether eternal, still it stands one with itself, refraining out of impossibility — not for the sake of what it has attained — for it is impossible that it should reason without itself, since there is no need for that.

We also say:

The one that is later in existence — issuing from the second resurrection — needs to be reasoned by what precedes it in existence: a compendium of the images. For the completeness in him acquires its survival through that intellect — I mean, through that which precedes him in existence — and since he<!-- - 159 -->

<!-- page 162 -->
needs, for his completeness, that intellect which has already come into existence from the limits upon whom the hermeneutic call (the second worship) is established — to clarify their ranks — that intellect must teach and reason them. For if they know and reason, then the very same will be multiplied: on one side by the multiplicity of its intelligibles, and on the other united, which is the very same — and it resembles that which precedes it in existence. Our book, the one known as *Rahat al-'Aql*, contains, of the demonstrations of the legal proofs and the religious figurations for this point, what is sufficient and what heals the inquirer of his adversary. May God make us among those who follow His pleasure in what is manifest and hidden, and who follow His friends (peace be upon them) regarding it.

Section Ten of Chapter Eight

The author of *al-Islah* said:

But it is the error of those who made qada' the prior and qadar the subsequent. The wise scholars have called the subsequent by the name of qadar — which is the name of the reality — because all the essences were emanated from the prior, and that emanation was the very qadar. So the wise people named it qadar. In the ordinary speech of the people, qada' and qadar were set together — you find them in such locutions as "the decree of destiny," or "this is qada' and qadar." The meaning of qada' and qadar was hidden from them, so they made qada' the prior and qadar the subsequent. An error has thus been made on this side. And this, as we have said, is because all the essences were emanated from the prior, and that emanation was the very qadar — the compendium of the essences. Belief in this is what God Almighty meant when He said: "Indeed, We have created all things according to a determined measure." Just as all the essences were emanated from the first — which is the command of the determining power — and it was the very qadar, so from the second issued forth the act of bestowal and qadar.

It contains changes and alterations, and this is the book of which God Almighty said: "God effaces what He wills and confirms." Qadar is the subsequent, as we have said, and qada' is what has been carried out, because<!-- - 160 -->

<!-- page 163 -->
the subsequent is the realization-into-actuality of what he stated in this chapter:

Qadar is the name of the subsequent, because all the essences were emanated from the prior pre-determined, as God Almighty says: "Indeed, We have created all things according to a determined measure."

The author of *al-Islah* said:

As for what he adduced concerning qada' and qadar — God's saying, may He be exalted, "Indeed, We have created all things according to a determined measure" — he has erred in the interpretation of this verse and in its exegesis as compared with what we explain and clarify, because it is purely innocent of his reading.

We say:

The meaning of the "I" by which He intends a Self not tainted by any attribute or qualification — and "every thing," by which the intellect (which is all things) is intended, and "We created it" intends an image by which the two worlds came to be among them — and "by a determined measure," meaning that by which the matter is established, by which all spiritual and physical images appeared among them in the witnessed realm. This is the pen, the tablet, the writing, and the writer. The writer is the aspect of "I," and He is pure eternity. "And the pen" is the pen of the intellect, which is the pen of the pen, "and We created it" in the form of the tablet, and "the pen" stands over against the letters that appear on the tablet. The measure is that by which the tablet, which receives all the forms of letters, holds all physical and spiritual things — the simple and the dense — under these intelligible letters that appear from the writer through the pen upon the tablet. In the same way, all the upper and lower images were made manifest by God Almighty by means of intellect and soul; He did not single out anything from among them, just as nothing of the ordered letters that actually appear from the writer upon the tablet is singled out. This is the position of the author of *al-Nusra* in the rebuttal.

We say:

The Sinai-men have mentioned in this chapter what cannot be undone — nothing more is in *al-Islah* beyond this, and nothing more in the rebuttal either, because the author of *al-Islah* did not make it obligatory that qadar<!-- 1 — In version (B), "*al-Nasira* came." -->
<!-- The Book of Riyadh [11] -->
<!-- - 161 -->

<!-- page 164 -->
be the name of the subsequent, and he supported that by saying that God Almighty's "Indeed, We have created all things according to a determined measure" depends on the fact that qadar is precisely what has been brought forth in the speech of the prior — and so it is not permissible to say it of the subsequent. The author of *al-Nusra*, opposing him, made his counter-saying:

The author of *al-Islah* erred in what he said about the verse, and his reading is neither comparative nor congruent.

This is because he said:

God Almighty, by "I," intends pure religion — the Self not tainted by any attribute — and by it He does not intend anything in which there is an attribute. The word "I" stands only to the extent that the prophet derives, by establishing his standing in the world, not pure atheism; and how could "I" fall upon pure atheism, when between Him and the world is the middle term — no, rather, she is upon the medium that is near to the Messenger of God (peace and blessings be upon him) in the commentary upon him? The meaning is the prophet, in that he is the one who carries this out.

Section Eleven of Chapter Eight

He said:

"Every thing" is what the intellect — which is all things — intends. So "every thing" in this place is not the intellect; we have shown that the first intellect does not multiply in the way that what derives its existence from it multiplies. Rather, "every thing" is what the community needs of the two worships that conjoin: outwardly the sciences are gained by works, and inwardly by the perception that leads to salvation from the torment of the worlds.

Section Twelve of Chapter Eight

He said:

"He created us" — and he does not intend by it what he went toward, because "He created us" means that we predetermined Him: I mean, we made the formulation of revelation and the Sharia a formulation in His potency that contains every science<!-- 1 — Dropped in copy (A). -->
<!-- - 162 -->

<!-- page 165 -->
and every religious act the community needs to ward off the pain of the soul in this world and the Hereafter, as though He said, on God's behalf, may He be glorified and exalted: "Indeed, We have created" — meaning, "to the extent of" — "the matter of the two acts of worship in knowledge and in deed" that the community needs in its religion and worldly life — "We have measured in it," that is, brought forth from revelation and Sharia in measure: meaning, We have placed it in His potency so that it may come forth, by interpretation, into what it deserves.

Section Thirteen of Chapter Eight

The author of *al-Islah* said:

Qada' is the subsequent act. It has been established on the one side that what passes into act passes out of the natural world by its abundance, and is performed by it; and on its side it attains its completeness. Therefore qada' became the very Speaker-Prophet, because he passed into act and gained perfection through its abundance and support.

Section Fourteen of Chapter Eight

He said:

That all the essences were emanated from the prior — there is a contradiction here, because the subsequent is the very essence emanating from the prior; and not "by predestiny." What is meant by his saying is rather the emanation by which the physical world was destined to receive something of it according to what we have explained in the chapter on destiny, not the subsequent.

Section Eighteen of Chapter Eight

The author of *al-Nusra* said:

In a chapter wherein "We created Him" — He intends the image of the two worlds among them — and "by a determined measure," whereby<!-- 1 — In copy (A), the sentence is omitted entirely. -->
<!-- 2 — Dropped in copy (A). -->
<!-- 3 — It dropped in copy (A). -->
<!-- - 163 -->

<!-- page 166 -->
He intends the subsequent through which the matter is established, all the spiritual and corporeal images appeared from among them. For if it were the case that the existence of things were corporeal, my body would have been and would not have been, in keeping with what he mentioned of the corruption in the foundation on which his words rest. Were it not that the discussion of what the existence of the foundations entails upon the first lies outside the bounds of writing, we would bring it forth — but it shall not be set down in the Qur'an, God willing — and we have written.

Section Sixteen of Chapter Eight

The author of *al-Islah* said:

As for the interpretation of the words of the Messenger of God, "Flee from God's decree to His destiny" — rather, he meant: flee from the subsequent of the prior endorsement, to the subsequent statement, as a man flees from the fire, which is the proof of the endorsement, to the water, which is the proof. According to the statement, he meant: flee from the thing that has been sealed and decreed, lest it be made ready to drive him into the pot he has not yet consumed — that is, while he is uncomposed. The Messenger of God (peace and blessings be upon him) passed from God's decree to His destiny because of a cause, and the cause was the leaning wall against whose falling he warned, walking faster past it, and he said: he meant to strike for them a parable, and the wall —

— inclined, is a building that has tended to fall, and it is at the boundary they may have something of recanting and hypocrisy, and it has tilted toward debauchery away from obedience. For if a man recants, he falls from his standing, as God says: "And they found in it a wall about to collapse, and he set it up" — I mean, one of the limits was concerned with hypocrisy and rebuttal, so he set it up, until it was made firm and did not topple, nor did it fall, just as did the leaning wall past which the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) walked quickly. He had known a limit that he felt<!-- 1 — It dropped in copy (A). -->
<!-- - 164 -->


<!-- page 167 -->
hypocrisy, and he had tended toward repudiating it — so the Prophet "set it up" (that is, walked past it briskly), because brisk walking, in this context, signifies the statement, the proof, the urgency, and the haste in moving along the path. He used to address those given to hypocrisy with preaching and warning — addressing them outwardly without the inward statement (decree on the outward side, as we have explained, and elimination on the inward side), so that his flight was from Allah's decree to His destiny.

This wall takes care not to fall — careful of itself, not fleeing from the dignity with which Allah Almighty has honored it; that dignity is the degree to which Allah has elevated it.

The author of al-Nusrah said:
"It is, to multiply, to be careful."

It is not appropriate to interpret "fleeing from Allah's decree to His destiny" in the way that the author of al-Islah imagined — namely, that he (3) was fleeing from the next rank back to the previous rank, because the prophets, peace be upon them, never claim that they are seeking the heights, and asking for the heights is not asking for [that].

Rather, we would say, with the help of Allah Almighty and His blessings, that the meaning here unfolds in three senses, and each of these proves that "the elimination of the previous and the predestined for the next" — that is, the first sense, which is the greatest and most precise of them in meaning — is that what is intended is his flight from Allah's decree to His destiny: that the "previous" is the support (al-sabq) and the "next" is the composition (al-tarkib), and that the speaker (al-natiq) is the author of the Shari'ah. This is the foundational interpretation in every case: the interpretation in which the previous (the supporting principle) is more like an endorsement, and the composition is more like a synthesis. So the Speaker (peace be upon him), in saying this, indicated that he was a likeness, in the composition of the laws, to the structure of the world. He is not the previous itself — he fled from the decree of Allah, which is the previous, and he did not [stand fixed in it].

(1) In copy (b) it appears with "the treachery."
(2) The sentence is omitted in its entirety in copy (A).
(3) Dropped in copy (A).
(4) In copy (b) it appears, and I have corrected it.
— 165

<!-- page 168 -->
He likens it from the aspect that the creation of the support is its destiny — that is, its interpretation. He likened himself to it.

The second meaning is the medium of his community by which he benefits from the first by means of the second — so he was, in everything he wanted to ground in the first, doing so by means of the second, as though he said, "Return — to all that I want you to understand." This is the second meaning, the link between me and the intellect.

The third meaning, which is the most honorable, is the one the author of the book has mentioned, peace be upon him: it is his flight from the intensity of the light of the support of the previous, to the support of the next, just as the Grandfather (al-Jadd) was overwhelmed by the spiritual power of the previous and could not bear it until his very identity nearly faded — and this is what he said in the story of Moses (peace be upon him).

We say:
As for the author of al-Islah, he moved in the interpretation of the verse in a way that treats the matter as concerning decree and destiny, and that is good — although, even if he had not said so, that would not have been [the issue].
