The Prophet (peace be upon him) is a prophet who responds to the unseen from the universes (2) and from events — among the pronouns of what Allah Almighty had singled out for him from the Revelation. He wanted to clarify how he used to open to him what was being thrust upon him in earnest. So he said: "Go back," and he said, "Flee from me." In earnest, I cross the limits of Allah Almighty for a number of events that are almost — and the universes (al-akwan), which are the judgment of Allah, which inevitably goes out into action by His destiny: that is, Allah Almighty's creation of the souls and the horizons and their movements, by which what is in His power becomes manifest, in order to open to me what was between us in the door of revelation. And therefore Allah says: "We will show them Our signs in the horizons and in their own selves until it becomes clear to them that it is the truth" (Quran 41:53). Those who contemplate the creation of the heavens and the earth say: "Our Lord, You have not created this in vain" (Quran 3:191). This does not indicate that his existence precedes and that his existence is delayed from the universes.

(1) The sentence is omitted in its entirety in copy (A).
(2) Dropped in copy (A).
(3) The sentence is omitted in its entirety in copy (A).
— 166

<!-- page 169 -->
Rather, he signifies it and pronounces it, as though he said, "Go back to me — for he will be thrust at me in earnest." Destiny (al-qadar) is seen — or opened — through its representation and his perception of me, and that is what He means by saying, "Flee from Allah's decree to His destiny." This is a kind of revelation, and the book *Rahat al-'Aql* contains an explanation of that.

**Chapter Seventeen of Title Eight**

As for the author of al-Nusrah, his behavior in the response he adopted is itself the proof against the author of al-Islah; for he did not mention anything that would pounce upon the author of al-Islah and overturn the words upon which he built his case, except the meanings he himself enumerated. He counted them first, and he mentioned that each of them proves the elimination of the previous and the determination of fate for the next.

But this is not because the speaker is similar in his composition to the Shari'ah, as he has said — namely, in order for him to return, in everything he wanted (1), to realize the following, as he has proved. Rather, what is incumbent upon him is to prove that the elimination of the previous and the destiny of the next is the case; and there is no occasion between "the elimination of the previous" and the meanings he has expressed, even if the meaning of decree and destiny is identical with the case of the first and the second. They correspond to each other on one side, so it may be said that the judiciary and the decree apply to them from that side. But the judiciary means what has been said above, and so does destiny — and it is not worth saying that "the first and the second" are two of the things that are obligatory in their meaning. Whoever says that of them is as one who says about honey, which is naturally sweet, that it is patience that is naturally bitter.

**Chapter Eighteen of Title Eight**

As for his analogy in the second meaning of the Speaker (al-natiq), it does not correspond to what

(1) Dropped in copy (b).
— 167

<!-- page 170 -->
the law of the guiding call requires — because the Speaker is in the world of bodies, and the world of religion, the place of the previous one, and the place of the guardian (al-wasi) in them is the place of the next, in the world of creativity. So the Speaker is two analogies: one for the previous and the next, and another for the sun and the moon.

**Chapter Nineteen of Title Eight**

As for the third meaning, which he affirmatively mentioned — namely, that the Speaker fled from the intensity of the light of the previous support to the support of the next — this is contrary to duty and to interpretation, in the case of the Grandfather (al-Jadd) that he cited. It requires investigation, and the words of the author of al-Islah on this chapter are stronger.

**Chapter Twenty of Title Eight**

The author of al-Islah (1) said, quoting from the verse in the story of Moses (peace be upon him):

> "My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look upon You." He said, "You will not see Me, but look at the mountain; if it remains in its place, then you will see Me. And when his Lord appeared to the mountain, He made it level."
> (Quran 7:143.)

He learned that he would not attain it, and He commanded him to limit himself to what was being conveyed to him in earnest. He learned that the one who asked Him could not be like Him in His subtlety — how could he attain what he could not see?

He says:
"If it remains in its place, then you will see Me — and when his Lord appeared to the mountain, He made it level, and Moses fell unconscious." When he learned that he had erred in asking for what he could not receive, he prayed to Me.

(1) Dropped in copy (A).
(2) The sentence is completely omitted in copy (1).
— 168–0

<!-- page 171 -->
When his Lord roused him, he said, "Glory be to You; I repent to You."

The author of al-Nusrah said:
"'O Lord, show me that I may look upon You.' He said, 'You will not see Me' — meaning, you cannot bear it, and you cannot endure that rank. But look at the mountain — and it is the Grandfather (al-Jadd), with his closeness to Me — and remain in his rank that I have given him; he bears what I have not shown you. If you had borne that, you would have seen Me. But the Grandfather did not bear that, because it was manifested to him and his identity was almost [obliterated]." This is what he said.

We say:
The interpretation of this verse rests on what each of these two men used to say. Moses (peace be upon him): he did not consider him to be among the highest of the prophets, and he reserves the upper ranks. Indeed, they removed him from the company of the scholars, and counted him among the ignorant, because he asked for what he could not perceive — for what was above him among the ranks. How is this, given the matter going on through him?

The interpretation of the verse is different from what he said, because it was not addressed to Moses (peace be upon him) [as one] standing among the upper ranks. Rather, He was addressing him at the rank he was already at, when he had not yet reached the rank of pronouncement. He asked the author of the Command:

[He asked] before his time had come, because he lived in a time of concealment, and the people of truth were under affliction, under the covering, and under secrecy from the side of opponents and violators. Moses (peace be upon him) saw in himself a power that, he reckoned, he could use in his time — if he were ordered to lift that ordeal from the people of truth, take revenge on the enemies of God, govern the community by the policy of truth, just as the master of peace (i.e., the prophet of his time) appraised by the power he found in himself what he appraised and then repented of it. When he learned that he had erred, the sun returned to him and answered

what we have explained in our letters as interpretation. So Moses (peace be upon him) asked the author of the Command, who is Shu'ayb — when he came to him and Shu'ayb was his Lord (i.e., his master and guide), after he had explained his command to him in the matter — finding in himself the strength and the ability for politics, when he said: "O my Lord, show me that I may look upon You" — meaning, command me, and I will manage this matter and lift the grief.

— 169

<!-- page 172 -->
"You will not see Me" means: you will not see Me do any of that, nor any of those in this time, [for] everything has its time and season, and this time is not the time to take from these things the means by which they pursue their whims; nor is it the time to bring things back down (1) under the laws of politics. What we say to you cannot be done. And Shu'ayb (peace be upon him) replied to him, alerting him to his error in his request in the gentlest of ways, and teaching him that what he asked for is difficult, and that in his time it cannot be done — even by one who has power, help, and the apparatus. How then could it be on the side of a great man in the call, great in worldly leadership, possessed of help and wealth and men and kingdom?

And he said to Moses (peace be upon him): "I have learned that this matter is not done in this time, and that it is difficult and cannot be undertaken, lest I be regarded as miserly toward you, or as preferring to delay you from your station — but look at the mountain," meaning, this man who has worldly power, equipment, and capacity. "If it remains in its place, then you will see Me," that is, if he is fixed in what he has been commanded, and is enabled, with his power and help, to extend the rulings of the politics of truth — then it will be said to him, "You will see Me do what you have asked of them." That is, when he commanded him to raise up a beacon of truth, He made it level — meaning, the matter narrowed for the people; they did not accept the command, they split the staff, the matter became confused and disturbed, and his leadership was about to disappear when the people went out against him. Moses (peace be upon him) then learned that what he had predicted would be accomplished at that time was instead destined to be impossible; and the author of the Command, who is his Lord, knew the matter better than him. So he was startled by that warning, and he said, "Glory be to Almighty Allah; I repent to You" — and he retracted his statement, regretted, and bore patiently with what the author of al-Nusrah had ordered him to do.

If that period in which he had hired him passed, then the time would come and something of him would be right — and he said, "You will see Me deliver the matter and do what you want." This is an interpretation

(1) Dropped in copy (A).
(2) The term is given in copy (1).
— 170

<!-- page 173 -->
of the verse that is not [merely] what is approximated by the one who proposes and elevates, and it does not correspond [to the matter].

**Chapter Twenty-One of Title Eight**

The author of al-Nusrah said:
"As for his appeal to the verse, 'And He created everything and determined it with precise determination' (Quran 25:2) — this is the clearest evidence that destiny is the next, and that decree is the moral aspect bearing upon the previous. The meaning of His saying, 'and He created everything,' is what the previous wanted to do — because the intellect is all things, and all things are the intellect."

We say:
The words "decree" and "destiny" have already been considered. We have shown, in dealing with them, that decree and destiny may not be said of the previous (1) and the next; and what is intended by the Almighty's saying, "And He created everything that He lost in estimation" is not what the author of al-Nusrah went to. We have shown the corruption of that, and that it is not worthy of the previous, and therefore [neither] of the next, to be called decree and destiny.

Rather, what is meant by "He created everything" is that He created everything, because He made everything, with regard to the matter of religion and its interests — outwardly or inwardly, in the power of revelation and the Shari'ah — and He estimated it in the estimation of what the revelation and the Shari'ah have to do with the sciences: all of them, which relate to religion and its interests. They reside within revelation and Shari'ah by power, and by interpretation that brings them out into actuality.

**Chapter Twenty-Two of Title Eight**

As for his claim that the intellect is all things and that all things are the intellect — that is a matter that is not correct. The physical world, with everything it gathers, is not an intellect; it is a thing. The best thing that can be attributed to it, regarding the meaning of saying "the intellect is all things and all things

(1) Dropped in copy (b).
— 171


It is the intellect — because the intellect, which is itself the command of Allah, is the cause of the existence of things. All of them, physical and non-physical, exist for the sake of the intellect and its purpose.

Section Twenty-Three of Chapter Eight

The author of al-Nusra said:

Allah has permitted the prophets, in the matter of the laws, to abandon their use, in His saying:

"Those who believe and do good deeds will not be blamed for what they have eaten, so long as they fear Allah and believe and do good deeds — then fear and believe — then fear and do good. And Allah loves those who do good." (the verse)

We say:

The verse contains no mention of permission for the prophets to abandon the use of the laws. I do not know how this point escaped him until he released this statement without investigating it. The interpretation of this verse is other than what he has said.

Allah Almighty knew that souls do not attain happiness, nor reach the degree of perfection, nor cross from the limit of potentiality to the limit of actuality, except through work and knowledge — those two acts of worship which cost every human being whatever is required so that he may attain happiness in the two abodes. If souls obtain their perfection and happiness only through these two acts of worship, then any soul that abandons both of them, or even one of them, is far from attaining perfection. This obligation remains binding upon them so long as the person is alive; and so long as he is alive he stands before it, until the time of his transfer — either while it is intact and he passes into Paradise, or, by leaving both or one of them, he is in the Fire, until Allah forgives him, as we have explained in our books.

What has been laid out above is that the only work of souls is the reformation of morals — which is the greatest jihad, as the Messenger of Allah ﷺ said — together with the acquisition of virtues by way of spiritual exercise and knowledge, in proportion to the soul's existence above the body, as the call requires. If the condition of souls in worship is on this analogy, then how can it be permissible to license oneself, or to license the Speaker, or anyone below them in rank, to abandon the work and the goodness of soul that are completed by them and lacking without them? No — the verse has an interpretation other than the first, and it has a specific application to those who summon the preachers to the Oneness of Allah Almighty and to the knowledge of His limits from the side of the Imams (peace be upon them).

The Speaker (peace be upon him), when he knew that his community would differ after him, and that each group would isolate itself in a doctrine framed by their own opinions — by their imperfect minds and their preference of themselves over the community whose guidance lies in the prayers of Allah upon those for whom Allah has not made a [special] light — and when he knew that the preachers would suffer at the hands of the various communities all the severity of their opposition in harming them and in challenging their doctrine: in response to this, our loyal Imams (peace and blessings of Allah be upon them) have licensed the preachers of the call to consider the sciences that are not of their own belief, on conditions. He said:

"Those who believe and do good deeds will not be blamed for what they study, so long as they fear Allah and believe and do good deeds."

That is, those who call to the Oneness of Allah and who know His limits from the side of the trusted Imams (peace be upon them) — those who believe in the respondents when they hold to the rope of Allah and grasp the wilayah of the Imams away from doubts and uncertainties about the religion of Allah, and so reach a degree of perfection and connection — will not be blamed in what they study, meaning in what they read of the sciences that are not their belief, so that those sciences become like a weapon by which they fight opposing positions in establishing the truth, provided they fear Allah and believe and do good deeds. That is one condition: that their path remains straight in obeying Allah Almighty by following His Messenger and believing in his limits, and in establishing both acts of worship in their entirety as he commanded.

"And fear and believe" — this is a second condition, meaning: obey the Messenger in the matter of his commandment and in the one whom he places in his stead, and call to him outwardly and inwardly.

"Then fear and do good" — this is a third condition, meaning: obey the Imam in the matter of the one whom he establishes and assigns, and do good in calling to the Oneness of Allah through his leadership.

When they have done this and observed these conditions, no blame falls upon them in turning to the doctrines and sciences belonging to other peoples; there is no constraint upon them in those religions, [for these] aid them in what they need to resist the enemies, and they aid whoever wishes to be reassured concerning the limits. This is the interpretation of the verse, and it contains no permission to abandon the use of the Shari'ah. Rather, it is a license to consider the [outside] sciences on the aforementioned conditions.

The summary of the matter: every interpretation that leads to declaring permissible what Allah has forbidden, or forbidding what He has permitted, or altering the ranks of the limits of Allah Almighty, and to disregarding the Shari'ah and the rites of the Hanifi religion — calling to the abandonment of work in it and to the acquisition of happiness through refusing to do so — is a corrupt interpretation. Our loyal Imams (peace of Allah Almighty be upon them) are pure of what he has said, and of every interpretation contradicting what the Prophet ﷺ and his companions said.

Section Twenty-Four of Chapter Eight

The author of al-Nusra said:

Judgment (al-qada) is the beginning, estimation (al-qadar) is the middle, and the end is the end — because estimation can only proceed from the judiciary.

Then he said:

If the judiciary is the end, it cannot rightly be called "judiciary," because the judiciary is what comes before everything, and things follow after the judgment. Judgment is for something, and perfection is not for anything except for the very thing made perfect.

We say:

What has already been said earlier, in the answer concerning fate and destiny, is sufficient to grasp the discourse on this matter; and there is no need to prolong it with anything that might be attributed to us beyond what we intended.

Chapter Nine of the Book of al-Riyad

On Ruling Between the Two
(He spoke about this in the chapter on the Shari'ah of Adam and the guardianship of Nuh, peace be upon him.)

Section One

The author of al-Islah said:

Whoever claims that the first of the Speakers (peace be upon them) had no Shari'ah is in error. He has also erred in counting him among the determined ones (al-muqarrarun), as we have explained, because determination is only by abrogating a Shari'ah or by renewing it; and the first Speaker had no Shari'ah before him to abrogate, so the name of "determined one" does not apply to him by what we have presented. He was the first to draw out the laws of the religion — of command and prohibition, and of the obedience by which one benefits — in the manner that the rest of the Speakers drew out their apparent laws, which are inferred from the subtleties deposited within them in the meanings of the parables. That [process of inference] is what is called interpretation: the manifestation of the words of the wise. Every drawing-out of law pronounced by them (peace be upon them) — every command, prohibition, and obligation of servitude — is a Shari'ah, whether small or great, whether of few rulings or many. Each Speaker has a body of laws in proportion to what he was commanded with, and he does not exceed it. Some of them brought many rulings; some were limited to a few, restricted to the laws of their wording — as we see fasting in the Shari'ah of Musa (peace be upon him) being less than what is in the Shari'ah of 'Isa (peace be upon him); or as in the Shari'ah of 'Isa (peace be upon him), where there is more than what is in the Shari'ah of Muhammad ﷺ. So too the other rulings in the religion decrease according to the difference of their cycles and the measure of what they were commanded with. (1)

No one should think that this great cycle, which was the longest of these cycles, passed without work, and that its people went without benefit — that there were no apparent laws by which they were drawn to its rulings. For if there had been no work, no benefit, no laws, no interpretation, no inner meaning, and no genuine call, then nothing would stand — because the call is built upon the Shari'ah, the interpretation depends on revelation, the inner depends on the outer, and the meaning depends on the parable. If there is no outer, then there is no inner, no call, and no Shari'ah; creation is neglected, and people are left ignorant — because knowledge is paired with work, and that is the root of wisdom among the wise, as they said: "Wisdom is action with knowledge." It is impossible to say that the people of that cycle were in such a state.

Rather, we say:

The first pronouncement requires the name of "Speaker," meaning the one who composes the Shari'ah; otherwise he would not have been counted among the six pronouncements, even granting that it is necessary for him to establish a foundation that calls to the unification of Allah Almighty. If he had no Shari'ah and no call, there would be no laws for those belonging to his cycle to live by, so that those after him might frame their additions upon the framework of the rest of the pronouncements. That is a sick belief. It is not permissible to say that there was no revelation in that cycle and no Shari'ah, because the command and prohibition are inspired by Allah, and the inspiration of Allah's command is itself revelation; and once command, prohibition, and revelation are proven, then the Shari'ah is proven by the manifestations of the laws — which itself indicates that the first Speaker had a Shari'ah, in addition to what indicates the necessary proof, as the wise men have said.

Allah said:

"And recite to them the news of the two sons of Adam in truth, when they offered a sacrifice, and it was accepted from one of them and not from the other." (the verse)

The offering (al-qurban) is one of the laws, and it is the most firmly fixed of the foundations of the Shari'ah. Allah has commanded it in every Shari'ah, and He has made it the root of asceticism and purity. This is why the sacrifice is called a "rite" (mansak), as Allah says:

"And for every nation We have appointed a rite, that they may mention the name of Allah over what He has provided them of the beasts of grazing livestock." (the verse)

"We have appointed a sanctuary, and they are its sanctuary-keepers, so let them not dispute with you" (the verse) — meaning the place to which the sacrifice is brought. In the hadith, "the lame woman drags her leg to the sanctuary" — and the sanctuary itself is the offering. A man was called a "monk" (rahib) when he was known by religion, piety, and purity; and the offering is called "asceticism" because it is the purpose of the duties of religion, for the purpose of closeness to Allah is in every Shari'ah and in all the nations, and among the foundations of the Shari'ah. It is called an "offering" (qurban) because by it they draw close (yataqarrabun) to Allah. By being close to Allah Almighty, it is the first cause of obedience and disobedience, of disbelief and faith, of command and prohibition; and it is the first of the laws of the Shari'ah.

The author of al-Nusra said:

The author of al-Islah argued for the Shari'ah of Adam (peace be upon him) by Allah Almighty's saying: "And recite to them the news of the two sons of Adam in truth, when they offered a sacrifice." But the sacrifice is only one of the laws after the distinction is drawn between determination and Shari'ah, and the most this evidences is no greater than the poetry of the Arabs — a saying with no benefit lying beneath it, and no argument here to confirm what he wished to prove. The interpretation of this verse is not as he supposed. For if the sacrifice in this place referred to nothing other than what appears in the apparent laws — the slaughtering of sheep and camels — then it would have been as he mentioned. But the command of sacrifice in this subject refers to something not found in the apparent laws: the establishing of arguments through those beneath their hands, and the establishing of who will take their place if they depart from the world. This is a story whose apparent meaning is to be interpreted by the wise, even if he reads the story to its end. For Adam (peace be upon him) had no Shari'ah, because none of his sons acted well [in this matter], until Allah sent a raven to scratch the earth, to show him how to bury his brother's corpse. If hiding the dead and burying them had been among the laws, and Adam had a Shari'ah, he would not have hidden the corpse — he would have been obliged to place it in its [prescribed] place, or to act upon it [according to the law]. This is what he said.

We say:

The Shari'ah of Adam (peace be upon him) is proven by what the author of al-Islah argued for, and what the author of al-Nusra mentioned does not undermine it. For the author of al-Islah argued for proving the Shari'ah — which is one of the duties of "speaking" (al-nutq) in the worship of Allah Almighty — by way of knowing the foundations from the apparent meaning of the Book in the verse in which the sacrifice is mentioned, together with the verse that is universally affirmative in the Almighty's saying: "And for every nation We have appointed a rite, that they may mention the name of Allah," and the other verse: "And for every nation We have appointed a sanctuary; they are its sanctuary-keepers." So it is established that the matter is not as the author of al-Nusra opined, and the interpretation of this verse is not as he supposed; nor does it contradict (2) the statement of the author of al-Islah — for the author of al-Islah did not argue for proving the Shari'ah only by the interpretation of this verse, but also by its apparent meaning. So if the statement of the author of al-Islah is confirmed regarding the Shari'ah of Adam (peace be upon him), then the saying of the author of al-Nusra in response does not contradict it: the Shari'ah is fixed as he has established it.

Section Two of Chapter Nine

The author of al-Islah said:

If the offering is not among the laws apparent from

---
(1) In version (b), "their differences are mentioned in their cycles."
(2) In version (b): "of al-Nusra."


<!-- page 182 -->
the slaughter of sheep and camels, then the matter would stand as he described it. But the command of offering in this passage is not within the laws in the way he has interpreted it, and his interpretation does not contradict the statement of the author of al-Islah. On the contrary, what the author of al-Islah obligated remains obligatory, since nothing in his argument went beyond what the manifest sense of the verse establishes — namely, the affirmation of the offering, which is one of the evident duties of religion and Sharia.

He had argued that what was intended could not be the apparent meaning, since (he said) the apparent reading is forced. We answer: the offering in this passage is not contrary to the manifest sense of the Sharia, and so his argument fails. What the author of al-Nusra has said proceeds along the path of demonstrative claim, which can only be established by evidence. It is permissible that the apparent meaning hold true at the same time that the inner meaning holds true — especially since this verse does not belong to the kind of parabolic discourse that calls for interpretation and the setting aside of its apparent sense. If that is so, then everything apparent in the verse stands, and the law of Adam (peace be upon him) is thereby established.

Chapter Three of Title IX

The author of al-Islah then said: The purpose of the sacrifice is the establishing of arguments by those whose hands rule, and by those who stand in their place. Adam must have had something to summon them to, if arguments were to be established and someone were to take his place. The boundaries are set up in order to call to what the Speaker proclaims and to mark out what lies within them — namely, the welfare of those who are summoned to Him in this world and the next, in religion and in worldly affairs. Adam, peace be upon him, must therefore have prescribed a Sharia and set down the ordinances that act as judge. He did not prescribe nothing, nor did he leave unmanifest any of the laws of worship, whether small or large. He needed to establish arguments and to dispatch preachers from among those who take his place, lest something be lost that needs preserving and that the community is supported by. With the affirmation of the author of al-Nusra, by way of interpretation, that Adam established arguments, the Sharia and its ordinances become obligatory — for the Sharia belongs to Adam, peace be upon him.

<!-- page 183 -->
Chapter Four of Title IX

Then we say: the need for the prophets, peace be upon them, was for the education of the community, so that it might attain happiness in the Hereafter and in this world, and be guided to its interests in both. Adam, peace be upon him, was a prophet; he must therefore have been a teacher and a guide. If he had to be a teacher and guide, he had to bring what would teach his community what makes them happy and what guides them to their interests. If such teaching had to exist, it could only be the laws of worship — which is the Sharia. Therefore the Sharia belongs to Adam, peace be upon him, immutably. This is borne out by the saying of Allah (interpretation of the meaning): "Mankind was one community, then Allah sent the prophets as bearers of good tidings and as warners, and sent down with them the Book in truth to judge between people in their differences." If he was a prophet, then Allah sent him only to preach, to warn, and to judge with good news and warning; and judgment is only effected through ordinances and obligations, which is the Sharia. So it is established.

Chapter Five of Title IX

The author of al-Nusra said: Had the author of al-Islah read this passage to its end, he would have said that Adam did not belong to such a station, because his son did not know how to deal with his brother and did not know until Allah sent a raven scratching the earth to show him how he should bury his brother. If burying the dead were among the laws — even if Adam had been endowed with Sharia — it would still be law; and if Adam had such a Sharia, he would have transmitted it to his sons. So this statement is one that issues from someone other than a careful searcher or examiner.

<!-- page 184 -->
We say: the author of al-Islah takes up a fallacy — the same kind of fallacy that the author of al-Nusra employed in the chapter on the verse he invoked concerning the meaning of the sacrifice. There, the interpretation of the verse, as he thought, was not free of supporting the author of al-Nusra; or else the verse he invoked in support of denying the Sharia of Adam, peace be upon him, must be taken on a single interpretation alone. In that, he is the one invoking it, and his argument is invalidated in trying to deny what he intends to deny of the Sharia of Adam, peace be upon him — since, on the face of it, his appeal first turns the verse into an interpretation, and only then runs it according to what its apparent meaning would imply, with the result that nothing of what he wants is indicated by it.

The verse offers no indication that the son of Adam did not carve open the carcass of a crow in the ground when he saw it, and saw his brother, and buried him — so that the argument was actually established before him. The verse reads: "Then Allah sent a raven scratching the ground, to show him how to hide the shame of his brother. He said: Woe to me! Am I unable to be like this raven and hide the shame of my brother? — and he became one of the regretful." All that is mentioned is that he saw the raven; there is no evidence that he had not already buried his brother.

Chapter Six of Title IX

The interpretation of the verse and its content likewise does not indicate anything of what he claimed. Rather, this is the situation: both the adversary and the wali believed that in the worship of Allah Almighty there is something one offers as sacrifice, through which one draws near to Allah Almighty. But the adversary held that worship is nothing other than what is apparent — only outward works — and that salvation lies in that alone, and is attained by such belief and by sincerity in it toward the one who stands in the place of the Prophet (peace). The wali, however, believed that the worship of Allah Almighty consists in both: in the outward, which is works, and in the inward, which is knowledge — and that salvation is in both together. The one who stands in the place of the Prophet (peace), from the position of the Messenger (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him and his family), is established on that, and they disputed about it.

<!-- page 185 -->
One day they argued before the Prophet, each of them explaining his belief about how one ought to worship. This is the meaning of His saying: "When they offered a sacrifice" — that is, each of them brought forward what he believed would draw him near to Allah Almighty, so that the Messenger of Allah might accept from one of them. The one accepted was the wali — that is, the Speaker who stands in the place of God was satisfied with him, since he believed in God and in His worship rightly; and the other was not accepted — that is, the Speaker did not accept the adversary's belief, since it was on a different footing. The Speaker satisfied himself with what he accepted from the wali's belief, but he saw fit to make outwardly apparent that he was displeased with the adversary, lest people impute partiality to the wali. He said: "If I had favored you with God, I would have feared Him" — like the Prophet (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him) saying to Abu Bakr, "May He be feared who was able to hear it from him." The Prophet recited the verse he recited only to confirm the wali's belief and the rightness of his path — that this wali was the one who would stand in his place after him. He stood in that station while in the heart of the adversary lay the conviction that he had been overcome by the wali. So the adversary said to him: "I will kill you, to drive you back from this belief which he did not accept from you, and from the station."

The wali said to him: "Allah accepts only from those who fear Him" — meaning that the Guardian of God, who is the Messenger, does not appoint just anyone in his place, and is not satisfied with the belief of just anyone except those who fear Him, who combine knowledge and action, and who draw near to Allah Almighty by establishing all of its dimensions. The adversary thus deliberately set himself in opposition and struck out at the wali, knowing nothing — so that they appealed to the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding their belief, and the Prophet was satisfied with the wali's belief. The wali said: "If you stretch out your hand to me to kill me, I will not stretch out my hand to you to kill you; I fear Allah, Lord of the worlds. You laid hold of me and let loose your tongue against me and turned aside from my obligation, refusing to be satisfied with what I believe concerning the worship of Allah, Lord of the worlds. I will not do as you do, nor will I oppose you — for I am under the command of the Speaker who stands in the place of God, who is the master of the Cycle, the Lord of the Imams; I am under his obedience, and I fear Him, and I will not

<!-- page 186 -->
do what you ask of me." Hearing this from the wali, the adversary abandoned what he had been holding to and obeyed his own self-will, and killed his brother. His soul rose up against the other, and he ran against him.

The other adversary — the one to whom things did not settle, were not held in check, and whose counsel was never sought except with him — that is to say, when the first adversary returned with what had happened to the second adversary, he told him that one should believe in God and in His worship in no other way than what I have said. The meaning of what he said is that the wali is keen-witted, and that what is best for him in the wali's situation is to be cut off from his rank — claiming that the wali does not do anything right. So he pushed the wali out of his rank, overcame him, and said: "Worship is nothing but this outward form, neither what he says nor what he claims." So he became one of the losers when he worshipped Allah by obeying the Speaker without obeying the Foundation, and by the outward without the inward.

"Then Allah sent a raven to scratch the earth" — that is, when time grew long and the moment came to move against the adversary, the wali wished to make the argument prevail over him, so that he might be separated from the world after due reminder. So He sent to him — which is His saying — "and Allah sent" (meaning: the one who is in the place of the Speaker, who is the one who takes the place of Allah) a man from among his companions, to explain to him his case and his standing, and what secret Allah Almighty held in keeping silent about pursuing his right, while opening for him a way out from what he had done. The wali longed to return the truth to its people; and this is the meaning of the raven's scratching in the earth, to show him how to dispose of what he had brought upon himself, and to guide him to what was required.

He is the man whom the Guardian of God sent for that purpose: Muhammad ibn Abi Bakr — for he is among those specially chosen for the guardianship from the standpoint of religion, and from the opposite standpoint by lineage. So Muhammad did what he had been sent to do, that the adversary might be alerted to his crime, and brought back to abide by his pledge. The second adversary, however, prevailed over his judgment and prevented him from doing what he had wanted to do. He came to regret what he had done to the wali, and said to himself: "O woe to me — am I unable to be like such a raven? — O would that I were like this man" (meaning Muhammad his son), who is a confidant of the wali, his exemplar in keeping God's covenant and religion, and keeper of the

<!-- page 187 -->
secrets of the Guardian of God. So he became one of those who regret what they missed of following the wali first, and of the deed which will bring him to the abode of the Hereafter in the end. We seek refuge in Allah from breaking the covenant.

If this is the case, then there is nothing in this verse for the author of al-Nusra to take as proof against this verse — nothing, that is, except the statement of the author of al-Islah, in which lies the very obligation he affirmed: the Sharia and the ordinances of Adam, peace be upon him.

Chapter Seven of Title IX

It has been mentioned that laws, dispensations, ordinances, and customary practices may increase or diminish according to the era in which a Speaker arises, and according to what the welfare of the community at that time requires. Reports have reached us that washing the dead, embalming, shrouding, and burial are among the things that Adam, peace be upon him, prescribed; and although the reports of this have been repeated as stated, this verse is not among what they have cited about the mention of the raven and the scratching of the earth when the son of Adam was killed. The verse does not run along the line of parables that call for interpretation; its apparent sense is not what is taken according to what we have done. By what we have shown, the Sharia of Adam (peace be upon him) is established beyond dispute.

Chapter Eight of Title IX

Then we say: invoking this verse to deny the ordinances of Adam's Sharia (peace be upon him) is itself invalidated, since the burial of the dead is not the only thing whose ordinances may grow. Greater things grow alongside it — purity, prayer, zakat, and the rest of the acts of worship. With the elevation of his rank stands the elevation of all the other laws that form the foundations of worship. Rather, [burial] is one of those things done to the dead — burning by fire, casting into water,

<!-- page 188 -->
carrying to a cave or to the wilderness, burying in the ground, or feeding to the beasts. If [burial] is a kind of legislated practice, then with the elevation of its ordinance — which may be replaced by another — one should not invalidate the ordinances of the Sharia in their entirety. And if he does not press for that, then the Sharia of Adam, peace be upon him, is established.

Chapter Nine of Title IX

The author of al-Nusra said: Adam, peace be upon him, was not the bearer of a Sharia; for had he been the bearer of a Sharia, Allah would not have commanded the angels to prostrate to him through it.

We say: the author of al-Nusra has, by his own statement, obliged himself to grant that Adam, peace be upon him, is the bearer of a Sharia. For Allah's command to the angels to prostrate — even if it appeared to come not from the side of Adam, peace be upon him — was only effected through him. This is shown by the fact that the command of prostration was either impossible for him, in which case he is not the Chosen One, or it was not impossible for him to be the Chosen One. It has been proven that he is the Chosen One, as Allah says (interpretation of the meaning): "Allah chose Adam and Noah" (verse). If he is the Chosen One, then it is impossible that Allah command those who are below the Chosen One except through the Chosen One. So Allah's command to the angels to prostrate to Adam was effected only through him; and if it was through him, then the Sharia is established for him. So we say:

Chapter Ten of Title IX

Then Allah commanded us to prostrate to Adam, peace be upon him, by His saying, "Prostrate to Adam," just as He commanded the Children of Israel by saying, "O Children of Israel, remember My favor with which I blessed


<!-- page 189 -->

"And fulfill My covenant, and I will fulfill your covenant with Me, and fear Me. And believe in what I have sent down confirming what you already have, and be not the first to disbelieve in it. And do not exchange My signs for a small price, and fear Me. And do not mix the truth with falsehood, or conceal the truth while you know it. And establish the prayer, give the zakat, and bow with those who bow."

If what appears here is a form that does not indicate the presence of an intermediary, it nevertheless remains the case that the command of God reached the Children of Israel by way of Muhammad ﷺ. And if it could be said that Muhammad has no law applicable to them—the verse concerns God's command to the Children of Israel, which indicates that the command came by way of him, since the two verses were placed in a single chapter not indicative of a single authentic intermediary, and this is impossible. For God's command to the Children of Israel, although it is outwardly devoid of any mention of an intermediary, is in fact only by way of Muhammad ﷺ. And if that is impossible, then Adam is the author of a sharia, and the sharia is established for him.

Chapter Eleven of Title IX

The author of *al-Nusra* said:

"The angels are not assigned obligations and are not bound by sharia, because they do not disobey God in the twinkling of an eye; rather, they offer the praise of their Lord and do what they are commanded."

We say:

The angels who were charged with prostration—if they were not among those assigned obligations, they would not have been commanded to prostrate to Adam, and he would not have ordered them to bow to him. As for those angels who are not under obligation, they have never been in the abode of the body, nor have they stood up to action; they are forerunners in the abode of eternity. But what is meant

<!-- page 190 -->

is the angels who are appointed over the heavens, who dwell in the abode of nature and who govern the celestial spheres—those who bear the likeness of the creative and prophetic angels in the abode of eternity, as we have demonstrated. For only those in the abode of creative origination advance in rank. As for sajda (prostration) and ruku' (bowing), these belong to the duties of worship and works, which exist only in the natural world. Those charged with the establishment of these two acts of worship are commissioned so that through them they may receive the increase and the blessings of felicity by which God exalts their station, and they may attain the perfection that gains them eternity and survival. That is why the entrustment of the angels and the call upon them to act took place.

Chapter Twelve of Title IX

The author of *al-Nusra* said:

He said: "If the prostration of the angels to Adam had been their sharia, Adam would have said to them, 'God commands you to prostrate and obey me,' and he would have striven against those who disobeyed his command from God. It would also have been one of his pronouncements."

We say:

The author of *al-Islah* may reply: It does not follow that, because Adam did not say "God commands you to prostrate to me and obey me," prostration is therefore not an obligation. For the Prophet ﷺ did not say to the Children of Israel "God commands you to establish prayer and give me zakat," and yet there is no evidence that prayer, zakat, and all that he commanded them to do are not among the duties of sharia. The laws were established by the very fact that their conveyor was striving for them. Were this not so, then Jesus (peace be upon him) would not have had a sharia legitimately his, since he did not strive militarily—and this is invalid.

Chapter Thirteen of Title IX

The author of *al-Nusra* said:

— 188

<!-- page 191 -->

"Also, God Almighty did not mention in the Qur'an that Adam (peace be upon him) had a sharia, and He did not mention his being sent as He did with others, where He says, 'Indeed, We sent Noah,' nor did He mention him in the passage on sharia where He says, 'He has ordained for you of the religion what He enjoined upon Noah, and that which We have revealed to you, and what We enjoined upon Abraham, Moses, and Jesus—to establish the religion and not be divided in it.' The verse."

The author of *al-Nusra* is not fair in his objection, nor should one expect the author of *al-Islah* to be fair when he addresses the reform in his book. As for Adam, he is mentioned in the Qur'an, as are his followers and his people—the angels who were commanded to prostrate to him and from whom he taught the names, as God commanded him: "O Adam, inform them of their names." This sending is greater than the sending of others, because the sending of messengers is so that they may be God's vicegerents upon His earth. God Almighty Himself wrote in His Book that He made Adam a vicegerent upon the earth, to whom its people would return for what they needed in the matter of their religion. As for the absence of his mention in the passage where the one who legislated the sharia is named, as the author of *al-Islah* has said: he was not mentioned with them because he was not one of the *ulu al-'azm* (those of resolute determination), and because he was not a determined one. If it is permissible for Adam to be excluded from the company of the people of sharia for the reason that his name is not mentioned with the five (peace be upon them), it is also permissible because he is not mentioned among those to whom revelation was given, when the Almighty said: "Indeed, We have revealed to you as We revealed to Noah and the prophets after him, and We revealed to Abraham, Ishmael, Isaac, Jacob, the tribes, Jesus, Job, Jonah, Aaron, and Solomon"—the verse—for him to be excluded from their company. But this is impossible, and the saying is the saying of the author of *al-Islah*, taken from him.

Chapter XIV of Title IX

The author of *al-Nusra* said:

"We must also know the reason for the existence of sharia, so that it may be sound for us to say that Adam (peace be upon him) had no sharia."

We say:

The reason for the obligatoriness of sharia is the neglect of the *hudud* (legal limits), their disruption, and the pursuit of contraries by creation—and their claim that it is what God has not made it to be. This meaning did not appear in the time of Adam, for the world that existed before him and his people were neighbors at the border, and none of them violated a single limit, as they themselves declared when they said, "Will You place in it one who corrupts it and sheds blood, while we glorify You with Your praise and sanctify You?" (the verse). So why would Adam have had to establish sharia, and say such-and-such?

We say:

The reason for the necessity of sharia's existence differs from what the author of *al-Nusra* claimed in saying that the limits are neglected, that they are disrupted, and that creation pursues contraries. This is not what makes a sharia obligatory, nor does it establish one. Rather, we say on our part: as for the reason for the necessity of sharia's existence, it lies in the prescription of works, which we have explained in our book known as *al-Masabih fi al-Imama* (Lamps on the Imamate). The closest thing to the need of mankind, foremost in their ranks, is what preserves them from destruction by means of the prescriptions through which blood is safeguarded and lives and property are secured, and by which they are redeemed in matters of necessity that arise among them. Were these not in place, their affairs would come to a halt and they would destroy one another, the strong oppressing the weak through power, on account of their anger at the wealth and other things he possesses, and the fires of strife would be kindled between them. Thus, in the wisdom of the Maker Almighty, He cannot but supply what preserves them from destruction continuously—and that is sharia, whose existence is necessary if

(1) In copy (A): "and to be saved."
— 190

<!-- page 193 -->

Chapter XV of Title IX

Then we say:

Its existence—that is, the prescriptions of the laws—has become a necessary existence, because the souls in human beings each fail to restrain themselves from what is peculiar to them. From these actions, the prescriptions are indispensable that the community uses to silence the sit-down strike of their own claims and by which they secure one another. If the prescriptions are necessary, we say:

Some of them are necessary at all times and seasons, and some are not. Some may exist at one time and not at the last, increasing and decreasing. What is permanently necessary is such things as drawing near to God, retaliation in cases of bodily injury and homicide, the prescription of marriage, the division of property such as inheritance and the like, the prescription for the absent and the dead, and the prescription for extracting rights and returning them to their owners. For the human being has three souls: the rational, possessing distinction and thought, which knows it has a Creator and accordingly submits to Him, and from that submission draws closer to Him. As it draws closer, it is compelled to prescribed acts that it requires in great number, and these prescriptions are many; and the irascible, by which one grows angry, loves to overpower and to take revenge, and is drawn to injustice, killing, and deception, so that one is compelled to a prescription by which one rushes with bold force to separate them from one another, and to take the rights of the wronged from the aggressor by force—whether against the body, by retaliation, or by what serves in its stead; and the appetitive, from which lust arises, seeking marriage as a necessity, so one is compelled to a prescription that removes the sit-down strike of jealousy among them which leads them to death. This soul demands

(1) The sentence is completely omitted in copy (A).
(2) Fell in copy (A).
(3) Fell in copy (A).
— 191

<!-- page 194 -->

all wealth for itself by any means whatever, so one is compelled to prescriptions by which mankind secure themselves against each other's cheating, cunning, and deceit, which would lead them to domination and combat. These souls move with their eagerness to seek sustenance, and transactions become necessary, so people are compelled to work with one another—since treachery and cunning are in nature, prescriptions are required by which the plaintiff and his opponent proceed in extracting truth for its rightful holder. Death, too, is necessary among them, and they cannot do without disposing of the absent and the dead among them—whether by burial, cremation, or some other gentle means—lest they suffer harm; one is compelled to adopt one of these courses. These are six gateways by which the courses of the races run, and under which lie the species whose existence among human beings is unceasingly necessary. As for what is not always necessary, but at one time appears as a surplus and at another as a deficit, these are the duties of worshipping God Almighty and drawing closer to Him through knowing His limits, by which perfection is attained—this falls under the category of drawing near to God. All these prescriptions belong to sharia. They must be set forth first and prescribed among human beings at the beginning of their existence. And if these prescriptions are needed in the time of Adam (peace be upon him), who was one of the human beings, then upon him—and his angels—the laws of Islam are established.

Chapter Sixteen of Title IX

But whoever existed before Adam, his ruling is the ruling of whoever stands at the end of the seventh cycle, for example—in a manner that cannot dispense with the necessary prescriptions we have mentioned. Yet it could dispense with the prescriptions that fall under the genus of worship in drawing near to God Almighty, of which we said that they may at one time increase and at another decrease, and by which the second perfection is gained. So God sent Adam (peace be upon him) to teach them and others, lest they remain uneducated, and lest they be rewarded only deficiently. For even if they are distinguished from others by virtue, they fall short of the degree of perfection through their abandonment of the work of worship. He therefore prescribed, believed, and called them to the two acts of worship in knowledge and in deed. Some of them followed and obeyed,

— 192

<!-- page 195 -->

and some refused and turned back to the previous six gateways at the beginning of the cycles. Such is the order of those people.

Chapter Seventeen of Title IX

The author of *al-Nusra* said:

"But Noah (peace be upon him) had to establish it—meaning the sharia—because in the cycles before his turn, people had taken roles in violating the limits and pursuing contraries, such as friendliness (uns), prostration to al-Suwa', al-Jawooth, and others. So it was incumbent upon Noah (peace be upon him) to establish it, that whoever perished might perish with full knowledge, and whoever lived might live with knowledge."

We say:

What he says does not imply that Adam should have no sharia. Rather, Adam too must have had duties and a sharia, because it has been established that the roles in violating the limits and pursuing contraries—and such violation only befalls a follower's follower by abandoning the master's command and prescription—the violation itself was proven by saying that the prescriptions had become a sharia, and by it the violation occurred against Adam (peace be upon him). So sharia is established for him.

[Nada Sur Azbakiya]

Chapter Eighteen of Title IX

The author of *al-Islah* said:

"It has been narrated that God Almighty ordered Adam (peace be upon him) to set up a tent that He brought down from Paradise, and made it his qibla and the qibla of his sons. Then Seth built a place after him." This refers to sharia, its wisdom, the gathering of revelation and interpretation in it, the establishment of its branches and roots, and the binding force of its obligations.

We say:

The Book of Riyadh [13]
— 193

<!-- page 196 -->

The statement of the author of *al-Islah* proves that Adam had a sharia and prescriptions like the others who have been mentioned.

Chapter Nineteen of Title IX

The statement of the author of *al-Nusra*:

"In the structure and ordering of the world, in the matter of signifying the divine unity, it is indispensable to attribute states and conditions to the activity of beings. But that mode of activity and its signs have led many moralists away from monotheism and into anthropomorphic simile—and this is utterly impossible. Yet the structure of the world, the orderings and wonders of his wisdom, what lies in the composition of the world of bodies, the composition of bodies, the marriage of opposing natures within different bodies, and what is appointed in human beings to signify monotheism—all this cannot dispense with positing acts, similar actions, and differing states that signify shared meanings, and with parables represented by an existing parable, and with proofs founded on a clear indication. What is contained in these proofs—and the apparently contradictory deeds—and in these parables that multiply to establish known boundaries, is itself evidence of the divine unity. For the elaborate and intricate works of the world are wondrous, and souls celebrate deeds and signs; yet many of these signs, deeds, and apparent words have led most people away from monotheism and placed them within an anthropomorphic simile inappropriate to even the lowliest of God's creatures. The upshot of the argument is that the composition of the world and its orderings, in the matter of signifying the divine unity, cannot dispense with the positing of works, conditions, and states—and the state of works and signs has led much of creation away from monotheism and placed them within simile."

We say:

If he says that, then the sending of messengers becomes obligatory—and this is an excellence that he does not need.

— 194


<!-- page 197 -->
If the world's elaborate order is itself an arbiter indicating monotheism, then the sending of the messengers (1) to call to and signify monotheism — sent with the very evidence that others are already producing — would amount to nothing more than a virtue God does not need. I do not see how that goes for him: with the world's evident order so plainly arranged, is that not enough to indicate tawheed? And if tawheed does not exist except through one who teaches and demonstrates it, [the messenger] still benefits from knowing the structure of the world, its systems, and its arrangement — for the existence of those whose station has risen in this science, in terms of teaching the limits, [is what makes possible] the works and signs that revelation and the law gather, by which one reflects and deduces, and from which one infers what He wills creatures to know of God's limits and of His verses that signify it. Humans must come to know this, as Allah says: "Even when they come, He says, 'You have denied My signs and did not take note of them...'"

But the ones preceding in existence — the honorable jewels, namely the angels who are near to Him — are themselves in this position. So the matter stands thus: if [even they] need works, signs, alerting, and education — because not all of the science, work, and alertness that can be deduced from existence is reachable by the truth of inference alone — then how could it be reached by one who does not know? In sum, the saying that leads to the disruption of the degrees of the limits is a saying not from our belief, nor from the doctrine of our loyalists (may Allah's peace and blessings be upon them). Perhaps this statement was inserted into the book by opponents so that it could be attributed to its author. And Allah knows best.

Chapter Twenty of Title IX

Moreover, the [purpose of] work is not only for monotheism, but also for the correction of the soul in worship, for emulating the highest assembly, and for acquiring — through exercise — honorable morals and noble habits by which they are prepared for joining what is above them, and by which they are kept from departing from
(1) Fell in copy (A).
- 195

<!-- page 198 -->
the power that preserves the dignity of the intellect by fulfilling its duties, [the one] who is concerned with its features. Were it not for the deeds and rites of the Ummah, and the wholeness of souls within it through perseverance in them, souls would perish. That is why God did not answer the angels when they reckoned that what they performed of worship was sufficient in the matter of nearness to God Almighty and of their own perfection, and that they did not need a caliph to teach them what they did not know, or to command them to do anything — when He said: "I know what you do not know."

The Almighty [said] this because the universes, by His decree and wisdom, are such that they hover between potency and act: sometimes a thing exists by potency [and is still standing], and sometimes it is already by act. And the thing was such that, when it ended, it had to end with respect to itself, returning regressively from potency to act, and from act to potency — like the hot, which, if the heat ends in the belly of [a body], cold appears, as in the melting of bodies and the like; and likewise, if the cold ends, the cold gives way and heat appears, as in snow; and like the gathering of [a mountain's] parts — when its gathering ends, dissolution appears in its parts, so they disperse and return to sand. Such is the change of beings in the abode of nature.

So too, science and ignorance in the world of the universe: corruption is sometimes ignorance, and knowledge is by potency — in the sense that ignorance is general because of the presence of its people, and ignorance (3) is lost because of the absence of its people. There was a time which was the time when ignorance had taken over and already reached its end, and knowledge was [then] introduced and reached its end in potency. The teachers were disturbed, the ceremonies of the laws were disrupted, and the deeds extended in duration while the times were shortened. By "the laws" I mean what cannot be present except through the rituals and rites; and by "the angels" I mean themselves — the best of them, the bearers of knowledge at that time, the ones who worshipped Allah. They reported that what they were in was sufficient for them in the matter of worship and perfection. And it was —
(2) In Copy (B) it came: "and the science."
- 196

<!-- page 199 -->
the matter is different from what they estimated, since they needed to know the limits in totality — those who are in the house of creativity and the first rebirth — and the knowledge of those who come later in existence is like the second revival, [along with] working with what earns them [this knowledge]. When they realized that their time was the time of the complete role of ignorance, and not the role of knowledge, nor the role of work, the Almighty taught them to leave them as they are when [things] reach the end of ignorance — since time is the time of the beginning of knowledge by [its] appearing, which is what wisdom is for.

The Almighty said: I know from the end of (1) ignorance in the world to its end-the-act, the completion of its role, its appearance — and from the attainment of knowledge in the world to its goal in potency, and the return of repentance to Him in the reappearance, and the resurgence of need in His appearance, to the establishment of the teachers and the guides — what you do not know. So Allah sent Adam (peace be upon him), making him His successor to manifest knowledge, to gain them integrity in act, to show them how to know the boundaries, and to teach them what they did not know. This was the beginning of the matter in the emergence of knowledge from potency to act, in the presence of his people, from his days (peace be upon him).

Its end extends to the one who stands up (peace be upon him). And it is also said that when the one who stands (peace) appears, knowledge has already become — meaning it has become widespread, and its people increase, while ignorance is internalized — meaning its people are reduced and its source is cut off. The treasures of the earth appear in his days, and he releases them to their manifestation; this is a sign that he is the one who stands. Until knowledge is already established, he reveals all that has been deposited in the bellies of the revealed books and the complex symbols. And though the angels, with the presence of the heavens and their good organization, mastering the structure of the world's assets, did not [themselves] know, nor were they perfect, and there is no inference from [their state] except what Adam (peace be upon him) uncovered and called them to — what the author of al-Nusra mentioned was uninvestigated: namely, that deeds, signs, and proverbs are indispensable in the chapter of inference

of some of them to monotheism, politics, self-correction, and their exercise in [coming to] knowledge and being brought out into the action (1). If that is the case, then Adam is the author of the Shari'ah and the customs that he called to, and which indicated the limits of Allah and His unity.

Chapter Twenty-One of Title IX

The author of al-Nusra said:

The author of this article says that creation in the great role did not know — was there not a similar apparent law among them? Or [does he] say that the one who is responsible — the one who stands (peace be upon him) — by raising the laws nullifies the knowledge of monotheism, and the people are driven to saying that it is obstructed? If creation was not ignorant of monotheism, nor of the role of the Qa'im — and they also did not know him, since the laws had been raised — then it would have been permissible for Adam (peace) to call people to monotheism (2) without any references, and the like of this by [the same] saying.

And we say:

Our words on knowledge and ignorance, and the fact that each of them is sometimes by potency and sometimes [by act], may already have established what explains the situation: in what creation was during the great role, being at the end of ignorance, according to the saying of the author of al-Nusra — that the call of Adam (peace be upon him), being a teacher to his followers of the landmarks of monotheism, was such that he did not need it [to be made], because it was [supposedly] obligatory that the people [already] knew about monotheism. And if they (3) knew the monotheism, Adam's invitation to them to do nothing more than what they already knew was nothing but a virtue that he did not need — and that is impossible.
