<!-- page 201 -->
The people did not need [from him] what they already had for themselves; and when Allah sent Adam, He would not have made him His successor among them, nor chosen him from among them, nor made him His caliph, nor made it obligatory for them to obey him, nor [made them] return to him in what they did not know. Rather, it would have been [as in] the saying of the angels: "We praise You and sanctify You" — in their estimation that they were already adults in praise, sanctification, and monotheism, and that what they were in was sufficient in the matter of worship and knowledge of the limits.

But Allah, who knows who is commanded, is the One whose mercy obliged the choosing of Adam from among them. He sent him to them, making him a caliph in their education, inviting them to their perfection in work and knowledge — all of which leads to knowing the limits in which tawheed is valid. Therefore He said: "I know what you do not know," because the Almighty knew that they were imperfect, that ignorance had reached its end in the act, and that the work had come to appear — yet they did not know it.

This is why it is obligatory in wisdom to establish the office of education, which is the cause of the expansion of knowledge and the multiplication of its people. So Allah selected Adam, and made [for him] law, and taught him, as Allah said: "And Adam knew all the names. Then He showed them to the angels and said, 'Tell Me the names of these, if you are truthful.' They said, 'Glory be to You! We have no knowledge except what You have taught us. You are the All-Knowing, the All-Wise.' He said, 'O Adam, tell them their names.' [And when he had told them their names,] He said, 'Did I not tell you that I know the unseen of the heavens and the earth?'" — the verse. The people were of two worlds; because they were so, Adam came to them as their teacher.

Chapter Twenty-Two of Title IX

We say:

As for his statement and affirmation that creation was not, in the first [role] of the Qa'im (Sal'am), ignorant of monotheism — that if they were not ignorant, they could only deduce it from the side of the supporters, which includes the integrity of themselves and their images, when there was chosen from
- 199

<!-- page 202 -->
among them Adam, so that he would be the caliph of God Almighty in teaching them what is in their interests, sooner and later — it is impossible to imagine such a perception in those [conditions] and to give them a status that would be theirs.

And his saying "and not in the role of the one who stands — they are ignorant" — if what he means is the image of the da'wah, that before the role of the Qa'im appears, and before it exists from the natural world — when [the da'wah] is preserved by his successors and preachers after the completion of the role of Muhammad al-Mustafa (Salam) and his [followers], until [the Qa'im's] time and the appearance of His signs in the world — then it is true. For then knowledge will have raised the beacon of its appearance through the many scholars; this is the time of which Allah said: "On the day when the children will grow old."

But if what he meant by his saying is that he is in the role of the guardian — no — they are ignorant after the Qa'im (Salam), who is the owner of the seventh stage, who dismisses speech, distinguishes the people of Paradise from the people of Hell, and closes the door of education. Repentance is not valid then, because they do not remain knowledgeable, and they are not of the people of knowledge. For that time is the time when the waters overflow and cover [the earth], the time when misery and austerity are seized and made manifest.

The words of the author of al-Nusra on this matter are not those of a true investigator.

Chapter Twenty-Three of Title IX

In summary, the author of al-Nusra mentioned only these sayings and quotes in support of the owner of the crop in his discharge of the law of Adam: that Adam is not devoid of his call to them; that, being a calm prophet, either he called them to encourage them toward Paradise and the neighborhood of the Lord of the Worlds, or he called them to intimidate them into Hell and to keep them far from the mercy of the best of the creators, or both. If He calls them to encouragement, the encouragement is not perceptible except by striking
(1) The sentence is omitted in its entirety in copy (a).
- 200

<!-- page 203 -->
proverbs, and by reports about [Paradise] with what is known by the senses of the kinds of pleasures, blessings (1), and good places — such as dwellings, orchards, rivers, and trees — so that they can conceive of it. That is a form of speech that requires interpretation. And even if He called them to intimidation, His saying is only by giving them proverbs that frighten them — [moving] from this to the world of the senses, so that they may visualize it; and in the interpretation of it there is a stream other than the above.

If He calls them to both, then His words are not without a valid outward appearance, [and yet] interpretation is necessary. If that is the case, then in [all] three sections, what is positive for the phenomenon is fixed for Adam, followed by interpretation — so Adam is the owner of a law and a clear custom.

Chapter Twenty-Four of Title IX

Then we say:

Adam (peace be upon him) is not without one of two cases: either he called the people of his time to obey God by obeying Him with wisdom, or [he called them] without wisdom; and the [supposition] that he did not call them is impossible, [as is] the belief that he was outside of religion. And if he calls them to wisdom, then wisdom requires knowledge and all of the work — so Adam is the owner of a law that combines knowledge and action. They are the worship of Allah, and by them one attains perfection and ascent to the houses of dignity.

Chapter Twenty-Five of Title IX

With regard to the one who stands (peace be upon him), and the saying that he raises the laws:

We say:

Believing that is unsound. For the estimation of its existence in the natural world does not arise from the second way of revival, because it does not invalidate anything related to the worship of Allah, the Almighty. Rather, it is the conclusion of the roles of the predecessors, the completion of something that has been done in its place; it removes the boundaries
(1) Fell in copy (A).
- 201


<!-- page 204 -->
the new creation. As Allah Almighty has said: "Are they then in doubt about a new creation?" — so nothing here invalidates that, after He has set down a judgement upon the souls of those from whom the course of the foundations is derived, namely those whose roles have run out. And Allah Almighty said, describing the Owner of the Day of Resurrection: "When the heaven is split apart (1) and when the planets are scattered (2)"; and "When the sun is darkened, when the stars are dimmed, when the mountains are set in motion" — and the rest of those signs. All of these similitudes are folded upon the limits, and the announcements concern their passing away — that is, their ranks — not the laws or the works whose time has elapsed; for those laws then survive with no teachers, except for what is necessary to its existence as we have mentioned.

Go forward, then, with the understanding that this is the time at which this act appears, when the door of teaching is closed and the summons from the side of the Owner of the Great Resurrection is broken off — the time at which knowledge returns to potency and is concealed, and ignorance begins to spread. We have already explained that the appearance of these signs along this path is sufficient — Allah suffices us, and the community of believers, those people of divine goodness, by following His guardians. May we be gathered with Muhammad and Ali and the pure Imams (peace and blessings of Allah be upon them), the lights of His purity, His mercy, and His grace.

Chapter Twenty-Six of Title IX

The author of al-Nusrah said:

"Anyone who claims that monotheism (3) is realized only through signs and acts has lost his way in judging that the First Intellect, the Soul, the Angels, the Jadd, the Fath, and the Khayal did not know Tawhid — since there were neither signs nor acts between them or among them."

We say:

Whoever says what he criticizes does not mean the First Intellect, nor the higher limits;

(1) In copy (b) it reads (al-Shaqt).
(2) In copy (b) it reads (al-Taturt).
(3) The sentence is omitted in copy (a).
— 202

<!-- page 205 -->
rather, he means the intellects in the world of body, which is the second emanation, since their nile — that is, their attainment of perfection — is by way of teaching (1), assignment, knowledge, signs, and works, by acquisition. The First Intellect and the transcendent limits beyond bodies do not attain their perfection by anything of that sort; they attained their perfections from the very beginning of their existence in origination and emanation. It is therefore not permissible to apply the ruling of this kind of statement (2) to that domain. And what the author of al-Nusrah mentioned concerning the meaning of the seven planets, their stations, their return, and their direct courses — and the six days in which Allah created the heavens and the earth and what is between them — together with what he mentioned by way of determination, the author of al-Islah left nothing of that without bringing forth the interpretation of it and its meanings. He had enough Arabization in the interpretation that this hardly balances (3) and does not match.

Chapter Twenty-Seven of Title IX

The author of al-Islah said:

The first line is not made obligatory until the second line appears, because the line indicates the speaker and the arda indicates the Sharia, and a thing's image is not made manifest except after its completion. Just as the arda appeared only after the second line, so too the law of the first was not fulfilled until the appearance of the second Speaker; and when the second Speaker appeared, the law of the first was completed and its image appeared in its perfection. The same goes for the six ardas among the seven lines, since there is no law before the first pronunciation and no law at the appearance of the seventh — instead there are six laws of the pronunciations, the seventh of which is the Qa'im. If you were to draw seven lines, six of them would appear between them along these similitudes — one, two, three, four, five, six — even if the calculation runs upon that.

(1) Omitted in copy (A).
(2) In Copy (B): "the intellects came."
(3) In copy (B) it reads (balanced).
— 203

<!-- page 206 -->
By the principle that the first pronunciation has no law, five ardas must appear among seven lines. When five appear between seven lines — six widths in all — the seven lines stand upon the owners of the seven cycles, and the six widths upon the six laws; and the law was thus made obligatory for Adam.

The author of al-Nusrah said:

In the matter of the six widths between the seven lines, and the inference of the lines as standing for the Prophets and of the presentations for the six laws, the matter is not as he says. Rather, the seven lines correspond to the higher letters, since each Prophet wrote a single letter from among them; and the six presentations correspond to the cycles of the Imams between Adam and his Successor, and to the seven Imams; and between Noah and Abraham in the second cycle, and between Abraham and Moses in the third cycle, and likewise down to Muhammad (M). No other cycle remains for the Qa'im except the cycle of his successors and substitutes in the world. The five lines fall between six widths, just as five pronouncements occurred among the cycles of the Imams of the Lawgivers and their like:

Adam — Noah — Abraham — Moses — Jesus — Muhammad — al-Qa'im.

The Successor; the Successor; the Successor; the Successor; the Imams; the Imams; the Imams.

Just as the Qa'im (may Allah's peace and blessings be upon him) has no Imams yet, so he is not called upon to establish a Sharia; and likewise, when there were no Imams before Adam (peace be upon him), he was not called upon to establish a Sharia. For if a Sharia is to be established, it must be preceded by Imams and followed by Imams. The Successor of Noah (peace be upon him) advanced upon the cycle of Adam's Successor (peace be upon him), so Noah was preceded by Imams, and he was therefore obliged to establish a Sharia. To Abraham, Moses, Jesus, Muhammad (peace be upon them) and al-Qa'im (may Allah be pleased with him), the cycle of Muhammad's Successor was advanced, and they were not preceded by Imams, so they were not obliged to establish a Sharia. Likewise Adam was preceded by his Successor, and no Imams came before him, so a Sharia had to be established. This is what they say.

— 204

<!-- page 207 -->
We say:

The statement of the author of al-Islah is sound in what he has indicated, but what he said — namely that just as the arda appeared only after the second line, so the law of the first is fulfilled only with the appearance of the second Speaker — is a defective and incorrect statement. For the law of Adam is itself its own basis, and he has established its basis even though it is permissible to say that the law of Adam was only completed with the coming of the Speaker of the second cycle. By that logic one could say that the law of Muhammad is not perfected, and is only completed with the coming of the Qa'im, the Owner of the Seventh Cycle — peace of Allah be upon him. But this is one of the impossibilities of speech: for a Sharia is a structure that requires no increase upon it and admits no decrease. And yet the author of al-Islah said: the first cycle, which is Adam's (peace be upon him), was completed only with the appearance of the second Companion, who is Noah (peace be upon him); and likewise the cycles are not completed except by the appearance of what follows them. So he made the location of his statement about the Sharia the first cycle. Had he reversed it, it would have been consistent. May Allah forgive us and him by His mercy.

Chapter Twenty-Eight of Title IX

As to the saying of the author of al-Nusrah on the line (1) and the arda, and his interpreting the six presentations upon the Imams: it is more fitting that the placements be upon the pronouncements and not upon the letters of the higher world, and that the presentations stand upon the duration of their authority in the cycle, not upon the Successors. Then his account does not require that Adam have no law; on the contrary, the Sharia is made obligatory for him by the presence of the Imams after him. The presence of the Imams entails the obligation of preserving the place of the Speaker, his Sharia, and his outlines in his community. Without that, it would not have been obligatory to obey the Imam of any speaking cycle. The five lines and the six widths the author of al-Islah may take up, placing the lines upon

(1) Dropped in copy (b).
— 205

<!-- page 208 -->
the five definite ones, and the presentations upon the cycles of the Owners of the Six Laws — because the principle is that the thing be paralleled and balanced in its interpretation; and if that is said it will hold in souls as well, yes.

In sum, we say:

The cycle is itself a cycle — a great cycle and a small cycle. The great cycle belongs to the Speakers who preserve their place in their community after the Imams; the small cycle belongs to the faithful Successors who seal the weeks. If the lines are assigned to the people of the great cycles — who are the Speakers — then the presentations would be only upon the duration of their community's governance. On the other side, the Successor after them, in their cycle, seals it with the coming of another like him, and his measure is known. If the lines are assigned to the seven minor figures — who are the Imams in each cycle — then the presentations would only be upon the duration of the Imams' order in each week, together with their attendants, who turn upon the seventh; and their number, and the number of the Imams in each cycle, and the number of the attendants according to the assets of the higher bodies, will be described — you will find mention of that in books other than this one, by the permission of Allah.

Chapter Twenty-Nine of Title IX

The author of al-Nusrah said:

For example, the words of Allah Almighty — "As you began, so shall you return" — mean "as you were at the beginning of the matter," which was not in terms of facts, so it is the last of the matter.

We say:

Even if this is one interpretation, the proper face is to say what corresponds to the saying of Allah Almighty: namely that this is a reference to the return of the Qa'im (peace be upon him) — the return of knowledge after it had gone out into action, its abundance into potency, its scarcity, and its predetermined apportionment over all the people after him, just as they were in the time of Adam; and the return of ignorance after it had been in
— 206

<!-- page 209 -->
potency, and its reduction into action, and its multiplication on account of its appointed wisdom — and blessed is Allah, the best of creators.

Chapter Thirty of Title IX

0

The author of al-Nusrah said:

If the author of al-Islah had looked into what is contained in his name — I mean Adam — by way of indication, he would have seen that Adam was not the owner of a Sharia. For his name, which consists of three letters — Alif, Dal, and Mim — does not yield a compound or a composite; each letter is indicated by another. This is a clear sign that he was not the author of a Sharia. The five Prophets who came after him, however, were the owners of names of composite form, like Noah, Abraham, Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad (peace be upon them) — a proof that each of them was the owner of a Sharia. This is what he said.

We say:

If it were the case that the separateness of the letters of Adam's name necessitated that Adam have no Sharia, then the fact that these letters are not separate in themselves would oblige him to have a Sharia — for the alif of the name is composed of alif, lam and fa; the dal is composed of dal, alif and lam; the mim is composed of mim, ya, and mim — and likewise for what is signified. If separateness did not entail that he have a Sharia, separateness would have obliged a Sharia; provided that inference of this kind is sound. But it is not, because if it were sound, it would entail that the one charged with the authorship of the Qur'an — namely the Prophet (peace be upon him), Muhammad, whose name is composed of four letters — would be impossible to be the owner of a Sharia, since his name is a compound. And it cannot be that the separateness of the letters of Adam's name proves that he is not the author of the Sharia while the meeting of the letters of the name of the Qa'im (peace be upon him), who is Muhammad,
— 207

<!-- page 210 -->
proves by the fact of being a compound that he is the author of the Sharia. And if that is invalidated, then no inference of this kind holds, and the Sharia for Adam stands established. Praise be to Allah, the Lord of the Worlds.

Chapter Thirty-One of Title IX

The author of al-Islah said:

As for the saying that Noah (peace be upon him) was the first to establish its foundation and was struck like the ship — this has been treated in the chapter on the basics, where there is abundance enough.

We say:

Rather, Noah was singled out by the Ark because he was the first to have intentions, and the first to abrogate laws and to compose a new law. His law was as a ship that he likened to the Flood: by it he destroyed the people of the earth, except those who responded to his law and boarded his ship — that is, established obedience to its foundation — meaning that this law of his held during his lifetime; and when he completed it he entrusted the matter to its foundation and commanded it to establish the call upon his law, and ordered the attendants to obey it. They did not receive the obedience of the servant except through it. Whoever obeyed its foundation was in the ship; and whoever disobeyed it and clung to the first law, do you not see that he was described as having drowned by the Flood — those who worshipped Wadd, Suwa', Yaghuth, Ya'uq, and Nasr? Allah Almighty said: "Because of their sins they were drowned, and were made to enter the Fire."

The author of al-Nusrah said:

If the status of the ship were the status of the apparent law, then those who held fast to the apparent would have been saved — and the inward, true call would have been annulled — provided that what appears from the book of Allah Almighty differs from what the author of al-Islah went to. Likewise, Allah Almighty described the beginning of the command of Noah (peace be upon him): He sent him to his people and called them to his law, and then ordered him to build the Ark. But these
— 208


<!-- page 211 -->
The description applies in Surat Hud as well: even if the Ark stands for the apparent shariʿah, God would have ordered Noah first to construct the Ark. But the actual sequence of the story shows otherwise. Noah composed the apparent law and called the creation to live by it, and only then was he commanded to make the Ark — which signifies the establishment of his guardian (waliyy). The evidence for this is God's words, may He be exalted, "And he made the Ark," and likewise, "And whenever the chiefs of his people passed by him, they laughed at him." That is, whenever Noah (peace be upon him) issued a directive, the chiefs of his people mocked him, marveling at him because he was the first to institute a guardian; that is why they ridiculed him.

So much for what they said.

As for our position: regarding the statement of the author of al-Islah, who interprets the Ark as a figure of the new shariʿah — because whoever enters under its rulings becomes like one who is aboard a ship, safe from indignity and every calamity, just as the one inside an actual ship is delivered from drowning — this interpretation is possible. As for the author of al-Nusrah, who interprets the Ark as a figure of the daʿwah and the guardian — because whoever enters the serene daʿwah and binds himself to obedience to the foundation has been delivered from drowning in error, has been brought to the unity of God Almighty and to the knowledge of His limits, with the guardian serving as his teacher and holding for him the rank of a refuge — this too has merit. The two interpretations are correct in their own respects: the first concerns the order of this world, and the second concerns the order of the brethren, and neither dispenses with the other, since each of them raises its own head.

## Chapter Thirty-Two of Title IX

As for the claim of the author of al-Nusrah that Noah was the first to establish a guardian by way of interpretation (taʾwil), this is not correct. The prophethood of Adam (peace be upon him) and his shariʿah are established, and it is firmly established that Seth (peace be upon him) was his guardian. The precedent of shariʿah, of the guardian, and of the guardian's appointment did not begin with Noah. It is also a matter of reason that each
<!-- page 211 marker: The Book of Riyad [14], page 209 -->

<!-- page 212 -->
speaker (natiq) requires a guardian who benefits from him, just as every moved thing necessarily has a mover — the mover being one, even if many derive their motion from it. And since the speaker in his own age is the source from which all souls draw inspiration through his summons to remain steadfast by performing the two acts of worship, he must necessarily have a one-of-a-kind successor through whom the course of the learner is traced, even though many learners take from him. The learner who comprehends the truth is the one we call the guardian. When Adam was the speaker, he therefore must have had a guardian, and likewise every speaker. And if Adam already had a guardian appointed for him, it is not permissible to believe anything contrary to that.

## Chapter Thirty-Three of Title IX

The author of al-Nusrah said:

After relating what God Almighty said in the story of the son of Noah — "I will take refuge in a mountain that will protect me from the water," and Noah's reply, "There is no protector today from the command of God" — he claimed that the matter of the guardian was unknown before Noah's time. He argued that the son of Noah did not know the rank of the guardian, and that if Adam had had a guardian, his son would have known of it.

So he said.

We say:

This statement of his contradicts what he himself said in the chapter on the cycles, namely that the cycle of Adam (peace be upon him) was followed by the Imams who succeeded him. I do not know how this slipped past him, and I suspect that the manuscript of his book has been altered and added to — for it is the failing of those who concern themselves with religious matters that they neglect to correct what they write by review and recitation to the people of knowledge and religion. And God is the helper.

The proper interpretation of the prayer of the son of Noah is that this son, though attributed to
<!-- page 212 marker: page 210 -->

<!-- page 213 -->
Noah, was not truly of his line spiritually. He was a powerful man, possessed of worldly authority, who occupied a position parallel to that of the adversary in the cycle of our Prophet Muhammad. He had drawn benefit from Noah, had learned what he knew, and so became, by the strength he derived, his rival; people too imagined that he was the guardian himself. When the command of God Almighty came to make the Ark — that is, the mother of Noah's daʿwah — the office of guardianship was assigned by prophetic warrant to another beside him, in accordance with what God had commanded. Noah called the people who followed him to enter under his command and to obey him. That is when he said, "Do not be among the disbelievers" — meaning, do not stand outside of what others have entered, namely obedience to his foundation (asas). His refusal to enter the Ark, then, was Noah's son's refusal to obey, when Noah (peace be upon him) said, "O my son, ride with us and be not with the disbelievers."

He held back from accepting the commitment of the order, hoping in particular to return to the position he had originally occupied. From what Noah had observed of him by way of fine obedience to his commands when he prayed to him, he came to despair, taken in total, of the rank he had been hoping for — and so he became hypocritical, arrogant, and refused obedience on the basis of what had happened. He said in effect, "The command of God is in this — if you do not restore my presidency to me, it will be voided," and the opposite happened with our guardian, peace be upon him. Noah accordingly counseled him at times and frightened him at others. He said, "The command of God is in it, and if you do not heed it, your presidency will be lost and you will be enveloped in disgrace; and you will not find anyone supporting you or aiding you in your affairs." That is the meaning of his words, "And there is no protector today from the command of God."

When the son heard this from him, he openly defied him and said, in effect: "It is not for you to gather the whole presidency into your house, even if it appears as good intention; I have been backed by chieftains who have tightened my hand and helped me to ward off humiliation from me." This is the meaning of his words, "I will take refuge in a mountain that will protect me from the water." The water here is what divides Noah's people against the force that destroys them, and the mountain is what is sustained by men and saints. So the son made his intent and took those of his kind with him, and they perished in his obstinacy. There remained only the one who had bound himself to obedience to Noah's foundation. God has indeed mentioned such reports in His Noble Book as a warning to the nation of what is done under the foundational command against those who are recalcitrant and refuse to bind themselves to it. Sometimes our Prophet refers to it with "O children of Adam," sometimes with the camel of Salih, sometimes with the son of Noah — so that one may know that the conditions of Muhammad's cycle are like the conditions of the cycles
<!-- page 213 marker: page 211 -->

<!-- page 214 -->
mentioned above. He did not consign Muhammad and his foundation, by the prayers of God upon them, to ignorance of the obstinacy of their community — peace be upon them — nor of their language. This is a pattern that has run through all the prophets and the guardians, all the way to the time of the seventh; and the apostasy of the son of Noah was not the last instance, but extended to every section of the people of the apparent (ẓāhir) who fell into a similar state of hypocrisy. He sought their help, and the sword of the maternal uncle was wielded by the power of God, and God cleansed the earth of them. That is the flood, which extended outwardly and inwardly. The same applies in the cycle of Muhammad, may God's prayers be upon him: among it is what is in conformity, of it is what corresponds, and of it is what is true, God willing. Only one who is counted among the people of the elevated (ʿalawī) daʿwah, of which God is the light, recognizes this.
<!-- page 214 marker: page 212 -->

<!-- page 215 -->
## Chapter Ten of the Book of Riyad

On what is more befitting in his treatment from the book of al-Mahsul, in the chapter on monotheism and innovation, which is the first of what comes first — to set right what was discussed, and to set right …

### Chapter One of Chapter Ten

We say:

The author of al-Mahsul, may God have mercy on him, exerted himself, exaggerated, and made up his mind concerning the right of the daʿwah and its sons, and concerning the opening of the gates of its landmarks, in what he composed from among the books of those charged with the work whose appearance he deferred. Sheikh Abu Hatim, may God have mercy on him, did not set this right, even though he was capable. Most people supposed that this proceeded from him: he reproached the author of al-Mahsul, made apology for the author of al-Mahsul's emendation of the matter in his book, and so let the matter slip; in turn others overturned the case — and that is corrupt estimation. They acted at a remove from the truth, while the actual situation is otherwise than what they imagined.

The speaker (peace be upon him), when he came to lay things out, knew that the sciences of religion are too vast to be encompassed in the single breath of an imam, or to be memorized in a single utterance, or to be arranged by any number of persons short of the imamate. The imams therefore cooperate with one another on all of the sciences — on awareness of them, on memorization of them — like the twelve sources (ḥujjas), so that each of them takes benefit from those whom God has appointed
<!-- page 215 marker: page 213 -->

<!-- page 216 -->
to him as a crisis-guide from the rightly guided sinless ones, peace and blessings of God be upon them, in the secrets of things, both inner and outer, according to their strength in receiving and in deducing — so that for others they serve as guidance, just as the imams, peace be upon them, do in expression and benefit. They are, in this respect, like one human being whose parts come together, and like the senses, which perceive things in such a way that if one sense misses something, another perceives it without that second perception being a defect for the one that missed it or for the others.

If that is so, then the emendation that Abu Hatim, by God's elevation, did not lift up — what he repaired in the book of al-Mahsul — is no fault on his part, nor is it a deficiency in the author of al-Mahsul. There is no objection against either of them, except what we have already said: namely, that the people of the rank charged with setting right the sciences of religion are like the senses — if one sense misses the perception of something in the world of nature, the other does not miss it, but rather cooperates with it.

The judgment of the truth in religion, and the confirmation of the obligation owed to the Lord of the Worlds, is what it is. Since that is so, when I saw in the book of al-Mahsul something that Abu Hatim was not obligated to address — and that he was not bound to expound and set right of what he had set right and not spoken of, but had passed by — it was incumbent on me to cooperate in discharging the right of religion by stating what is at stake in what I have mentioned. It is not permissible to believe in the chapter on tawḥīd, and on the First Creator, without addressing the other branches — which God help us through by deferring the term — that we have treated in other than this book. So it is not permissible for me to make apology for that, because the souls of religion are all on the same footing. If I make apology for myself, may God accept from us what we draw near to Him with from the guardianship that leads, by the satisfaction of God Almighty, the Lord of the worlds, and gather us together with him — the foremost, the alternates, and the followers, and the latecomer — together with our pure loyalists in the Abode of Holiness, by a noble degree.

### Chapter Two of Chapter Ten

The author of al-Mahsul said:

In the chapter on monotheism: He is the creator of the thing — the rational thing, the imaginal thing,
<!-- page 216 marker: page 214 -->

<!-- page 217 -->
the intellectual thing, the logical thing — I mean, that which is beneath these elements, and that which is not beneath them.

We say:

It has been established by this statement that, among the possessions of God Almighty which fall under His innovation (ibdāʿ), there is nothing that is not perceived by reason, imagination, or thought, and reported about by speech — its existence is impossible, and to believe in such a thing is impermissible. For whatever is not perceived by reason, nor by imagination, nor by thought, nor reported about by speech, cannot be such that, if it exists, it either precedes the intellect in its existence, or is with the intellect in its existence, or follows the intellect in its existence. It is impossible for it to precede the intellect, since the intellect is the first act, behind which there is none but God Almighty, the One who issued it, and from whom it came into existence: it is pure innovation, and the prior existence — which is the first existence — is from God Almighty.

It is likewise impossible that its existence be with that very intellect¹, for the intellect is the same intellect that is innovated, and the same intellect, when it occurs, is only a single essence, not two. Moreover, were its existence to be with the intellect, the two being differentiated and yet sharing in existence, it would necessarily follow that God Almighty has issued, by reason of it, a multiplicity of innovated causes — or that there is, alongside Him, the Almighty, another Almighty in this matter. Such an effect would have its existence corresponding to its cause; and the fact that differential causes require two or more common causes — so that the existence of each one of them depends on a proportion of its causes that differs from the others, primarily — makes it impossible that distinct things exist by a single cause that is itself free of differentiation in its proportions and additions. If its existence with the intellect would require all this, then its existence is impossible. God is exalted above all multiplicity, additions, and othernesses, and above whatever could become a cause for Him.

It is also impossible that its existence be subsequent to the intellect, because if its existence were after the intellect, and proceeded from Him, the intellects would not be devoid of knowing it,
<sup>1</sup> Dropped in copy (b).
<!-- page 217 marker: page 215 -->

<!-- page 218 -->
nor would they find it difficult to define it; rather, it would have been known and defined, just as every other asset is — sensed and intelligible, in the domain of the positive and the negated. And if it is not free of one of these three sections, and each one of them obliges that its existence is impossible, it is proven that its existence is impossible — and to speak of it is misguidance.

### Chapter Three of Title X

Then, if the author of al-Mahsul does not say that the result of the thing is positive, then "the thing" is not what God created — I mean what is beneath these elements, and what is not beneath them. We would have said that what he meant by "the thing" was what is described from among the bodies, and the nothingness — only the attributes of bodies from the world of innovation are described — on the error of one who says that, believing in him. But when he mentioned what entails, on the positive side, the impossible, he ended up believing in an existence in which God shares in His being pure; in this way, corruption spreads from every side, and so we have shown that this is impossible.

Then we say:

The "thing" is known as a common term under which all the essences and intentions, sensed and intelligible, fall. It is not without significance to call something "a thing" — nothing — but, as some sages have said, it is a positive for that which is other than something. Positively, it is stripped of what the thing in all its parts is restricted to, of essence and accidents and the elevation of the amended condition, and they pointed to it: the human being is a positive for everything that is not a human, in the sense that the predicate strips us of existence without affirming what is not for anything. They said: it is not the case that one means by a letter that has no eye to remove from existence, denying it to that which is without positivity, which is another to him — as in saying "the thing" that is the eye of all, from which everything that exists has fallen, if we meant it as a cliff; no, we said "nothing," and the eye of the thing became vanished from what is in it.
<!-- page 218 marker: page 216 -->


<!-- page 219 -->
Without a proposition, he is distinct from him; and if by "eye" he means a partial eye within that very eye, and if he intends to affirm something as positive and something else as not, then he is saying that God created Him wrongly — because that which has no attribute and no being is nothing at all. In the same way, it would be both essence and accident, and would amount to the negation of the attributes of a thing from God Almighty (exalted and sanctified is He). For God Almighty is neither essence nor accident, that He should become endowed with attributes. A thing has no attribute within Him, nor of Him, nor like Him — as if he were saying that the Creator of the thing and of nothing is the creator of Himself (who is other than the thing) and the creator of that thing which is other than Himself; and that is false, even if his intention was to negate the very thing itself. His statement that God is the Creator of His own Creator was wrong, for the thing is creativity itself, and it is the very eye of existence; nothing is the negation of creativity, the nullification of existence — and if nothing is the negation of creativity, then how can it be true that He is creative, when he has denied creativity from the impossible?

### Chapter Four of Title Ten

The author of *al-Mahsul* said:

The Creator of things is not of anything; only He, with nothing alongside Him, has always been — He and nothing with Him. We have denied the thing and the nothing, and we have made them both creative. We have denied every simple and complex image of who He is, and we have made everything of what occurs to Him to say, or not to say, that He is creative and that He is finite. Nothing comes after the thing, because the occurrence of its name only happened when the thing appeared.

We say:

It may be that the words "the Creator of things" suffice for the sake of nothing, and the words "the Creator of things only — He, and nothing with Him" still involve what is impermissible to believe — namely, that "nothing" is a positive thing for that which is not, in the eyes of the wise, over what we have already mentioned. So if he says "nothing with Him," it is as if he said "He, and what is not — 217

<!-- page 220 -->
with Him, He still is" — and that is shirk. In any sentence, no human language is adequate to express God in a way befitting Him. The words and letters of the composer, simple and compound alike, are all temporally generated; and words, letters, and languages are merely instruments by which human beings convey to one another what is in themselves. Such speech is generated, and it indicates only what was present in the state of its origination. We have explained this in our epistle known as al-Rawda. He, the Almighty, is beyond minds and perceptions, behind a veil from understandings and thought — and without that veil, the paths of inquiry would be cut off. When it becomes necessary to prove His existence, after knowing that everything uttered by (1) the tongue, and everything the lips move with, is a reality belonging to what He has created and originated — then for the Almighty no word is borrowed to affirm Him except by way of proving an attribute or a multiplicity. The only exception is a word that God Himself has affirmed after exception, as in His saying: *God bears witness that there is no god but He* — He said "but He," not "but God," and likewise. In the same way, Imam al-Mu'izz (may God's peace and blessings be upon him) indicated this in his well-known book, saying that He alone is, even if the word is a borrowed one. It belongs among the intellects to be that which is positive for what lies outside it, without any attribute or multiplicity. The course of the path is established by the use of a letter not in the negation of any genus of existence — better informed in the meaning of safety than analogy, disruption, or the first — and it holds true according to the scholars and jurists of the people of religion.

### Chapter Five of Title Ten

He said:

And if we say "He, and nothing with Him," we have denied the thing and the nothing, and we have made everything creative. From where, then, is it true that there is nothing — which is said to be nothing — that falls under

(1) In copy (a) it is interrupted.
- 218

<!-- page 221 -->
creativity, and that the existence of what is said to be nothing precedes creativity, which is His? God has changed it, so that it is correct to deny it; but it is nothing other than the thing, and the thing is the thing. What we have denied is that there exists in languages anything that expresses Him, because of the greatness of His majesty and the inadequacy of the written letters generated to signify Him in a manner befitting His grandeur. The thing that is the good thing is other than God, who is the Finder — not something that has an eye and a real right to existence in all its parts. Thus, nothing is the negation of the eye of the thing which is pure creativity, as we have already stated. And if nothing is the negation of the eye of creativity — which is the very eye of existence and the truth of existence — then how can it become creative in existence? These statements are not true.

### Chapter Six of Title Ten

He said:

We have denied every simple and complex image of His identity, and we have made everything of what comes to Him to say, or not to say, that He is creative — and is influenced by the truth.

We say:

The saying about which the findings were found — which does not fall upon Him — Yasir Bag: all of them are creative; that which is caused is wrong?

### Chapter Seven of Title Ten

He said:

Nothing comes after the thing, because the occurrence of its name is the appearance of something. Nothing is not a name that falls upon an eye that has any share in existence; the thing is

a name, and the one who is the pure letter has not been denied — and the negation of the name does not become a noun, except for that which the name has as a name. Nothing is told about Him except what the wise have mentioned, namely that
- 219

<!-- page 222 -->
this involves the affirmation of something that has no name. And it has already been preceded by words that include the answer to it, and that is sufficient.

### Chapter Eight of Title Ten

The author of *al-Mahsul* said:

In the first chapter on the Creator, the Almighty said: He created the world all at once. This is because He created the intellect in a single sentence, and within it the images of the two worlds emerged, along with what is in them — without those images being indicated by their identities at the level of intellect. Rather, the science of intellect is grounded upon it, and it has information, except that the intellect has already advanced upon it — either by actuality, or by potency — and the images are all together.

We say:

This science, the intellect that is said to have been created and within which the images of the worlds emerged along with what is in them — it is not permissible for this to be the First Intellect that exists from God, may He be exalted, in the first place. For what is advanced in existence is dispensed from the perception of what exists within it; its existence is related to its Creator who created it, and it does not need to know more than its knowledge of itself — namely, that it is creative, and that its existence is not by itself but by another, who is its Creator. It is the cause of the existence of what is below it, like the Imam who does not need to know others below him: he knows nothing more than that he is the cause of salvation belonging to those below him, and the mediator between those above him and those below him. Rather, his function is to know from above him, and to make known to others advanced below him; for him to be present and his rank to be discharged of this duty, it is therefore necessary to delay — and existence is to imagine all that he offers in existence, from the limits, in order to attain that survival and complete existence. And if so, then what he mentioned is
- 220

<!-- page 223 -->
the intellect that emanates from the natural world — not the First Intellect — supporting what we have said: that it is the most prominent of the images of the worlds, without (1) those images being indicated by their identities in the eyes of intellect; rather, the world of intellect is grounded upon it, as he said. In potency, it and all the images are together. So saying this is a positive claim about what exists by potency — and reporting on what exists by potency is not actually existence, nor is the First Intellect existence. Indeed, it is not permissible to say that there is something within it that exists by potency, because if there were, then this would have to be preceded by the kind of existence that already exists, so as to bring it out to action; and that is invalidated for existence with the First Intellect. Or else something would have to precede Him — and since that is invalid, the claim of the author of *al-Mahsul* is not correct, except in the case of late existence behind which there is no other existence, whose essence carries the knowledge of that which precedes it in existence from the two worlds. This is the second emanation from the world of nature, like the speaker who has the images of the worlds, and the harvest of these images has already come to exist, and is already emitted.

### Chapter Nine of Title Ten

The author of *al-Mahsul* said:

When the intellect's illness was the unity of the Almighty and the unity of eternity, the intellect became eternal.

Based on that, we say:

He has made it obligatory by saying that something precedes the First Intellect in its existence — namely, the disease which he called unity. But we have already said that the intellect is not preceded in existence, and from God by anything in the first place; so it is the first of the eternal because of the impossibility of something existing before it. Solitude is not a cause of intellect, and is not other than it so as to advance upon intellect; rather, it is intellect itself, and the intellect is the unity, which is the one.

(1) In copy (a) it is said.
- 221

<!-- page 224 -->
It is the cause, it is the effect, it is the creativity, it is the creator, and it is the Perfect — the Perfect, which is the Eternal, the Eternal, the Existence, and the Essence. One. The meanings these names require are worth adding to what is said about Him concerning His existence, which is God Almighty, or by adding Him to what is in Him, what is below Him, or by adding Him to Himself, because the self in its existence is sufficient to indicate it; it is said in this chapter that creativity is concerned with creation. The Creator is the very Creator, and His image is the image of the rational — if that is the case. Also, when unity was the same as intellect, he said that the intellect became eternal — there is no eternity of its cause, for the intellect is nothing but the cause; so the cause becomes Eternal. Rather, it is He, and He is it. Such an attribute, that I have made him eternal — there is no eternity of his illness — is only true in the intellects that emerge from the natural world, such as the pronouncement and the foundations and their followers, peace be upon them, whose intellects are something, and through which they are humbled by something else. For the reason that they are holy and eternal is the eternal paradox: the intellects called the Close Angels, and the extension of their lights to them, and their union with it. The words of the author of *al-Mahsul*, if they apply in the world of nature to the intellects, do not apply at all in the world of creativity. And if his words are directed toward the intellects in the abode of nature, then imagine that great limit, and the rank in this capacity is impossible.

### Chapter Ten of Title Ten

Then we say:

It is not without significance that what he said is that it is the cause of intellect itself — but rather it is derived from it, from the stream of the Creator the Almighty. He called it unity, or it is the one from which the stream of the monster flows.

- 222
۔
۔

<!-- page 225 -->
Or it runs through the course of the image from the body-house, or it is that from which runs the course of the purpose for which it was created — which is its eye and its self — and the hero of being the one who runs the course of his creator. For unity, if it is a bug — the intellect has a thing, and the cause is a thing — and if the thing is not its existence by itself, then it is as if it were obligatory upon God Almighty, whom he called the Unitarian, to be (modernized) (1) after He was Creative — by being based on others, in the sense in which he said it; and that is impossible. Then, because unity is other than God, and that which is other than God was creative and modern, it has been made obligatory upon God whom he called unity to be creative and modern — and this is impossible. It is invalid that it is the one from which the stream of the cytoplasm flows from existence, because it is impossible that a thing should exist; so it exists in existence with God Almighty; therefore He has something else with Him on top of what he said. And it ceases to be the one from which the flow of the image from the assets in which it is found, because it is impossible that the image should exist except with that to which the course of matter pertains — and the existence of matter with God Almighty has been nullified, the presence of the image with Him. To the glory of God Almighty above the multiplication of images, which is the verse of the event — so that He becomes creative after He was creative — God Almighty is great above that. It is invalid that He be the one from whom a stream of something is flowing for which it was created, because the intellect was created not for the sake of unity; for if unity had something for which the intellect were created, it would not have created a multitude, and yet it has found a multitude — so there is no unity if it exists. And when these aspects are invalidated among the meanings of saying that the cause of the unity of the intellect is refrained from being established, then it has been proven that the eye of unity and the cause are one and the same. And his right: if the intellect is the eye of unity, then the reason for his saying that the intellect became eternal for the eternity of its cause — even though what he meant by it was that First which is the First Being — is not true.

<!-- page 226 -->
Chapter Eleven of Section Ten

The author of *al-Mahsul* said:

Since the image was made manifest in the intellect from the One, the image too is eternal in the intellect, because the intellect has become eternal in its entirety, not in part only.

We say:

This is a description of something whose power gives rise to the image projected within it, and it cannot rightly be said that this comes about in accordance with the great limit which already exists — rather, only by the power within it. And if that is so, then his statement is correct only of the intellects that emerge from other intellects, such as the intellects of the speaker-prophets, of the foundations, of the Imams, and of their followers, peace be upon them, who came forth into intellect through their own words. This is the responding intellect, called the proximate sovereign and nicknamed "the Pen," in its entirety and in its timeless duration. Their intellects, and the intellects of those who follow them upon the truth, attain this — and one praises it by saying: the author of *al-Mahsul* would not have said what he said. For if the images were not creatively brought forth in eternity, they would have no permanence, and there would be no return to the source from which they emerged bearing the image. They would have perished and faded, and there would be no hope, no fear, no reward, no recompense, and no punishment — for whatever has its cause eternal in it becomes eternal in it. If he claims this, it follows that the images he mentions have no permanence, and no connection to their source, except through the eternal power — namely, the One who is in the natural world acting through the souls that do not work by their own action, and who gain permanence and continuance only for those who have attained eternity and perfection. Without them, reward and punishment would be invalidated by what he says, since the speaker-prophets and the trustees hold their position rightly because it is so. Therefore, the meaning of what he said is valid only of the intellects that emerge from the world of nature into the world of creative origination.

Chapter Twelve of Section Ten

The author of *al-Mahsul* said:

And for us, the intellect was affected by the eternity which we have explained in the compound saying

<!-- page 227 -->
the Word of Allah, with no third intermediary between them, and so the intellect became like it.

We say:

His statement leads to meanings such as those mentioned above, and he is not entitled to it. It is not fitting to say this of the First Intellect, because it is an essence which is itself said to be the Word — not something other than the Word, nor something that was and then became like it. All that follows the intellects which pass from potentiality into actuality in the realm of nature — accepting the effects of the Word, which is the First Intellect, and becoming united with it as he himself affirms when he says: "For us, the form of the act has become the image of the intellect" — the intellect is thus a cause of what is below it, and has come to be encompassed by the Word with what is in the Creator's vision of it. For the one whose image has become an image of the Word, and who has become a cause of what is below it, requires that he was not previously a cause and did not possess an image. The Word, by contrast, is like this: it is the source of the intellects of the speaker-prophets, not the creative intellect. There is no intermediary; there has never been anything other than the Word, nor is it the cause of being. Its existence is first and foremost, beyond which descriptions break off — it grows and is exalted. We have already spoken of this above on the basis of this great limit, enough to spare any further extension. And if it were so, then the procedure of that great limit would be the course of these lower limits — which is impossible.

Chapter Thirteen of Section Ten

The author of *al-Mahsul* said:

Since this is so, it is called creativity, because creativity comes from the Creator. With complete creativity, it is only complete.

And we say:

The Book of *al-Riyad* [15]

<!-- page 228 -->
We have already addressed this, and his saying is mistaken on two counts. The first is that he names the intellect Creator and makes it like its Creator, of whom he said it is perfect — this is shirk, and it is not permissible to say, because Allah is free in His essence from perfection and from completion bound up with His creative act. Nor is it correct to say that the Creator, in the completeness of creativity, is anything but the Complete in order that creativity be complete. The second is that he has set the name of Creator upon what is of the natural world, which is impossible, because the one who truly merits the name Creator is the Being prior to all things, not anything which is itself among things. Since this is so, it is not permissible to believe what he said except in the way which is correct.

Chapter Fourteen of Section Ten

The author of *al-Mahsul* said:

The intellect only derives images from its cause, which is the Word, just as the two plugs derive benefit from the sun's light, not from its substance. If it is to bring forth, this is by virtue of what is within it from the power of its cause, which is the Word — and the Word also benefits from it. It is thus clear that the Word is what has become the cause of the things issuing from the intellect, to the very identity of the intellect. The intellect, however, has become an intermediary between the Word and what is below it.

We say:

The intellect he mentions is the intellects of the speaker-prophets and their followers, deriving from their cause — which are others they cite, not the First Intellect, which is the First Creator. For the First Intellect needs nothing else for the existence of whatever exists through it, because it is itself the cause. The particular Word, concerning which it is and from which it derives, is itself the existence of what exists. Therefore we said that the Perfect with regard to Him finds the perfect and needs nothing outside

<!-- page 229 -->
itself, because it is the cause of the existence of things. If it were preceded by something else, that antecedent would have to be the First Creator, not it — and this would lead to a misalignment of the ranks of the principles, which is impossible. As for his saying that "the intellect has become an intermediary between the Word and what is below it," we have already shown that this is impossible in what was mentioned at the beginning of our book.

It would be fitting to add and say:

How is it permissible for anything to precede the First Intellect, so as to be a means of action between it and what is below? The intellect is itself the Word, which is the cause that exists from Allah the Almighty. If it were permissible for something to precede it, then that antecedent could only be either of the same kind as its existence — in which case its precedence is invalidated, since their ranks and limits would be equal as being of the same kind, even though not identical in existence — or it would be either something or nothing. If it is something, it is an object for the perception of the intellects; and what the intellect perceives, the intellect is prior to. Therefore, it is not. And if it is nothing, then it is not the Word, but rather That which transcends the qualities a thing merits in its parts — the Almighty, exalted above all that. In what manner, then, can such precedence be? And given the impossibility of anything being prior to the First Intellect, there is nothing above the thing except the Thing of the Almighty. And if that is also the Word, it is the Cause, it is Oneness, and it is the Intellect, in accordance with what we have explained above. Allah suffices us, and He is the best Trustee.

Chapter Fifteen of Section Ten

(1) Dropped in copy (A).

The author of *al-Mahsul* said:

<!-- page 230 -->
Creativity is an intermediary between Creator and created, an effect of the influencer upon the influenced. He said: The image of creativity is intermediate between the doer and the object, and its existence is on the side of the creative agent, while the effect of the image is found on the side of the created in the thing affected. This image — I mean creativity — has thus come to be in the Creator, the Almighty.

We say:

This statement contains the positing of something that Allah has not created, that He has not invented, nor brought into being. This is contrary to what the monotheists are bound to hold, because by saying that he has made creativity uncreated, he has made it obligatory that creativity be an image within the Creator. It then follows from his saying that the image he calls creation must itself have an image, which he calls the influencer within it — fixed, not invented, not produced. Nor is it permissible to suppose that creativity is something not creative; for were what he imagines as creative anything other than creativity, it would have to be either with Allah the Almighty, or existent through itself. Its existence through itself is invalidated, since it would then be fragmented within Him while remaining alongside Allah the Almighty — because its presence with Allah is not without it being either similar to Allah, may He be glorified and exalted, and thus an example of Him, so that they would be two examples positing competence in each in the manner of duality, and competence in two things, which arises only from a positive cause because they would each be on that same footing prior to themselves; or their existence would not be alike, and there is no likeness; and the existence of two opposites occurs only in the presence of another that preserves their existence — for in the presence of one of the opposites, the existence of the other is invalidated, because its existence is necessitated. This requires that the Creator be the Almighty Creator, and that He precede what is primary, by the Almighty — and that is

<!-- page 231 -->
even more impossible, even more excluded. And if there is anything that necessitates its existence, it must be one of two faces: if it is the hero of all faces, that is, the principle of its existence in itself; and if its existence in itself is invalidated, then it is proven that its existence is from Allah Almighty, and creativity has its existence from Allah Almighty. So the one is not one through creativity, and to call him a creative or primary creator distinct from the other — if that were so likewise — would invalidate the existence of what the existent produces, except by the One. It is proven that creativity is the Creator; and if creativity is the Creator, then what the author of *al-Mahsul* said is in error, and let him avoid it, if Allah wills. We have thus arrived at what we promised at the beginning of the book.

We say:

Tawhid and knowledge of the limits is difficult, involving the life and survival of souls and their salvation from the world of nature and its impossibilities. Since it is difficult, and since Allah Almighty knows that all His servants do not attain the degree by which they reach perfection in knowledge of the limits of Allah and in His tawhid from Allah — among them is in every age one man, perfect and chosen, like the Imam, may Allah's prayers be upon him — by "perfection" I mean to be complete in accepting the overflow taking place in the natural world from the side of the angels who are close to him. Thus he is sovereign over them, raised to His wholeness, and Allah makes obligatory upon them obedience to him, and His love so that they may become part of him, and His compassion for them — and there is no way except by adopting the two acts of worship and fulfilling the truth from them in knowledge and action, both outward and inward, and being keen to preserve them. This is only by remembering death and what follows from it — the paradox of parents, children, relatives, and homeland — and the certainty that no one reaches the promised time except by surplus, and that there is no surplus to be stored except by obedience. Its opportunities are an obligation: fulfilling its right, doing good deeds, and knowledge of the realities of things. May Allah place us and the body of believers in the east of the earth and its west — those who conclude their lives with goodness — and may our sustenance and their intercession be the intercession of Muhammad, Ali, and the

(1) Dropped in copy (A).

<!-- page 232 -->
pure nation, and our Imam [the Ruler by the Command of Allah] and his fathers, peace be upon them, in the land of Jerusalem with His mercy and compassion. Then we conclude the book with praise to Allah, Lord of the Worlds, and prayers upon the best of the first and the last, Muhammad, the family of the Imams, and the guiding Imams. And we say: there is no power but from Allah the Almighty, what Allah wills; I ask Allah for forgiveness, and I delegate my affair to Allah, sufficient is Allah, yes the Trustee, yes Allah, yes the Supporter. Praise be to Allah unto the praise of Him, and prayers upon Muhammad and his good, pure family, all of them.

Subject Index

(Introduction to Evolution)
From page 5 to page 39

A brief presentation of the cultural mindset that dominated Islamic society in the late third century of the Hijra, of the doctrinal disputes and their causes, and of the influences that dominated writers and scholars in that early era. To what extent were the discussions and lectures taking their course? Among the things worthy of note is that the Ismailis are considered one of the sects that contributed a large amount to these discussions, but on a larger scale and in a new and innovative field. The researcher acknowledges that intellectual liberation has a great effect on their beliefs, given that the debate was not limited to other ideologically hostile sects, but extended beyond them to the Ismaili preachers themselves.

The first scientific discussion that took place in that period was the one in which each of the Ismaili preachers participated:

<!-- page 236 -->
Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Nasafi, the author of the book (*al-Mahsul*); Abu Hatim al-Razi, the author of the book (*al-Islah*); Abu Ya'qub al-Sijistani, the author of the book (*al-Nusra*); and Hamid al-Din al-Kirmani, the author of the book (*al-Riyad*). These figures dedicated the aforementioned books to declaring their views on theology.

The importance of the book (*al-Riyad*) emerged in the scientific community when the traces of al-Kirmani's work came to be known, so it is considered one of the rare books that represent the prevailing philosophical opinions in the third century AH and afterwards.

The introduction also contains the biography of al-Razi, his activity, his writings, and his opinions; then the biography of al-Sijistani and his opinions; then the biography of Hamid al-Din al-Kirmani; and then the contents of the book (*al-Riyad*), its sections, and a quick, brief glimpse of the book, its chapters and divisions.

(*Author's Introduction*)
From page 47 to page 52

A brief account of the reasons that led him to write the book (*al-Riyad*), followed by a review of the views expressed in the three books — (*al-Mahsul*), (*al-Islah*), and (*al-Nusra*) — in summary.

(*Chapter One*) from page 53 to page 100

Divided into thirty-eight sections that examine the essence of the soul, which is the first issue.

(*Chapter Two*) from page 101 to page 110

Divided into nine sections and examining the first chapter of the intellect, which is the First Creator.


<!-- page 237 -->

((Chapter Three)) from page 111 to page 118
Divided into six chapters, examining the soul and the body and whether or not they are similar to the First.

((Chapter Four)) from page 119 to page 126
Divided into eight chapters, examining the proposition that souls are parts and not traces.

((Chapter Five)) from page 127 to page 134
Divided into seven chapters, arguing that the human being is the fruit of the world.

((Chapter Six)) from page 135 to page 142
Divided into nine chapters, examining motion, rest, transformation, and form.

((Chapter Seven)) from page 143 to page 152
Divided into seven chapters, treating the divisions of the world.

((Chapter Eight)) from page 153 to page 175
Divided into twenty-four chapters on fate and destiny.

((Chapter Nine)) from page 176 to page 212
Divided into thirty-three chapters, on the chapter of the law of Adam and the keeper of the tablet.

((Chapter Ten)) from page 213 to page 230
Divided into sixteen chapters, on the chapter of Tawhid and the First Creator.

((French Introduction)) — includes a definition of the book of al-Riyad and the reason for its placement.

((French Introduction))

...une étude comparée entre celle-ci et les théories philosophiques officielles des Ismaïlites.

Si la série d'ouvrages historiques et philosophiques déjà établis et publiés par nos soins s'enrichit aujourd'hui d'un volume nouveau, «ar-Riad», cela ne saurait se faire sans un mot de reconnaissant hommage à l'éminent orientaliste qu'est le Professeur W. Ivanow. Car c'est grâce à son infatigable initiative au service de la science que la plupart des manuscrits ismaéliens ont pu être mis à jour. Travail énorme et désintéressé qu'inspire un seul souci: celui d'informer le monde islamique sur cette philosophie universelle qui fit bouillonner les esprits pour un temps, fascina le regard des savants et fut, en Orient comme en Occident, à la fine pointe de leurs approximations.

Beyrouth — Liban
1960
Aref Tamer

— 251

<!-- page 254 -->

...le profit pour qui recherche l'origine de ces controverses, lorsqu'il s'applique à creuser les diverses opinions. Il découvre que l'œuvre des auteurs de cette époque lointaine constitue un monument de science nouvelle par rapport à leur siècle, tant pour la pénétration des problèmes philosophiques que pour l'ampleur de la recherche dans le domaine des commentaires de textes et de l'exégèse des versets coraniques. Cet effort leur a valu d'approcher de la réalité et de s'en faire une conception raisonnée.

La plupart du temps les controverses en question avaient pour origine des discussions orales que se livraient les cercles et les centres d'études scientifiques. Elles prenaient la forme de longues conférences qui s'étalent dans les livres à longueur de pages. Parmi les groupements qui contribuaient abondamment à ces débats, nous trouvons celui des Ismaéliens. Mais ce qui est assez surprenant, c'est que, ces débats de théologie à peine terminés entre différentes sectes, nous voyons les Ismaéliens s'engager dans un autre genre de controverse, un genre tout nouveau... C'est la controverse entre leurs Da'is eux-mêmes. Ceci prouve clairement que la liberté de pensée était chez eux une loi souveraine. Le dynamisme de leur réflexion intellectuelle, affranchi des chaînes de la tradition, s'imposait ainsi aux milieux conservateurs et les forçait à l'abandon des positions surannées.

L'ouvrage intitulé «al-Mahsul» parut dans les milieux ismaéliens au début du 4ème siècle de l'Hégire (10ème siècle après Jésus-Christ). Il est attribué au Da'i an-Nasafi; il est curieux qu'al-Kirmani, en parlant de ce texte, omette de mentionner son auteur. Peut-être cette omission est-elle un indice que le livre a été attribué par erreur à an-Nasafi. La faveur obtenue par «al-Mahsul» dans les milieux ismaéliens amena le Da'i Abu Hatim ar-Razi à rédiger de son côté un écrit intitulé «al-Islah». Ce livre renferme une critique de certaines opinions rapportées par «al-Mahsul». Cette discussion poussa un autre grand Da'i, Abu Ya'qub as-Sijistani, à intervenir à son tour contre ce qu'il estimait contraire à la vérité et aux principes philosophiques. Dans son livre «an-Nasra», il prit le parti de l'auteur d'«al-Mahsul» et critiqua ar-Razi sans toutefois chercher à ruiner la position de celui-ci.

Peu de temps après lui, al-Kirmani aborda le même sujet de façon magistrale. Ce fut dans ce précieux traité «ar-Riad»: il se donna le rôle d'arbitre entre les auteurs d'«al-Islah» et d'«an-Nasra» et sut, avec une habileté consommée, à la fois combler d'éloges et critiquer avec rigueur scientifique les différentes positions des trois livres, en les confrontant avec la doctrine fondamentale de l'Ismaélisme. Autrement dit, son ouvrage constitue une étude

— 252


The book we present today to specialists in the philosophical sciences is called *al-Riyad*. Its author is Sayyidna Hamid ad-Din Ahmad b. Abdallah al-Kirmani, who served as Da'i of the Fatimid Imam, the Caliph al-Hakim bi-Amrillah, in Iraq and Persia. He bore the title *Hujjat al-Iraqayn* — the one charged with the Ismaili mission for these two regions.

The religious leaders of the Ismailis count *al-Riyad* among the important works addressed to the most learned of those who occupy the highest ranks of the community. It is therefore distinguished by the elevated level and the rigor of its doctrine. This explains why beginners in philosophical inquiry have found it difficult to grasp the implications of the text and to penetrate the nuances of its technical vocabulary.

*Al-Riyad* marks a stage in the evolution of Islamic thought and offers a clear picture of the growth of the Ismaili doctrinal edifice. When we consider that the book sets out, in condensed form, the dominant teaching of this period of Ismailism, we understand the influence it must have exerted. There are good reasons to regard this text as a general philosophical conference elaborated by four of the great Ismaili Da'is of the early period, who entered into it their philosophical reflections together with their esoteric exegesis. These four are: the Da'i Muhammad b. Ahmad al-Nasafi, Abu Hatim al-Razi, Abu Ya'qub al-Sijistani, and Hamid ad-Din al-Kirmani. In this common work of critique, the four thinkers devoted themselves passionately to the defense of their speculation and condensed their doctrine within it.

It is now accepted that toward the end of the 3rd century of the Hijra, the Islamic world displayed the features of an advanced state of culture — scientific, philosophical, and literary — yet one worked through by new messages and varied doctrinal controversies. The theologians of the time engaged in endless debates, free of any fixity in expression. How great, then,

— 253 —
